Demanding Correction of the Cause in Qiyas
Author defines and explains the first type of objection to the effective cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the objection of 'demanding correction of the cause' and explain its implicit acknowledgments.

Core Idea
Demanding correction of the cause acknowledges the attribute and the original ruling, only challenging causality.

In dialectical reasoning within usul al-fiqh, an opponent may object to the effective cause used in analogical argument. One such objection is 'demanding correction of the cause', where the opponent asks for evidence that the chosen attribute is truly the cause. This lesson will unpack this objection, its examples, and what it implicitly concedes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection type
Locate where the author defines the first type of objection to the effective cause. Note the Arabic term: المطالبة بتصحيح العلة.
2
Understand the examples
Examine the three examples: wine (intoxication), barley (measurement), and a woman changing religion. See how the objector asks 'why is this attribute the cause?'
These are standard usul al-fiqh examples.
3
Recognize implicit acknowledgments
The author argues that this objection implicitly acknowledges both the existence of the attribute in the original and branch, and the ruling in the original. Trace his reasoning.
If the objector denied the attribute or ruling, he would have used a different objection.
4
Understand the burden on the arguer
When this objection is raised, the arguer must provide evidence (tashih) for the causaliy. This can be via indicative expressions or explicit texts.
This shifts the burden to the proponent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثالث الاعتراضات الواردة على العلة المؤثرة ونعني بالعلة المؤثرة ؛ كون الوصف هو المؤثر الموجب للحكم في الأصل المقاس عليه . والاعتراضات التي ترد على هذه العلة على أنواع، وهي كما يلي : المطلب الأول : المطالبة بتصحيح العلة وهو أن يطلب المعترض من المستدل الدليل على أن الوصف الذي جعله جامعا بين الأصل والفرع علة، كقوله فيما إذا قال : مسكر فكان حراما كالخمر، أو مكيل فحرم فيه التفاضل كالبر : لم قلت إن الإسكار علة التحريم؟ وإن الكيل علة الربا؟ ولم قلت إن التبديل علة القتل؟ فيما إذا قال في المرأة أنها إنسان بدل دينه فقتل كالرجل . . وهذا الاعتراض يتضمن اعتراف المعترض بوجود العلة، أي الوصف في الفرع والأصل، ويتضمن تسليم الحكم. أما تضمنه تسلي الوصف في الفرع ،والأصل فلأنه يسأل عن عليته وهو كونه علة، وذلك فرع على تحقيق الوصف في نفسه في الأصل والفرع، إذ لو لم يكن ذلك لكان منعه وجود الوصف أولى به وأجدى عليه . وأما تضمنه تسليم الحكم، فكتسليم تحريم الخمر والربا ووجود القتل في الأمثلة المتقدمة، ذلك أنه إذا كانت العلة فرع الحكم في الأصل لاستنباطها منه، والحكم أصل لها فمنازعة المعترض في الفرع الذي هو العلة يشغر بتسلي الأصل الذي هو الحكم، إذ لو لم يكن تسليمًا له لكان منعه أولى وأجدى على المعترض. وإذا وجد هذا الاعتراض تطلب من المستدل أن يستدل على صحة علته، وهذا التصحيح والاستدلال إما أن يكون بسوق الألفاظ الدالة أو الناطقة من .
The third section: Objections directed at the effective cause. By effective cause we mean the attribute being the effective necessitating factor of the ruling in the original case analogized upon. The objections directed at this cause are of types, and they are as follows: The first requirement: Demanding correction of the cause. It is that the objector requests from the arguer evidence that the attribute which he made a common factor between the original and the branch is a cause, such as his statement when he says: 'It is intoxicating, so it is forbidden like wine,' or 'It is measured, so disparity is prohibited in it like barley': 'Why did you say that intoxication is the cause of prohibition? And that measurement is the cause of riba? And why did you say that changing religion is the cause of killing?' in the case where he says about a woman that she is a person who changed her religion and was killed like a man. This objection involves the objector's acknowledgment of the existence of the cause, i.e., the attribute in the branch and the original, and it involves acceptance of the ruling. As for its inclusion of acknowledgment of the attribute in the branch and the original, it is because he asks about its causality, i.e., its being a cause, and that is subsidiary to verifying the attribute itself in the original and branch, for if that were not so, then denying the existence of the attribute would be more appropriate and beneficial for him. As for its inclusion of acceptance of the ruling, it is like accepting the prohibition of wine and riba and the existence of killing in the preceding examples. That is because if the cause is subsidiary to the ruling in the original, being derived from it, and the ruling is the root for it, then the objector's disputing the subsidiary which is the cause implies acceptance of the root which is the ruling, for if it were not acceptance, then denying it would be more appropriate and beneficial for the objector. When this objection occurs, it requires the arguer to provide evidence for the validity of his cause. This correction and argumentation is either by adducing indicative or explicit expressions…
Source pages p. 432

Commentary
This passage defines the first type of objection to the effective cause ('illa mu'aththira) in the context of qiyas (analogical reasoning). The author begins by defining the effective cause as the attribute that necessitates the ruling in the original case. Then he enumerates types of objections, focusing on 'demanding correction of the cause' (matalabat tasheeh al-illa). This objection requests proof that the attribute identified is indeed the cause. The author explains that this objection implicitly acknowledges both the existence of the attribute in the original and branch and the ruling in the original. He illustrates with examples: wine being forbidden due to intoxication, barley being subject to riba due to being measured, and a woman changing her religion being killed like a man. The objector's demand for proof of causality implies that he accepts the attribute exists and the original ruling, otherwise he would have directly denied them. When such an objection is raised, the arguer must justify his cause, either by using indicative expressions (alfaz dalla) or explicit texts.

Key Terms
العلة المؤثرة
المطالبة بتصحيح العلة
الوصف
الأصل
الفرع
الحكم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the objection 'demanding correction of the cause' implicitly acknowledge?
A) The attribute does not exist in the branch.
B) The original ruling is invalid.
C) The existence of the attribute in the original and branch and acceptance of the original ruling.
D) The cause is invalid.

Key Takeaway
The objection of demanding correction of the cause challenges the causality of an attribute while conceding its existence and the original ruling.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a scenario where an objector demands correction of the cause in a qiyas about the permissibility of a new financial transaction. Identify the attribute and the original and branch cases.
?
Given the objection 'Why is intoxication the cause?', explain what the objector implicitly acknowledges according to this passage.

Using Amr to Derive 'Illa: The Divorce Example
First example: Maliki's argument from Qur'an on lodging divorced women

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze how the Maliki uses the command 'lodge them' to infer an 'illa and extend the ruling from revocable to irrevocable divorce.

Core Idea
A command implies obligation and can be extended to similar cases via 'illa.

In jadal debates among usuliyyin, a command from revelation is a key piece of evidence. The Maliki here quotes the Qur'anic command to lodge divorced women. He argues that the command indicates obligation, then considers whether it applies to the irrevocably divorced (ba'in) or the revocably divorced (raj'iyya). By identifying a shared 'illa, he extends the ruling from one to the other. This lesson walks through that reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the command and its default meaning
The Maliki cites 'Lodge them from where you lodge.' He states that a command (amr) apparently indicates obligation (wujub). This is the default assumption in usul.
In later usul, there is debate, but here it is taken as default.
2
Consider the possible referents
The command could refer to a separated (ba'in) or revocable (raj'iyya) woman. The Maliki explicitly considers both possibilities.
This is a common dialectical move: to test the scope.
3
Apply the 'illa to extend the ruling
If the command intends the revocable case, the 'illa (cause) is invoked to include the separated case by analogy. The 'illa is the reason for the ruling, here derived from the command itself.
The command itself 'orders analogy' (wurud al-amr bi al-qiyas).
4
Confirm the conclusion
Thus, both ba'in and raj'iyya are covered by the obligation due to the shared cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيقول المالكي : الدليل على ذلك قوله تعالى : ﴿أَسْكُنُوهُنَّ مِنْ حَيْثُ سَكَنتُم مِّن وجدِكُمْ﴾، والأمر ظاهره الوجوب، ولا يخلو أن يريد به البائن أو الرجعية، فإن أراد به البائن لم تحل مخالفته، وإن أراد الرجعية حملنا عليها البائن بالعلة التي ذكرناها لورود الأمر بالقياس ومثاله من السنة ; قول الشافعي في بيع البر : إنه مطعوم جنس، والدليل عليه أن النبي ﷺ نهى عن بيع الطعام بالطعام إلا مثلا ، وذكر الصفة في الحكم تعليل، فدل على أن الطعم علة.
The Maliki says: The evidence for that is His saying: 'Lodge them where you lodge from your means.' The command apparently indicates obligation. It is not free from intending either the separated (ba'in) or the revocable (raj'iyya) woman. If He intended the separated, then violating it is not permissible. If He intended the revocable, then we apply the separated to it by the 'illa we mentioned, due to the command for analogy. An example from the Sunnah: Al-Shafi'i's statement regarding the sale of wheat: that it is a food category. The evidence for that is that the Prophet forbade selling food for food except equal, and mentioning the attribute in the ruling is a cause, so it indicates that food is the 'illa.
Source pages p. 434

Commentary
This passage presents two examples of using a textual command (amr) to establish an 'illa and then apply analogy (qiyas). The first example is from the Qur'an: the Maliki argues that the command to lodge women necessarily indicates obligation. He then considers two possibilities: the command could refer to a divorced woman who is irrevocably separated (ba'in) or one who is revocably divorced (raj'iyya). If it refers to the ba'in, then the command is direct and must be followed. If it refers to the raj'iyya, then by analogy the ba'in is included because they share the same 'illa. The second example is from the Sunnah: Al-Shafi'i argues that wheat is a food category, and since the Prophet forbade selling food for food except in equal amounts, the attribute of being a food is the 'illa for the ruling. Both examples illustrate how commands in revelation are used in jadal to identify the cause and extend the ruling by analogy.

Key Terms
الأمر
العلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Maliki's argument, if the command 'lodge them' is interpreted to refer to the revocable (raj'iyya) divorce, what is the basis for including the irrevocable (ba'in) case?
A) The general wording of the command
B) The cause ('illa) shared between them
C) Consensus of the companions
D) The apparent obligation of the command

Key Takeaway
A command's scope can be extended to non-explicit cases by identifying a shared cause ('illa), a key tool in jadal and qiyas.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct an analogical argument using a command from the Qur'an (e.g., the command to honor parents) and identify a possible 'illa to extend it to a new case (e.g., grandparents).
?
In the divorce example, the 'illa is not explicitly stated; what could be a plausible 'illa for the lodging obligation? Derive it from the text.

Using Amr to Derive 'Illa: The Food Sale Example
Second example: Al-Shafi'i's use of hadith on food sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how Al-Shafi'i extracts the 'illa from the prophetic prohibition on unequal food sales and applies it to all food categories.

Core Idea
Mentioning an attribute in a ruling indicates that attribute is the cause ('illa).

In the second example, Al-Shafi'i uses a hadith from the Prophet forbidding selling food for food except in equal amounts. He notes that the attribute 'food' is mentioned in the ruling, which signifies that being a food is the effective cause ('illa) for the prohibition. This allows him to apply the ruling to all food items, not just the specific ones mentioned. This lesson breaks down that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the hadith and its ruling
The Prophet forbade selling food for food except equal. This is a prohibition (nahy), which also indicates obligation to avoid the forbidden act.
2
Spot the attribute mentioned in the ruling
The attribute 'food' (ta'am) is explicitly mentioned. Al-Shafi'i argues that this shows the ruling is tied to the attribute.
In usul, mentioning an attribute in the ruling implies it is the 'illa.
3
Conclude that the attribute is the 'illa
Because the ruling mentions 'food' as the subject of the prohibition, being a food (ta'amiyya) is the 'illa for the prohibition of inequality.
This is a standard way to extract 'illa from nass.
4
Apply the 'illa to all food items
Thus, the prohibition on unequal sale applies to all food items, not just those originally mentioned, showing how qiyas works from a single command.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيقول المالكي : الدليل على ذلك قوله تعالى : ﴿أَسْكُنُوهُنَّ مِنْ حَيْثُ سَكَنتُم مِّن وجدِكُمْ﴾، والأمر ظاهره الوجوب، ولا يخلو أن يريد به البائن أو الرجعية، فإن أراد به البائن لم تحل مخالفته، وإن أراد الرجعية حملنا عليها البائن بالعلة التي ذكرناها لورود الأمر بالقياس ومثاله من السنة ; قول الشافعي في بيع البر : إنه مطعوم جنس، والدليل عليه أن النبي ﷺ نهى عن بيع الطعام بالطعام إلا مثلا ، وذكر الصفة في الحكم تعليل، فدل على أن الطعم علة.
The Maliki says: The evidence for that is His saying: 'Lodge them where you lodge from your means.' The command apparently indicates obligation. It is not free from intending either the separated (ba'in) or the revocable (raj'iyya) woman. If He intended the separated, then violating it is not permissible. If He intended the revocable, then we apply the separated to it by the 'illa we mentioned, due to the command for analogy. An example from the Sunnah: Al-Shafi'i's statement regarding the sale of wheat: that it is a food category. The evidence for that is that the Prophet forbade selling food for food except equal, and mentioning the attribute in the ruling is a cause, so it indicates that food is the 'illa.
Source pages p. 434

Commentary
This passage presents two examples of using a textual command (amr) to establish an 'illa and then apply analogy (qiyas). The first example is from the Qur'an: the Maliki argues that the command to lodge women necessarily indicates obligation. He then considers two possibilities: the command could refer to a divorced woman who is irrevocably separated (ba'in) or one who is revocably divorced (raj'iyya). If it refers to the ba'in, then the command is direct and must be followed. If it refers to the raj'iyya, then by analogy the ba'in is included because they share the same 'illa. The second example is from the Sunnah: Al-Shafi'i argues that wheat is a food category, and since the Prophet forbade selling food for food except in equal amounts, the attribute of being a food is the 'illa for the ruling. Both examples illustrate how commands in revelation are used in jadal to identify the cause and extend the ruling by analogy.

Proving the 'Illa by Ta'thir: The Nabidh Analogy
Explanation of ta'thir with a dialectical exchange example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the ta'thir argument for the validity of an 'illa in a dialectical exchange and identify the co-variation evidence.

Core Idea
The validity of an 'illa is proven when the ruling follows its presence and absence.

In Islamic legal dialectics (jadal), proving that a proposed cause ('illa) is effective requires demonstrating that the ruling co-varies with it. This passage from a usul al-fiqh text introduces the method of ta'thir (co-extensiveness) and illustrates it with the classic debate over nabidh. The Maliki/Shafi'i argues that nabidh is haram because it shares the intoxicating effect with khamr; the Hanafi challenges the evidence. The response provides the co-variation proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dialectical parties
Find the claimant (Maliki/Shafi'i) who asserts an 'illa and the objector (Hanafi) who demands evidence. Notice that objector asks: 'What is the evidence for validity of this 'illa?'
2
Extract the proposed 'illa
The 'illa is 'the intoxicating effect' (shidda mutriba). The claimant uses it as the basis for the analogy between nabidh and khamr.
3
Analyze the ta'thir evidence
The responder points to co-variation: grape juice is halal before becoming intoxicating, haram when intoxicating, halal again when the effect disappears, and haram if it returns. This shows the ruling depends on the presence/absence of the 'illa.
This method is called 'aks (co-extensiveness) because the ruling 'reverses' with the 'illa.
4
Relate to dialectical rules
In jadal, the objector can demand proof of the 'illa. Presenting ta'thir (co-variation) is a valid response that shifts the burden back. The example shows how empirical observation of legal cases supports the 'illa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الدلالة على صحة العلة بالاستنباط : وذلك من طريقين : التأثير، وشهادة الأصول. الدلالة بالتأثير والتأثير ; أن يوجد الحكم بوجود معنى ويعدم لعدمه، فيدل ذلك على أن الحكم متعلق به وتابع له، وهذا ما يسمى بالعكس وهو على وجهين : الأول : السلب والوجود ومثاله; أن يستدل المالكي أو الشافعي في النبيذ أنه حرام، لأنه شراب فيه شدة مطربة، فكان حرامًا كالخمر . فيقول الحنفي : ما الدليل على صحة هذه العلة؟ ويجيب المستدل قائلا : الدليل عليه وجود الحكم بوجودها وزواله بزوالها، وذلك أن عصير العنب قبل حدوث الشدة حلال، فإذا حدثت الشدة المطربة حكم بتحريمه، فإذا زالت الشدة المطربة حكم بإباحته ، فلو عادت الشدة عاد التحريم كما كان، فدل على أن ذلك هو العلة.
Evidence for the validity of the 'illa by extraction (istinbat) is via two paths: al-ta'thir (effect) and shahadat al-usul (testimony of principles). Evidence by al-ta'thir: it is that the ruling exists with the existence of a meaning and ceases with its cessation, so that indicates that the ruling is attached to it and dependent on it. This is called al-'aks (co-extensiveness), and it has two types: The first: negation and existence. Its example: that the Maliki or Shafi'i argues regarding nabidh that it is forbidden because it is a drink containing an intoxicating effect, so it is forbidden like khamr. The Hanafi says: What is the evidence for the validity of this 'illa? The arguer answers: The evidence for it is the existence of the ruling with its existence and its removal with its removal. That is, grape juice before the occurrence of intoxication is permissible; when the intoxicating effect occurs, it is judged forbidden; when the intoxicating effect disappears, it is judged permissible; if the intoxication returns, the prohibition returns as before. So it indicates that that is the 'illa.
Source pages p. 435

Commentary
This passage introduces the method of extracting the validity of an 'illa through istinbat (extraction). Two methods are listed: al-ta'thir and shahadat al-usul. The focus here is on al-ta'thir, which means that the ruling exists when the proposed cause exists and disappears when it disappears — this co-variation proves the causal link. This method is called al-'aks (or 'aks). It has two subtypes: the first is 'negation and existence' (salb wa wujud). An example is given: the Maliki or Shafi'i argues that nabidh (a date drink) is forbidden because it contains an intoxicating effect, like khamr. The Hanafi challenges: what is the evidence for this 'illa? The responder provides the evidence by pointing to the co-variation: grape juice before becoming intoxicating is halal; once it becomes intoxicating, it becomes haram; if the intoxication disappears (e.g., by turning to vinegar), it becomes halal again; if it becomes intoxicating again, it returns to haram. This shows that the 'illa is indeed the intoxicating effect. Thus the argument demonstrates that the ruling turns on the presence or absence of the proposed 'illa.

Key Terms
التأثير
العكس
العلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the nabidh example, what evidence does the arguer give to prove that the 'illa is the intoxicating effect?
A) A hadith that intoxicants are forbidden
B) The fact that grape juice is halal before becoming intoxicating
C) Consensus among scholars
D) That the Prophet drank nabidh

Key Takeaway
The ta'thir method proves an 'illa by showing that the ruling changes with the presence and absence of the proposed cause, as demonstrated in the nabidh analogy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a similar ta'thir argument for another case: Suppose one claims that the sale of a commodity before possession is prohibited because it involves risk (gharar). How would you prove that gharar is the 'illa by co-variation? (Hint: use examples where gharar is present/absent, like selling dates before harvest vs. after).
?
Identify the 'illa and the co-variation evidence in the following passage: 'Wine is forbidden because it intoxicates. Grape juice is halal before fermentation, becomes haram when it intoxicates, and returns to halal if it turns to vinegar.'

Validating an 'Illa by Division and Counter-Check
Example of using division to validate an 'illa by eliminating alternatives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the method of al-taqsim wa al-muqabala to evaluate a proposed 'illa in a legal debate.

Core Idea
Find the true 'illa by eliminating all false alternatives through exhaustive division.

In Islamic legal theory, establishing the 'illa (effective cause) for a ruling often requires rigorous dialectical reasoning. One powerful method is al-taqsim wa al-muqabala (division and counter-check). In this lesson, we examine a Maliki-Hanafi debate over riba in wheat, where the Maliki uses this method to defend his proposed 'illa. By learning this technique, you will be able to construct and critique arguments for legal causes in usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposed 'illa
The Maliki claims that the 'illa for riba in wheat is that it is a 'muqtat muddakhar' (a staple food stored for livelihood). Note this as the position to be defended.
2
Recognize the opponent's challenge
The Hanafi asks for evidence that this 'illa is valid in the original case (e.g., a large quantity of wheat). This challenges the Maliki to prove the 'illa's correctness.
In jadal, the mu'tarid (objector) demands proof of the 'illa's validity.
3
Observe the division of possible 'illas
The Maliki responds by dividing the possible 'illas: either his proposal (stapleness) or measure/weight (the Hanafi view). This is the 'division' step (taqsim).
The division must be exhaustive to be valid.
4
Eliminate alternatives
The Maliki begins to argue that measure/weight cannot be the 'illa (e.g., 'if riba were based on weight, then...'). The text cuts off, but the method requires showing why each alternative fails. This is the 'counter-check' (muqabala).
Successful elimination leaves only the proposed 'illa as valid.
5
Verify exhaustiveness of the division
Check whether the division covers all plausible 'illas. For riba in wheat, are there other candidates? If an alternative is omitted, the argument is weak.
Here, the Maliki assumes only two possibilities; a critic might add a third.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : التقسيم والمقابلة : ومثاله; أن يستدل المالكي على أن قليل الحنطة يجري فيها الربا، لأن هذا مقتات مدخر للعيش غالبا، فحرم فيه التفاضل كالكثير . فيقول الحنفي : ما الدليل على صحة العلة في الأصل؟ فيقول المالكي: لا يخلو أن تكون العلة ما ذكرناه أو الكيل أو الوزن على ما تقوله، ولا يجوز أن تكون علة الربا الكيل والوزن لأنه لو كان الربا في
The second type: division and counter-check. Its example: that the Maliki argues that a small quantity of wheat is subject to riba, because it is a staple food stored for livelihood generally, so disparity is forbidden in it as in a large quantity. The Hanafi says: What is the evidence for the validity of the 'illa in the original case? The Maliki says: It is not free from the 'illa being what we mentioned or being measure (kayl) or weight (wazn) as you claim. It is not permissible that the 'illa of riba be measure and weight because if riba were in
Source pages p. 435

Commentary
This passage presents the second method of validating an 'illa: division and counter-check (al-taqsim wa al-muqabala). The Maliki proposes that the 'illa for riba in wheat is that it is a staple food (muqtat muddakhar). The Hanafi challenges this. The Maliki responds by dividing the possible 'illas: either it is the proposed 'illa (stapleness) or it is measure (kayl) or weight (wazn) as the Hanafi school holds. He then argues that the latter cannot be the 'illa because if riba were based on measure or weight, then... (the text cuts off). The argument is incomplete, but the method involves enumerating all plausible 'illas and then disproving the alternatives to establish the one remaining as valid. This is a common jadal technique called 'division and elimination'.

Key Terms
التقسيم والمقابلة
المقتات المدخر

Key Takeaway
Al-taqsim wa al-muqabala is a dialectical method that strengthens an 'illa by showing it is the only one that survives after eliminating all alternatives.

Proving the Validity of a Cause through al-Ta'thir
Author explains al-ta'thir with two subtypes and examples from fiqh of khamr and riba.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two subtypes of al-ta'thir (salb wa wujud and taqsim wa muqabala) and apply them to prove the validity of a legal cause in a jadal exchange.

Core Idea
Al-ta'thir proves a cause by showing the ruling follows it like a shadow.

In this passage, the author explains al-ta'thir, a dialectical method to prove that a proposed legal cause ('illa) is genuinely effective. The method relies on demonstrating that the ruling co-varies with the cause. Two distinct techniques are presented: direct empirical correlation (salb wa wujud) and elimination of alternatives (taqsim wa muqabala). Understanding these techniques is essential for engaging in juristic debates where the validity of a cause is contested.

Guided Path
1
Identify the putative cause and ruling
In each example, locate the 'illa being claimed (e.g., intoxicating intensity for nabidh, staple property for wheat) and the corresponding hukm (forbidden, subject to riba).
2
Analyze the first subtype: salb wa wujud
Notice how the arguer demonstrates that the ruling exists when the cause is present and ceases when it is absent, using the temporal sequence of grape juice turning into wine and then vinegar.
This is a direct empirical proof.
3
Analyze the second subtype: taqsim wa muqabala
See how the Maliki lists possible causes (staple property, weight, volume) and eliminates all except his own by showing that weight/volume lead to contradictions in accepted rulings (e.g., permissibility of exchanging gold for wheat).
This is a logical elimination process.
4
Compare the two subtypes
Recognize that salb wa wujud uses temporal correlation and requires observable change, while taqsim wa muqabala uses logical division and requires agreement on rejection of alternatives.
5
Apply to a new case
Practice using either method on a different fiqh issue, such as proving that the cause of the prohibition of gambling is the presence of gharar (uncertainty).
Use the method that fits the available evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ب - الدلالة على صحة العلة بالاستنباط : وذلك من طريقين : التأثير، وشهادة الأصول. 1 - الدلالة بالتأثير والتأثير ؛ أن يوجد الحكم بوجود معنى ويعدم لعدمه، فيدل ذلك على أن الحكم متعلق به وتابع له، وهذا ما يسمى بالعكس وهو على وجهين : الأول : السلب والوجود ومثاله؛ أن يستدل المالكي أو الشافعي في النبيذ أنه حرام، لأنه شراب فيه شدة مطربة، فكان حرامًا كالخمر . فيقول الحنفي : ما الدليل على صحة هذه العلة؟ ويجيب المستدل قائلا : الدليل عليه وجود الحكم بوجودها وزواله بزوالها، وذلك أن عصير العنب قبل حدوث الشدة حلال، فإذا حدثت الشدة المطربة حكم بتحريمه، فإذا زالت الشدة المطربة حكم بإباحته ، فلو عادت الشدة عاد التحريم كما كان، فدل على أن ذلك هو العلة . الثاني : التقسيم والمقابلة : ومثاله؛ أن يستدل المالكي على أن قليل الحنطة يجري فيها الربا، لأن هذا مقتات مدخر للعيش غالبًا، فحرم فيه التفاضل كالكثير . فيقول الحنفي : ما الدليل على صحة العلة في الأصل؟ فيقول المالكي: لا يخلو أن تكون العلة ما ذكرناه أو الكيل أو الوزن على ما تقوله، ولا يجوز أن تكون علة الربا الكيل والوزن لأنه لو كان الربا في الموزونات يجري للوزن لما جاز إسلام الدراهم والدنانير فيها، لأن كل شيئين جمعتهما علة واحدة في الربا لم يجز إسلام أحدهما في الآخر، كالذهب والفضة، فلما اجتمعنا على جواز إسلام الذهب والفضة في الموزونات، دل على أن الوزن ليس بعلة للربا فيهما، فثبت أن علة الربا ما ذكرنا
B - Indicating the validity of the cause through deduction: and that by two ways: al-ta'thir and shahadat al-usul. 1 - Indicating by al-ta'thir: that the ruling is found with the existence of a meaning and ceases with its non-existence, so that indicates the ruling is attached to it and dependent on it. This is called al-'aks, and it has two aspects: first: al-salb wa al-wujud, and its example: that the Maliki or the Shafi'i argues concerning nabidh that it is forbidden, because it is a drink containing an intoxicating intensity, so it is forbidden like khamr. The Hanafi says: What is the evidence for the validity of this cause? The arguer answers: The evidence is the existence of the ruling with its existence and its removal with its removal: that grape juice before the occurrence of intensity is permissible; when intoxicating intensity occurs, it is ruled forbidden; when the intensity ceases, it is ruled permissible; if the intensity returns, the prohibition returns as before. So that indicates that it is the cause. Second: al-taqsim wa al-muqabala, and its example: that the Maliki argues that a small amount of wheat is subject to riba, because it is a staple food stored for livelihood generally, so disparity is forbidden in it like a large amount. The Hanafi says: What is the evidence for the validity of the cause in the original? The Maliki says: The cause is either what we mentioned or measurement by weight or volume as you say. It cannot be that the cause of riba is weight or volume because if riba in weighed items were due to weight, then it would not be permissible to exchange dirhams and dinars among them, because any two things united by one cause in riba, it is not permissible to exchange one for the other, like gold and silver. Since we agree on the permissibility of exchanging gold and silver in weighed items, that indicates that weight is not a cause for riba in them. So it is established that the cause of riba is what we mentioned.
Source pages p. 435

Commentary
This passage presents the first of two methods for proving the validity of a legal cause ('illa) in dialectical argumentation (jadal): al-ta'thir (effect). The author begins by defining al-ta'thir: the ruling is found when a certain meaning (the putative cause) is present and ceases when it is absent. This is called 'aks (inverse). He then distinguishes two subtypes. The first subtype, al-salb wa al-wujud (negation and existence), is illustrated with the example of nabidh (date wine). The Maliki or Shafi'i claims nabidh is forbidden because it shares with khamr the attribute of being an intoxicating drink. When challenged to prove the cause (intoxicating intensity), he shows that before intensity, the juice is halal; after intensity, it becomes haram; when intensity ceases (through vinegar), it becomes halal again. This empirical correlation proves that the intensity is the cause. The second subtype, al-taqsim wa al-muqabala (division and opposition), is illustrated with the example of riba in wheat. The Maliki claims that a small amount of wheat is subject to riba because it is a staple foodstuff, just like a large amount. The Hanafi objects by suggesting alternative causes: measurement by weight or volume. The Maliki refutes these by showing that if weight were the cause, then it would be impermissible to exchange dirhams and dinars for wheat (since they are also weighed), but there is consensus that such exchange is permissible. Thus, weight is eliminated as a cause, leaving only the original claim (being a staple) as the valid cause.

Key Terms
التأثير
العكس
السلب والوجود
التقسيم والمقابلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) By showing that grape juice is permissible before intensity, forbidden after, and permissible again when intensity ceases
C) By comparing nabidh to khamr only
D) By eliminating all other possible causes

Key Takeaway
Al-ta'thir validates a cause by demonstrating either temporal co-variation (salb wa wujud) or elimination of alternatives (taqsim wa muqabala).

The Method of Shahadat al-Usul
Author defines shahadat al-usul with three illustrative examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the structure of a shahadat al-usul argument and identify its difference from a simple analogy.

Core Idea
Shahadat al-usul proves a cause by demonstrating its consistency across multiple established legal principles.

In dialectical reasoning within usul al-fiqh, one often needs to prove that a proposed 'illa (cause) is valid. The method of shahadat al-usul appeals to the agreement of multiple legal principles (usul) that treat similar cases similarly. This lesson introduces the method as described by the author, with the first example from the Shafi'i school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim and proposed 'illa
Find the initial statement of the claimant (e.g., the Shafi'i on vomit). What is the ruling and what 'illa is given? Usually the 'illa is a property that is claimed to be the cause of the ruling.
2
Recognize the opponent's challenge
The opponent asks: 'What is the evidence for the validity of this cause?' (ما الدليل على صحة هذه العلة?). This is a standard jadal move to demand proof of the 'illa.
3
Understand the response: shahadat al-usul
The respondent appeals to the consistency of the usul (principles). He lists several established rulings that follow the same pattern, demonstrating that the 'illa is supported by the overall system.
The consistency is not just one analogy but multiple independent principles all aligning.
4
Formalize the argument
The argument form: If a certain property (e.g., 'large amount does not nullify if small amount does not') is found consistently across all principles, then it is a valid 'illa. This is a form of inductive generalization or pattern recognition.
In jadal, this is considered a strong proof if the opponent cannot show a counterexample.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ب - 2 - الدلالة بشهادة الأصول : وذلك مثل أن يقول الشافعي في القيء : إنه لا يبطل الوضوء، لأن ما لا ينقض قليله الوضوء لم ينقض كثيره الوضوء، كالدمع والعرق . فيقال له : ما الدليل على صحة هذه العلة؟ فيقول مجيبا : الدليل هو شهادة الأصول، وذلك أن الأصول متفقة على التسوية بين القليل والكثير في ما ينقض وفي ما لا ينقض الوضوء، ألا ترى أن البول والغائط والنوم والمباشرة، لما نقض استوى قليله وكثيره؟ وما لا ينقض كالدمع والعرق والكلام يستوي فيه الكثير والقليل، فدل على ما قلناه . ومثاله أيضًا : أن يقول المالكي في القهقهة : إنها لا تنقض الوضوء في الصلاة، لأن ما لا ينقض الوضوء خارج الصلاة لا ينقضه داخل الصلاة. فيقول الحنفي : ما الدليل على صحة هذه العلة؟ فيقول مجيبا : الدليل عليه أن أصول الشرع مبنية على أن ما لا ينقض الوضوء خارج الصلاة فإنه لا ينقضه داخل الصلاة، كالكلام والضحك واللعب والأكل وغير ذلك، وما ينقض الوضوء داخل الصلاة فإنه ينقضه خارج الصلاة كاللمس ومس الذكر والبول والغائط وسائر أنواع الأحداث، ولما رأينا القهقهة لا تنقض الوضوء خارج الصلاة، دل ذلك على أنها لا تنقضه في الصلاة . ومثاله أيضًا أن يقول الحنبلي : لا تجب الزكاة في إناث الخيل، لأنها لا تجب في ذكورها كالبغال والحمير والفيلة وغير ذلك من الحيوانات وعكسه الإبل والبقر والغنم .
B - 2 - Indicating by shahadat al-usul: such as when the Shafi'i says concerning vomit that it does not invalidate wudu, because that whose small amount does not nullify wudu, its large amount does not nullify wudu, like tears and sweat. It is said to him: What is the evidence for the validity of this cause? He answers: The evidence is shahadat al-usul, and that is that the principles agree on equal treatment between small and large amounts in what nullifies and what does not nullify wudu. Do you not see that urine, stool, sleep, and touching, when they nullify, their small and large amounts are equal? And what does not nullify, like tears, sweat, and speech, its large and small amounts are equal. So that indicates what we said. Another example: that the Maliki says concerning loud laughter: it does not nullify wudu in prayer, because what does not nullify wudu outside prayer does not nullify it inside prayer. The Hanafi says: What is the evidence for the validity of this cause? He answers: The evidence is that the principles of the Shari'a are based on the fact that what does not nullify wudu outside prayer does not nullify it inside prayer, like speech, laughter, play, eating, and other things. And what nullifies wudu inside prayer nullifies it outside prayer, like touching, touching the penis, urine, stool, and all types of impurities. Since we see that loud laughter does not nullify wudu outside prayer, that indicates that it does not nullify it in prayer. Another example: that the Hanbali says: Zakat is not obligatory on female horses, because it is not obligatory on male horses, like mules, donkeys, elephants, and other animals. The opposite is true for camels, cows, and sheep.
Source pages p. 436

Commentary
This passage presents the second method for proving the validity of a cause: shahadat al-usul (testimony of principles). The author provides three examples. The first example concerns the nullification of wudu by vomit. The Shafi'i argues that a large amount of vomit does not nullify wudu because its small amount does not nullify it, analogizing to tears and sweat. When asked to prove the cause (the principle that small and large amounts are treated the same), he appeals to the agreement of the principles: for things that do nullify (urine, stool, etc.), small and large are equal; for things that do not nullify (tears, sweat, speech), small and large are also equal. This uniformity across principles supports the cause. The second example is about loud laughter in prayer. The Maliki claims that laughter does not nullify wudu inside prayer because it does not nullify outside prayer. To prove the cause, he points to a consistent pattern in shari'a: things that nullify outside also nullify inside (e.g., contact with impurities), and things that do not nullify outside do not nullify inside (e.g., speech, ordinary laughter). Since laughter does not nullify outside, it should not nullify inside. The third example is about zakat on female horses. The Hanbali argues that zakat is not obligatory on female horses because it is not obligatory on male horses, just like mules, donkeys, etc. The opposite is true for camels, cows, and sheep, where zakat is obligatory. The passage does not show the refutation, but the method implies that the principle (no zakat on equines) is consistent across similar animals.

Key Terms
شهادة الأصول (shahadat al-usul): A method of proving a cause by appealing to the consistency of multiple legal principles that treat similar cases similarly. [Note: This term is specific to usul al-fiqh jadal and is not commonly used in mantiq or kalam.]
الأصول (al-usul): Here refers to the established legal principles or precedents in the Shari'a that demonstrate a pattern. [Note: In other contexts, 'usul' often refers to the science of usul al-fiqh itself.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) It shows that all legal principles treat small and large amounts the same for both nullifiers and non-nullifiers.
C) It appeals to the authority of the Shafi'i school.
D) It compares vomit to urine directly.

Key Takeaway
Shahadat al-usul validates a cause by demonstrating its consistency across multiple independent legal principles, providing a robust dialectical proof.

Applying Shahadat al-Usul: Three Fiqh Examples
Author illustrates shahadat al-usul with three examples from fiqh.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze a given fiqh dispute and apply shahadat al-usul to support a proposed cause.

Core Idea
Each example shows how shahadat al-usul works in a different legal context, revealing its flexibility and strength.

The passage provides three concrete examples of shahadat al-usul from different madhabs. By examining each, you will learn to identify the structure of the argument and apply it yourself. Let's walk through the Shafi'i, Maliki, and Hanbali arguments.

Guided Path
1
Analyze the Shafi'i argument on vomit
Identify the claim (vomit does not nullify wudu in large amount), the 'illa (small amount does not nullify), and the opponent's challenge. Then list the usul the Shafi'i invokes (urine, stool, sleep, touching: nullifiers; tears, sweat, speech: non-nullifiers). Notice the pattern: for nullifiers, all amounts nullify; for non-nullifiers, no amount nullifies. This uniform pattern supports the 'illa.
2
Analyze the Maliki argument on laughter in prayer
The Maliki claims that loud laughter does not nullify wudu inside prayer because it does not nullify outside prayer. The opponent (Hanafi) asks for proof. The Maliki responds by pointing to a consistent pattern in Shari'a: things that nullify outside also nullify inside, and things that do not nullify outside also do not nullify inside. He lists examples on both sides. Since laughter is a non-nullifier outside, it follows that it is also a non-nullifier inside.
This is a different pattern: inside/outside prayer, rather than small/large amount.
3
Analyze the Hanbali argument on zakat for horses
The Hanbali claims no zakat on female horses, analogous to male horses and other equines. The implied proof is that the principle 'no zakat on equines' is consistent across all similar animals (mules, donkeys, elephants), unlike camels, cows, and sheep which have zakat. The pattern is across species.
This example is more concise; the opponent's challenge and response are not shown, but the method is the same.
4
Compare the three examples
All three use the same method: they show that the proposed 'illa fits within a broader pattern established by multiple usul. The pattern differs in each case (amount, context, species), but the structure is identical.
Recognizing the common structure helps you apply it to new cases.

Commentary
This passage presents the second method for proving the validity of a cause: shahadat al-usul (testimony of principles). The author provides three examples. The first example concerns the nullification of wudu by vomit. The Shafi'i argues that a large amount of vomit does not nullify wudu because its small amount does not nullify it, analogizing to tears and sweat. When asked to prove the cause (the principle that small and large amounts are treated the same), he appeals to the agreement of the principles: for things that do nullify (urine, stool, etc.), small and large are equal; for things that do not nullify (tears, sweat, speech), small and large are also equal. This uniformity across principles supports the cause. The second example is about loud laughter in prayer. The Maliki claims that laughter does not nullify wudu inside prayer because it does not nullify outside prayer. To prove the cause, he points to a consistent pattern in shari'a: things that nullify outside also nullify inside (e.g., contact with impurities), and things that do not nullify outside do not nullify inside (e.g., speech, ordinary laughter). Since laughter does not nullify outside, it should not nullify inside. The third example is about zakat on female horses. The Hanbali argues that zakat is not obligatory on female horses because it is not obligatory on male horses, just like mules, donkeys, etc. The opposite is true for camels, cows, and sheep, where zakat is obligatory. The passage does not show the refutation, but the method implies that the principle (no zakat on equines) is consistent across similar animals.

Key Terms
شهادة الأصول (shahadat al-usul): A method of proving a cause by appealing to the consistency of multiple legal principles that treat similar cases similarly. [Note: This term is specific to usul al-fiqh jadal and is not commonly used in mantiq or kalam.]
الأصول (al-usul): Here refers to the established legal principles or precedents in the Shari'a that demonstrate a pattern. [Note: In other contexts, 'usul' often refers to the science of usul al-fiqh itself.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Maliki example, what is the key pattern used to prove that loud laughter does not nullify wudu inside prayer?
A) Small and large amounts are treated equally for laughter.
B) Laughter is similar to speech, which does not nullify wudu.
C) Everything that does not nullify wudu outside prayer also does not nullify it inside prayer.
D) The Maliki school has a direct text on laughter.

Key Takeaway
Shahadat al-usul arguments rely on demonstrating that the proposed cause fits a pattern observed across multiple legal principles, making it a powerful dialectical tool.

Defining 'Adam al-Ta'thir and Classifying Descriptors
Defines 'adam al-ta'thir and divides descriptors

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define 'adam al-ta'thir (lack of effect) with its three meanings and distinguish the two types of descriptors in legal dialectic.

Core Idea
Lack of effect means the ruling exists without the cause—a key objection in legal dialectic.

This passage introduces a critical objection used in usul al-fiqh debates: the claim that a proposed cause has no effect on the ruling. The author defines it with three distinct meanings and provides a concrete example from the Shafi'i argument on selling absent goods. He then divides descriptors into those whose removal nullifies the cause and those whose removal does not, clarifying when this objection is valid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three meanings of 'adam al-ta'thir
The author gives three formulations: (1) the ruling exists without the cause, (2) the ruling is not absent despite the absence of the cause, or (3) the ruling exists without the descriptor in the same case. Parse each meaning carefully.
These formulations are not mutually exclusive but capture different aspects of the objection.
2
Analyze the example from bay' al-gha'ib
The Shafi'i argues that selling an absent object is invalid because it is unseen, analogizing to a bird in the air. The objector responds that the bird's invalidity is due to inability to deliver, not being unseen—so the cause (unseen) has no effect.
Notice how the objector shows the ruling remains even when the descriptor is absent (if the bird were seen, it would still be invalid).
3
Distinguish the two types of descriptors
A descriptor whose removal nullifies the cause makes the objection invalid (the cause is still effective). A descriptor whose removal does not nullify the cause makes the objection valid—the cause lacks effect.
This division is crucial for evaluating whether a given 'adam al-ta'thir objection is sound.
4
Apply the division to the example
In the bird case, the descriptor 'unseen' is removed (even if seen, the ruling still holds). Therefore it falls into the second type—the objection is valid because the cause has no effect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المطلب الثاني : الاعتراض على العلة بعدم التأثير عدم التأثير، معناه : وجود الحكم مع عدم وجود العلة، أو هو ألا يعدم الحكم لعدم العلة في موضع من المواضع، أو هو وجود الحكم بدون الوصف في المحل الذي أثبتت عليته فيه . ومثاله : أن يقول الشافعي مستدلا على عدم صحة بيع الغائب : الغائب مبيع فلا غير مرئي يصح بيعه كالطير في الهواء .. فقد جعل العلة في عدم صحة بيع الطير في الهواء كونه غير مرئي، ليلحق بيع الغائب به في هذه العلة . فيقول المعترض له هذه العلة لا تأثير لها في الأصل لأن عدم الصحة يوجد في الطير ولو كان مرئيا، فإن الطير في الهواء لا يصح بيعه مطلقا مرئيا أو غير مرئي لعدم القدرة على تسليمه فالوصف غير مؤثر في الحكم لوجود الحكم بدونه والأوصاف على ضربين وصف إذا أسقط انتقضت العلة، وهذا الذي يخل بالعلة بعدم التأثير فيه والثاني ألا تنتقض العلة بإسقاطه، فهذا لا يخل بالعلة عدم التأثير فيه.
The second section: Objection to the cause by lack of effect. Lack of effect: its meaning is the existence of the ruling without the existence of the cause, or that the ruling is not absent due to the absence of the cause in some place, or it is the existence of the ruling without the descriptor in the locus in which its causality was established. Its example: that the Shafi'i says, arguing for the invalidity of selling an absent object: 'The absent object is a commodity that is not seen, so its sale is invalid, like a bird in the air.' So he made the cause for the invalidity of selling the bird in the air its being unseen, to attach the sale of the absent object to it in this cause. Then the objector says to him: 'This cause has no effect in the principle because invalidity exists in the bird even if it were seen, for the bird in the air is never valid to sell, whether seen or unseen, due to the inability to deliver it.' So the descriptor has no effect on the ruling because the ruling exists without it. And descriptors are of two types: a descriptor whose removal nullifies the cause, and this is what harms the cause by lack of effect; and the second: that the cause is not nullified by its removal, so this does not harm the cause by lack of effect.
Source pages p. 437

Commentary
This passage defines 'adam al-ta'thir (lack of effect) as an objection against a legal cause. It gives three meanings: (1) the ruling exists without the cause, (2) the ruling is not absent despite the absence of the cause, or (3) the ruling exists without the descriptor in the same case. The example clarifies: the Shafi'i argues that selling an absent object is invalid because the object is unseen, analogizing to a bird in the air. But the objector responds that the bird's invalidity is not due to being unseen but due to inability to deliver, so the cause is ineffective. The passage then divides descriptors into two types: those whose removal nullifies the cause (making the objection valid) and those whose removal does not nullify it (making the objection invalid). This division is crucial for distinguishing valid from invalid objections of this type.

العلة

Key Takeaway
The objection of lack of effect tests whether a ruling is truly tied to a cause; if the ruling persists without the cause, the cause is ineffective.

Objection by Lack of Effect: First Face
Presents the first face of this objection with a Maliki/Shafi'i example

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the first face of the objection by lack of effect in a dialectical exchange and reconstruct the Maliki/Shafi'i example.

Core Idea
Lack of effect in a descriptor does not automatically invalidate the cause; it triggers an answer.

In jadal al-usuliyyin, an objector may challenge a proponent's cause by arguing that the descriptor has no effect on the ruling when examined across different cases. This lesson dissects the first face of that objection, using a classic Maliki vs. Shafi'i debate on the imam's recitation. You will learn to pinpoint the exact logical move the objector makes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proponent's cause
The Maliki claims that when the imam recites aloud, the follower is prohibited from reciting. The cause is 'leading prayer with audible recitation' (جهر الإمام بالقراءة). Note that the ruling (prohibition of follower's recitation) is attached to this descriptor.
2
Understand the objector's challenge
The Shafi'i objects that the same prohibition exists when the imam recites silently and the follower joins in ruku'. Therefore, the descriptor 'audible recitation' has no effect—it does not change the ruling. This is the first face of the objection: the descriptor has effect in the proponent's school but not in the principles (uṣūl).
3
Parse the objection's structure
The objection argues that the cause is invalid because it fails a test of effect: if the ruling remains when the cause is absent, the cause is considered ineffective. In this case, even without audible recitation, the follower still does not recite, so the cause is lacking effect.
4
Connect to the broader dialectical move
Recognize that this is a specific subtype of objection by lack of effect. The objector is not denying the cause's existence but pointing to a counterexample that undermines its causal efficacy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أ - الاعتراض بعدم التأثير في الوصف الذي تنتقض العلة بإسقاطه : وهو على وجهين : أ - -1- أن يكون الوصف له تأثير على مذهب المستدل، والحكم يتعلق به عنده، ولكن لا تأثير له في الأصول : وذلك مثل أن يقول المالكي : إن من جهر بالقراءة وهو إمام، فلا يقرأ المأموم، والدليل على ذلك أن هذه صلاة جهر الإمام فيها بالقراءة، فكان المأموم ممنوعًا فيها من القراءة كما لو أدركه راكعا . فيقول شافعي معترضا : لا تأثير لقولك جهر «الإمام» في الأصل، فإنه لو أسر بالقراءة وأدركه المأموم راكعًا، لكان ممنوعًا أيضًا من القراءة، ومندوبا إلى اتباع الإمام وإدراك الركعة. والجواب على هذا من وجوه : الأول : أن يقول : إن عدم التأثير ليس بمفسد للعلة، إذ دل دليل آخر على صحتها، لأننا قد أجمعنا على أن ما جاز أن يثبت بالنص، جاز أن يثبت بالاستنباط، ولو نص صاحب الشرع على علة ولم يبين تأثيرها لوجب الحكم بصحتها، فكذلك إذا دل الدليل على صحة العلة المستنبطة ولم يبين تأثيرها وجب القول بصحتها، وإذا ثبت ذلك لم تصح مطالبتك بالتأثير، لأن ذلك اقتراح منك في الدليل على صحة العلة وذلك مصروف إليَّ أدلُّ على صحة العلة بما شئت من أنواع الأدلة غير التأثير .
A - The objection by lack of effect in the descriptor whose removal nullifies the cause: It is of two aspects: A-1- That the descriptor has effect according to the proponent's school, and the ruling is attached to it in his view, but it has no effect in the principles. Example: that the Maliki says: Whoever recites aloud while being an imam, then the follower does not recite. The evidence for that is that this is a prayer in which the imam recites aloud, so the follower is prohibited from reciting in it, as if he caught him in ruku'. Then a Shafi'i objects: 'Your statement "the imam recites aloud" has no effect in the principle, because if he recited silently and the follower caught him in ruku', he would still be prohibited from reciting, and would be encouraged to follow the imam and catch the rak'ah.' The answer to this is in aspects: The first: that he says: Lack of effect does not invalidate the cause, because another proof indicates its correctness, for we have agreed that what is permissible to establish by text is permissible to establish by derivation. If the Lawgiver had explicitly stated a cause and did not explain its effect, it would be obligatory to rule by its correctness. Similarly, if evidence indicates the correctness of a derived cause and its effect is not explained, it is obligatory to say it is correct. And if that is established, your demand for effect is not valid, because that is a proposal from you regarding the evidence for the correctness of the cause, and that is referred to me: I may prove the correctness of the cause by whatever types of evidence I wish, other than effect.
Source pages p. 437

Commentary
This passage presents the first face of the objection by lack of effect in descriptors that nullify the cause: when the descriptor has effect according to the proponent but not in the principles (uṣūl). The example is a Maliki argument that when the imam recites aloud, the follower should not recite, analogizing to the case where the follower catches the imam in ruku' (where the follower does not recite). The Shafi'i objects that the cause (imam's loud recitation) has no effect because if the imam recited silently and the follower caught him in ruku', the follower would still be prohibited from reciting. Thus the cause is ineffective. The Maliki's first answer is that lack of effect does not necessarily invalidate the cause, as long as other evidence supports its correctness. He argues by analogy to a textual cause: if the Lawgiver explicitly stated a cause without explaining its effect, we accept it; similarly, if a derived cause is supported by evidence other than showing its effect, it is valid. Therefore, the objector cannot demand proof of effect as the only way to validate the cause.

Key Terms
التأثير
الأصول
الاستنباط

B) The same prohibition exists when the imam recites silently.
C) The follower must always recite.
D) The analogy is weak.

Key Takeaway
The first face of the objection by lack of effect challenges a cause by showing that the ruling remains even when the descriptor is absent, indicating the cause is ineffective.

Answer to Lack of Effect: Sufficiency of Other Evidence
Gives the first answer that lack of effect does not invalidate if another proof exists

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the first answer to the objection by lack of effect: that a cause can be valid if supported by other evidence, even without demonstrating its effect.

Core Idea
Lack of effect is not a fatal flaw if other evidence proves the cause's correctness.

After the Shafi'i objects that the Maliki's cause lacks effect, the Maliki responds with a powerful principle: a cause need not show its effect if there is other evidence for its validity. This lesson unpacks that answer, which draws an analogy to textual causes from the Lawgiver. Understanding this response is crucial for defending causes in jadal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection being answered
Recall the Shafi'i's objection: the cause 'audible recitation' has no effect because the prohibition remains even when the imam recites silently. The Maliki now provides a general answer that applies to any such objection.
2
Extract the core principle of the answer
The Maliki says: 'Lack of effect does not invalidate the cause, because another proof indicates its correctness.' This means that effect is not the only way to validate a cause; other forms of proof (from text, consensus, etc.) can suffice.
3
Understand the analogy to a textual cause
The Maliki argues: if the Lawgiver explicitly stated a cause (e.g., 'wine is forbidden because it intoxicates') without explaining its effect, we still accept it. Similarly, if a derived cause is supported by evidence (e.g., a text indicating the underlying principle), it is valid even if the effect is not demonstrated.
4
Recognize the implication for the debate
The objector cannot demand that the proponent prove effect as the only method of validation. The proponent may use other types of evidence. Thus, the objection by lack of effect is not a decisive refutation; it merely shifts the burden to provide alternative proof.

Proving the Descriptor's Effect in Jadal: Text and Principles
Provides the second answer to the objection: proving the descriptor's effect through textual evidence and analogical reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a dialectical answer that proves a descriptor's effect using explicit text (nuṭq) and analogical reasoning (istidlāl bi'l-uṣūl).

Core Idea
A descriptor's effect can be proven by textual mention or by legal principles.

In a jadal exchange, the objector may claim that the selected descriptor has no effect. The proponent's second answer is to demonstrate that the descriptor does have an effect in the Shari'ah. This can be done by citing explicit scriptural text where the descriptor is mentioned in connection with the ruling, or by deriving from established legal principles that the descriptor has a recognized effect. In this lesson, we will study the logical structure of this dialectical move using the Maliki argument on loud recitation (jahar).

Guided Path
1
Identify the two sub-arguments
The passage presents two distinct ways: (a) by explicit text (nuṭq), (b) by derivation from principles (istidlāl bi'l-uṣūl). Identify each in the Arabic text.
The first method uses a hadith; the second method uses analogical reasoning.
2
Examine the textual evidence
The Maliki cites a hadith where the Prophet, after reciting aloud, asked if anyone recited with him, and said 'Why am I contended with the Quran?' The ruling is that followers should not recite when the imam recites aloud. Notice how the descriptor 'jahar' (loud recitation) is mentioned in the ruling, establishing it as the cause.
The principle 'mention of a descriptor in a ruling indicates causality' is invoked.
3
Apply the principle: mention of descriptor indicates causality
The author states that because the descriptor is mentioned in the context of the ruling, it serves as evidence that the descriptor is the cause. This is a key usul principle: ذِكْرُ الْوَصْفِ فِي الْحُكْمِ تَعْلِيلٌ.
Check how this principle justifies the proof: the hadith explicitly links the ruling to the descriptor.
4
Understand the analogical reasoning
The passage begins the second method (istidlāl bi'l-uṣūl) but cuts off. The idea is that by analogy, the imam's loud recitation has an effect similar to other known causes. For example, just as other causes prevent recitation, loud recitation does so by analogy.
Complete the reasoning: analogical reasoning uses established principles from usul al-fiqh.
5
Formulate the dialectical response
Summarize how the proponent constructs this response to counter the objection: the answer demonstrates that the descriptor indeed has an effect, rebutting the objection.
The burden shifts to the objector to challenge the evidence or the analogical link.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني : أن يبين للوصف تأثيرًا في الشرع، بأن يثبت ذلك بالنطق والاستدلال بالأصول، فمن الاستدلال بالنطق، أن يقول : الشرع الجهر قد ورد تأثيره وتعلق الحكم به، والدليل على ذلك ما روي أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم انصرف من صلاة جهر بالقراءة، فقال: «هل قرأ معي أحد منكم آنفًا؟، فقال رجل: نعم، أنا يا رسول الله، فقال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم : إني أقول : مالي أنازع القرآن؟»، فانتهى الناس عن القراءة مع رسول الله فيما جهر بالقراءة فيها فنقل الجهر في هذا الخبر وتعليق الحكم عليه دليل على كونه علة له، لأن ذكر الوصف في الحكم تعليل فدل على تأثيره في الحكم وكونه مقتضيا .. وأما الاستدلال بالأصول، فهو أن يقول : إن لجهر الإمام بالذكر تأثيرا في منع ...
The second: that he demonstrates for the descriptor an effect in the Shari'ah, by establishing that through explicit text and through derivation from principles. As for derivation from explicit text, he says: The Lawgiver has indicated the effect of loud recitation and the attachment of the ruling to it. The evidence for that is what is narrated that the Prophet, peace be upon him, finished a prayer in which he recited aloud, and said: 'Did any of you recite with me just now?' A man said: 'Yes, O Messenger of Allah.' The Messenger of Allah said: 'I say, why am I contended with in the Quran?' So the people stopped reciting with the Messenger of Allah in what he recited aloud. So the mention of loud recitation in this report and the attachment of the ruling to it is evidence that it is the cause for it, because the mention of the descriptor in the ruling is an indication of causality, so it indicates its effect on the ruling and that it is a requisite. As for derivation from principles, he says: The imam's loud recitation of dhikr has an effect in preventing...
Source pages p. 438

Commentary
This passage presents the second answer to the objection of lack of effect. The Maliki can prove that the descriptor (imam's loud recitation) actually has an effect in the Shari'ah in two ways. First, by explicit text: the hadith where the Prophet, after a prayer with loud recitation, asked if anyone recited with him, and when a man said yes, the Prophet said 'Why am I contended with the Quran?' This indicates that loud recitation is the cause for prohibiting the follower from reciting. The principle is that mentioning a descriptor in a ruling indicates its causality. Second, by derivation from principles (uṣūl), though the text cuts off, the idea is that the imam's loud recitation has an effect in preventing the follower from reciting, analogically to other cases. This shows that even if the objector claims lack of effect, the proponent can prove effect through textual and analogical evidence.

الاستدلال بالأصول
الوصف

D) The imam's status.

Key Takeaway
In jadal, proving a descriptor's effect requires either a textual mention linking it to the ruling or an analogical inference from established principles.

Objection of Lack of Effect in the Original Case and Response
Author explains the objection and two responses to 'adam al-ta'thir in usuli jadal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the objection of 'adam al-ta'thir and reconstruct the two responses (methodological and evidentiary) used in usuli jadal.

Core Idea
An 'illa can be valid even if its effect is hidden; evidence from text or principles proves it.

In jadal, a common tactic is to object that the proposed cause ('illa) has no observable effect in the original case. This passage from a usul al-fiqh debate shows how the respondent can defend the cause on two fronts: first by arguing that lack of effect does not invalidate the cause if other evidence supports it, and second by providing explicit textual or analogical proof of the cause's effect. Understanding this dialectical move is essential for anyone engaging in juristic reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection (mu'tarad)
The objector says that loud recitation (jahr) has no effect because even if the imam recites silently and the follower catches him bowing, the follower is still prohibited from reciting. So loudness is irrelevant.
This is a classic 'adam al-ta'thir objection.
2
Analyze the first response (methodological)
The respondent argues that lack of effect in one case does not necessarily invalidate the cause, as long as there is other evidence supporting the cause. He draws an analogy with divine law: if the Lawgiver explicitly states a cause without showing its effect, the cause is still valid. Similarly, if evidence indicates a deduced cause, it is valid even without manifest effect.
This response shifts the burden: the objector cannot demand effect as the only proof.
3
Analyze the second response (evidentiary)
The respondent provides two proofs: from explicit text (nass) – a hadith where the Prophet attached the ruling to loudness – and from analogy (usul) – consensus that it is disliked to recite when the imam recites loudly, but permissible when he recites silently. This directly shows that loudness has an effect.
The second response proves effect rather than denying its necessity.
4
Assess the dialectical outcome
In jadal, the respondent's two-pronged answer successfully rebuts the objection. The first response neutralizes the demand for effect, and the second provides independent proof of effect. Thus, the cause remains valid.
This demonstrates the sophistication of usuli jadal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيقول شافعي معترضا : لا تأثير لقولك جهر «الإمام» في الأصل، فإنه لو أسر بالقراءة وأدركه المأموم راكعًا، لكان ممنوعًا أيضًا من القراءة، ومندوبًا إلى اتباع الإمام وإدراك الركعة. والجواب على هذا من وجوه : الأول : أن يقول : إن عدم التأثير ليس بمفسد للعلة، إذ دل دليل آخر على صحتها، لأننا قد أجمعنا على أن ما جاز أن يثبت بالنص، جاز أن يثبت بالاستنباط، ولو نص صاحب الشرع على علة ولم يبين تأثيرها لوجب الحكم بصحتها، فكذلك إذا دل الدليل على صحة العلة المستنبطة ولم يبين تأثيرها وجب القول بصحتها، وإذا ثبت ذلك لم تصح مطالبتك بالتأثير، لأن ذلك اقتراح منك في الدليل على صحة العلة وذلك مصروف إليَّ أدلُّ على صحة العلة بما شئت من أنواع الأدلة غير التأثير . والثاني : أن يبين للوصف تأثيرًا في الشرع، بأن يثبت ذلك بالنطق والاستدلال بالأصول، فمن الاستدلال بالنطق، أن يقول : الشرع الجهر قد ورد تأثيره وتعلق الحكم به، والدليل على ذلك ما روي أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم انصرف من صلاة جهر بالقراءة، فقال: «هل قرأ معي أحد منكم آنفًا؟، فقال رجل: نعم، أنا يا رسول الله، فقال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم : إني أقول : مالي أنازع القرآن؟»، فانتهى الناس عن القراءة مع رسول الله فيما جهر بالقراءة فيها فنقل الجهر في هذا الخبر وتعليق الحكم عليه دليل على كونه علة له، لأن ذكر الوصف في الحكم تعليل فدل على تأثيره في الحكم وكونه مقتضيا .. وأما الاستدلال بالأصول، فهو أن يقول : إن لجهر الإمام بالذكر تأثيرا في منع القراءة، والدليل على أنه لا خلاف بيننا أنه يكره أن يقرأ في حين قراءة الإمام بالجهر، وإنما يقرأ عندهم في السكت بعد قراءة أم القرآن، وإذا أسر الإمام القراءة قرأ في حين قراءته.
A Shafi'i objector says: Your statement 'loud recitation of the imam' has no effect in the original case, because if he recited silently and the follower caught him bowing, the follower would also be prohibited from reciting and recommended to follow the imam and catch the rak'ah. The answer to this is in several ways: First: He says that lack of effect does not invalidate the cause, since another evidence indicates its validity, because we have agreed that what is permissible to prove by text is permissible to prove by deduction, and if the Lawgiver specified a cause and did not clarify its effect, it would be obligatory to rule it valid; likewise, if an evidence indicates the validity of a deduced cause and does not clarify its effect, it is obligatory to rule its validity. When this is established, your demand for effect is not valid, because that is a proposal from you regarding the evidence for the validity of the cause, and that is left to me to provide evidence for the validity of the cause by whatever types of evidence I wish other than effect. Second: He clarifies that the description has an effect in the Law, by proving that through explicit text and analogy from principles. As for proof by explicit text: he says that loud recitation in the Law has its effect and the ruling is attached to it; evidence for that is what is narrated that the Prophet, peace be upon him, turned from a prayer in which he recited loudly and said: 'Did any of you recite with me just now?' A man said: 'Yes, I did, O Messenger of God.' So the Messenger of God, peace be upon him, said: 'Indeed I say: Why am I contended with in the Quran?' So the people stopped reciting with the Messenger of God when he recited loudly. Mentioning loudness in this hadith and attaching the ruling to it is evidence that it is a cause for it, because mentioning the description in the ruling is a causal indication, showing its effect in the ruling and that it is a requisite... As for analogy from principles: He says that the imam's loud recitation of dhikr has an effect in prohibiting recitation, and the evidence is that there is no dispute among us that it is disliked to recite while the imam recites loudly; rather, they recite during the silence after reciting Surat al-Fatiha, and if the imam recites silently, they recite during his recitation.
Source pages p. 438

Commentary
This passage presents a dialectical exchange in the context of usul al-fiqh. A Shafi'i jurist raises an objection (naqd) against a Maliki's argument that loud recitation (jahr) of the imam is the 'illa (cause) for prohibiting the follower from reciting. The objector argues that the description 'jahr' has no effect (la ta'thir) because even if the imam recited silently and the follower caught him bowing, the follower would still be prohibited from reciting and recommended to follow the imam. Thus, loudness is not the operative cause. The answer (jawab) provides two responses. First, the respondent argues that lack of effect in one particular original case does not invalidate the 'illa as long as there is other evidence supporting it. He draws an analogy: if the Lawgiver explicitly stated a cause without clarifying its effect, the cause would still be accepted; similarly, if evidence points to a deduced cause without effect being manifest, it remains valid. Therefore, demanding effect is an unwarranted condition. Second, the respondent can prove the effect of loudness by explicit text (nass) and by analogy from principles (istidlal bi'l-usul). The explicit text is a hadith where the Prophet stopped people from reciting when he recited loudly, showing that loudness is attached to the prohibition. The analogy is that there is consensus that it is disliked to recite when the imam recites loudly, whereas if he recites silently, the follower recites during his recitation, indicating that loudness is the operative factor.

Key Terms
عدم التأثير
علة
الجهر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) By arguing that lack of effect in one case does not invalidate the cause if other evidence supports it, and then proving the effect through hadith and analogy.
C) By changing the cause to something else.
D) By accepting the objection and concluding that loud recitation is not a valid cause.

Key Takeaway
The objection of lack of effect can be countered either by arguing that effect is not a necessary condition if other evidence exists, or by directly proving the effect through text or principles.
