Objection of No Effect in Any Principle and Its Response
Author presents the objection that the description has no effect in any principle and the response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the 'lack of effect' (adam al-ta'thir) objection in a jadal exchange and evaluate the two standard responses to defend a cause.

Core Idea
Lack of effect is not fatal if other proofs support the cause.

In this passage, the author presents a classic dialectical maneuver in usul al-fiqh: the objector claims that the description offered as the 'illa has no actual effect on the ruling, neither in the original case nor in any other established principle. This objection, if left unanswered, would destroy the analogy. The author provides two strategies for the defender: either prove the description does have an effect, or argue that effect is only one proof and its absence does not invalidate the cause. This lesson helps you recognize this objection and respond effectively in jadal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection structure
The objector claims that a description has no effect on the ruling in the original case (asl) or in any other valid principle (usul). In the example, the description is 'not preceded by disobedience' and the ruling is 'repetition (odd number of stones)'. Locate the description and ruling in the passage.
2
Examine the objector's evidence
The objector uses istinja' and ramy al-jimar to show that the description does not affect the ruling: both cases require odd numbers regardless of prior sin. Understand why the objector thinks the description is irrelevant here.
3
Analyze the first response: prove effect
The defender can try to demonstrate that the description does have an effect on some ruling. For example, one might find a related case where the presence or absence of prior sin changes the required number. If such an effect is shown, the objection fails.
4
Analyze the second response: effect is not the only proof
The defender can argue that even if the description lacks effect in some principles, the cause can still be valid if supported by other evidence (text, consensus, etc.). This shifts the burden back to the objector.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ألا يكون للوصف تأثير على أصل المعلل ولا في شيء من الأصول : وهو مثل أن يقول شافعي أو بعض المالكية في الاستجمار : إنه لابد فيه من العدد، لأنه عبادة تتعلق بالأحجار لم تتقدمها معصية، فوجب فيها التكرار، كرمي الجمار. فيقول له مالكي مخالف : لا تأثير لهذا الوصف لأن ما تقدمه معصية وما لم يتقدمه في باب العدد ،سواء، ألا ترى أن في الاستنجاء لا فرق بين أن تتقدمه وبين ألا تتقدمه في أن العدد معتبر به عندك، وكذلك رمي الجمار بالإجماع يعتبر فيه العدد سواء تقدمته معصية أو لم تتقدمه، وإذا لم يكن لهذا الوصف تأثير على ما بيناه لم يجز أن يجعل علة على أصلك . والجواب عنه أن يبين - أولا - أن له تأثيرًا فيما علق عليه من الأحكام إن وجد إلى ذلك سبيلا .. والثاني: أن يقول : هذا غير لازم لي، لأن التأثير دليل من أدلة صحة العلة وليس إذا عدم بعض أدلتها مما يوجب بطلانها، لأني أدل على صحتها بدليل آخر من نص أو غيره.
Or that the description has no effect on the original case of the person who gives the cause, nor in any of the principles: such as when a Shafi'i or some Malikis say regarding istijmar (cleaning with stones): that it requires repetition, because it is a worship connected to stones that was not preceded by disobedience, so repetition is necessary, like ramy al-jimār. Then a Maliki objector says: This description has no effect because what is preceded by disobedience and what is not preceded are the same in the matter of repetition. Do you not see that in istinja' (cleaning with water) there is no difference between its being preceded by disobedience or not, in that repetition is considered in your view? Likewise, ramy al-jimār by consensus considers repetition whether disobedience preceded it or not. When this description has no effect as we have shown, it is not permissible to make it a cause according to your principle. The answer to this is: First, to show that it has an effect on the rulings attached to it if there is a way to do so. Second, to say: This is not binding on me, because effect is one of the proofs of the validity of the cause, and if some of its proofs are absent, that does not necessitate its invalidity, because I can prove its validity by another proof from text or other evidence.
Source pages p. 439

Commentary
This passage presents another dialectical objection concerning lack of effect (adam al-ta'thir). Here, the objector claims that the description (not being preceded by disobedience) has no effect either in the original case (istijmar) or in any other established principle (asl). The example given is a claim by a Shafi'i or Maliki that istijmar (cleaning with stones after toilet) requires an odd number of stones because it is a form of worship involving stones not preceded by a sin (ma'siya), like ramy al-jimār (stoning the Jamarat in Hajj). The objector argues that the description of not being preceded by sin is irrelevant because in other cases (istinja' with water, and ramy al-jimār itself) the number is considered whether or not sin preceded. Thus, the description has no effect, and it cannot be a valid cause on the objector's own principle. The answer provides two responses. First, the defender should try to demonstrate that the description does have an effect on the rulings to which it is attached, if possible. Second, the defender can argue that the objection is not binding because effect (ta'thir) is only one proof among many for the validity of the cause; absence of effect does not necessitate invalidity, as long as other proofs (text or principles) exist. This reflects the dialectical principle that objections based on lack of effect are not always decisive if the cause can be supported by other evidence.

Key Terms
تأثير
أصل
الاستجمار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The defender must admit the cause is invalid.
B) The defender can argue that effect is only one proof of validity, and other proofs can support the cause.
C) The defender can change the description.
D) The defender can abandon the analogy.

Key Takeaway
The objection of lack of effect (adam al-ta'thir) is not always decisive; the defender can either prove the description's effect or rely on other proofs to sustain the cause.

The objector says: 'But supererogatory prayers also require intention, and they are not preceded by sin? That shows no effect.' Formulate the second response from the defender's perspective.

Objection of Redundant Description (Hashw) and Response
Author explains the objection that a description is redundant (hashw) and the response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a description in a cause is being attacked as hashw and formulate the appropriate response of emphasis (ta'kid).

Core Idea
A redundant description (hashw) can be defended as mere emphasis (ta'kid) without harming the cause.

In juristic dialectics, an opponent may attack a causal description (wasf) by claiming it is superfluous — removing it does not change the ruling. This is the objection of hashw. The passage explains the objection and the first response: that the description is only for emphasis (ta'kid). Understanding this move is essential for defending causal arguments in usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection of hashw
Look for an opponent's claim that a particular description in the cause is redundant: if removed, the cause still holds and the ruling remains unchanged.
The key term 'hashw' literally means stuffing or padding.
2
Understand the significance of the objection
If a description is truly hashw, it means the cause does not depend on it; the objector uses this to undermine the causal reasoning.
3
Examine the response of ta'kid
The proponent responds that the description is not essential but added for emphasis (ta'kid) to reinforce the argument.
Ta'kid shows the description is explanatory, not a necessary component.
4
Apply to the example
In the Shafi'i hybrid animal case, the description 'one of which has no zakat in any case' could be seen as hashw. The response is that it is only for emphasis.
The hybrid's origin from a gazelle (no zakat) is emphasized to clarify the analogy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ب - الاعتراض بعدم التأثير على الوصف الذي لا تنتقض العلة بإسقاطه : وهذا الوصف يسمى الحشو في العلة، أي أنه وصف زائد لا تأثير له فيها وجودًا أو عدما . والجواب عن هذا الاعتراض إذا توجه به من وجوه الأول : أن يقول المستدل أن هذه الزيادة ذكرتها للتأكيد، وذلك مثل أن يقول شافعي في المتولد بين الظباء والغنم : إنه لا زكاة فيه لأنه متولد بين أصلين لا زكاة أحدهما بحال، فلم تجب فيه الزكاة كما لو كانت الأمهات من الظباء.
B - The objection of lack of effect regarding a description by whose removal the cause is not invalidated: This description is called hashw (redundant) in the cause, i.e., it is an extra description that has no effect in it, whether present or absent. The answer to this objection, if made, is in several ways. First: The proponent says that this addition is mentioned for emphasis. For example, a Shafi'i says regarding the hybrid between gazelles and sheep: There is no zakat on it because it is a hybrid between two origins, one of which has no zakat in any case. So zakat is not obligatory on it, just as if the mothers were from gazelles.
Source pages p. 439

Commentary
This passage addresses the objection of a redundant description (hashw) in the cause. A hashw is a description that, if removed, does not affect the original cause; it is extraneous and has no impact (whether present or absent) on the ruling. For instance, in the example of a hybrid animal between gazelles and sheep, a Shafi'i argues that no zakat is due because the animal is a hybrid between two origins (usul), one of which (the gazelle) never requires zakat. The objector may claim that the phrase 'one of which has no zakat in any case' (لا زكاة أحدهما بحال) is redundant – perhaps both origins require no zakat, so the condition is unnecessary. The answer is that such an addition is merely for emphasis (ta'kid) and not an essential component of the cause. The defender thus clarifies that the redundant description does not weaken the overall causal reasoning; it is simply stylistic or explanatory.

Key Terms
الحشو
التأكيد
المتولد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone objects that a description in a cause is hashw (redundant) and has no effect, how might the proponent respond according to this passage?
A) By removing the description entirely.
B) By claiming that the description is actually the main cause.
C) By stating that the description was added for emphasis and is not essential to the cause.
D) By ignoring the objection.

Key Takeaway
The hashw objection can be neutralized by asserting that the redundant description is merely for emphasis (ta'kid) and does not affect the causal efficacy.

Consider a Shafi'i argument: 'Wine is forbidden because it is an intoxicating liquid.' An objector claims 'liquid' is hashw since intoxication alone suffices. Formulate the response using ta'kid.

Objection of Lack of Effect in Jadal
Defines the first type of objection of lack of effect and gives a fiqh example with two answers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the objection of lack of effect in a jadal argument and construct the two valid responses.

Core Idea
A description may lack effect but the cause stands if supported elsewhere.

In dialectical debate (jadal), one common objection is that the description attributed to the legal cause (illah) has no actual impact on the ruling. This lesson unpacks that objection using a concrete fiqh example and shows the two ways a claimant can defend their cause. Understanding this helps you navigate objections in jurisprudential reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Find in the passage where the opponent claims the description has no effect. Note the phrase 'لا تأثير لهذا الوصف' (this description has no effect).
The objection is that the description fails to make a difference in the ruling.
2
Analyze the example
Work through the Shafi'i argument: istijmar requires a number because it is a worship related to stones not preceded by sin, like stoning the jamarat. Then the Maliki counter: the description 'not preceded by sin' does not affect the number, since in istinja' and jamarat the number is considered regardless.
The opponent shows the description is irrelevant by pointing to cases where it is absent yet the ruling remains.
3
Understand the first answer
The first response is to show that the description does have an effect on some ruling attached to it. The claimant must find a case where the absence of the description changes the ruling.
If the claimant can demonstrate effect, the objection fails.
4
Understand the second answer
The second response is to argue that even if this particular proof of effect is missing, the cause can still be valid if supported by other evidence (text, consensus, etc.).
The claimant does not need to prove effect in every case; other proofs can establish the cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ألا يكون للوصف تأثير على أصل المعلل ولا في شيء من الأصول : وهو مثل أن يقول شافعي أو بعض المالكية في الاستجمار : إنه لابد فيه من العدد، لأنه عبادة تتعلق بالأحجار لم تتقدمها معصية، فوجب فيها التكرار، كرمي الجمار . فيقول له مالكي مخالف : لا تأثير لهذا الوصف لأن ما تقدمه معصية وما لم يتقدمه في باب العدد ،سواء، ألا ترى أن في الاستنجاء لا فرق بين أن تتقدمه وبين ألا تتقدمه في أن العدد معتبر به عندك، وكذلك رمي الجمار بالإجماع يعتبر فيه العدد سواء تقدمته معصية أو لم تتقدمه، وإذا لم يكن لهذا الوصف تأثير على ما بيناه لم يجز أن يجعل علة على أصلك . والجواب عنه أن يبين - أولا - أن له تأثيرًا فيما علق عليه من الأحكام إن وجد إلى ذلك سبيلا .. والثاني: أن يقول : هذا غير لازم لي، لأن التأثير دليل من أدلة صحة العلة وليس إذا عدم بعض أدلتها مما يوجب بطلانها، لأني أدل على صحتها بدليل آخر من نص أو غيره
That the description has no effect on the original principle nor on any of the principles: Such as a Shafi'i or some Malikis say regarding istijmar (using stones for cleaning): It must have a number, because it is a worship related to stones that was not preceded by a sin, so repetition is required, like the stoning of the jamarat. Then a Maliki opponent says: This description has no effect, because what is preceded by a sin and what is not preceded by sin are equal in the matter of number. Do you not see that in istinja' (using water for cleaning) there is no difference between it being preceded by sin or not in that the number is considered according to you? Similarly, stoning the jamarat by consensus considers the number whether it is preceded by sin or not. Since this description has no effect as we have shown, it is not permissible to make it a cause according to your principle. The answer to this is: First, to show that it does have an effect on the rulings attached to it, if a way to that is found. Second, to say: This does not necessarily apply to me, because effect is one of the proofs of validity of the cause, and the absence of some of its proofs does not invalidate it, because I can prove its validity by another proof, whether text or other.
Source pages p. 439

Commentary
This passage explains the first type of objection called 'adam al-ta'thir (lack of effect) in the context of jadal (dialectical debate). The objection is that a particular description attributed to the cause (illah) does not actually affect the ruling either on the original case (asl) or on any other principle. The author gives an example from istijmar (using stones for purification after relieving oneself). A Shafi'i argues that repetition of stones is required because it is an act of worship involving stones that was not preceded by sin, similar to stoning of the jamarat in Hajj. The Maliki opponent objects that the description 'not preceded by sin' has no effect, because in istinja' (using water) the number is considered regardless of sin, and in stoning the jamarat it is also considered regardless. Therefore the description is irrelevant. The answer provides two responses: first, if possible, show that the description does have an effect on some ruling; second, argue that even if this proof is lacking, the cause can still be valid if supported by other evidence. This illustrates that in jadal, an objection of lack of effect does not automatically defeat the cause.

An objection of lack of effect can be answered either by proving the description's effect in other cases or by citing alternative evidence for the cause.

Objection of Filler (Hashw) and the Answer of Emphasis
Author presents the objection of hashw and the first answer: emphasis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify an objection of hashw (filler) in a cause and evaluate the validity of the answer of emphasis in a dialectical exchange on legal reasoning.

Core Idea
A redundant description in the cause can be defended as Arabic linguistic emphasis, not filler.

In dialectical reasoning on causes ('illal), an opponent may object that a part of the cause is redundant (hashw) — removing it does not affect the ruling. The proponent's first answer is that the extra word is for emphasis (ta'kid), a common feature of Arabic. This lesson unpacks the objection and the response, using the example of zakat on a hybrid animal.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection of hashw
The opponent claims that a certain description in the cause has no effect on the ruling; if removed, the cause still holds. So it is redundant and should be omitted.
Hashw literally means filler — something that does not contribute to the logical validity of the cause.
2
Identify the proponent's first answer: emphasis
The proponent states that the extra word is mentioned for emphasis, which is legitimate in Arabic. Emphasis does not change the logical structure but strengthens the expression.
3
Examine the example from Shafi'i on zakat for a hybrid
The Shafi'i argues that a hybrid between gazelle and sheep has no zakat because one of its origins (gazelle) definitely has no zakat. The opponent says 'definitely' (bi-hal) is redundant.
The cause is 'it is a hybrid between two origins, one of which has no zakat.' The word 'definitely' adds no logical weight.
4
See the Quranic precedent for emphasis
The verse 'the angels prostrated, all of them together' (kulluhum ajma'un) uses double emphasis, showing that such linguistic practice is not considered vain or hashw.
The Quranic example justifies that emphasis is a legitimate feature of Arabic, even if it seems redundant.
5
Evaluate the strength of this answer
Does emphasizing a word that is logically unnecessary make it acceptable? In legal reasoning, extra wording may be harmless if it does not contradict the cause. The answer of emphasis is accepted under the principle that Arabic style permits ta'kid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ب - الاعتراض بعدم التأثير على الوصف الذي لا تنتقض العلة بإسقاطه : وهذا الوصف يسمى الحشو في العلة، أي أنه وصف زائد لا تأثير له فيها وجودًا أو عدما . والجواب عن هذا الاعتراض إذا توجه به من وجوه الأول : أن يقول المستدل أن هذه الزيادة ذكرتها للتأكيد، وذلك مثل أن يقول شافعي في المتولد بين الظباء والغنم : إنه لا زكاة فيه لأنه متولد بين أصلين لا زكاة أحدهما بحال، فلم تجب فيه الزكاة كما لو كانت الأمهات من الظباء. فيقول المخالف : لا تأثير لقولك بحال»، فإنك لو اقتصرت على قولك : لا زكاة في أحدهما لم ينتقض بشيء، فقولك بحال» حشو في العلة لا يحتاج إليه . فيقول الشافعي : هذا ذكرته للتأكيد، وتأكيد الألفاظ لغة العرب فلا يعد حشوا، ولهذا قال الله تعالى : (نَسَجَدَ الْمَلَيْكَةُ كُلُّهُمْ أَجْمَعُونَ ﴾ (1)، فأكد ثم أكد ولم يعد ذلك لغوا .
B - The objection of lack of effect on a description whose removal does not break the cause: This description is called 'hashw' (filler) in the cause, i.e., it is an extra description with no effect in existence or absence. The answer to this objection, if directed, is from several aspects: First: That the proponent says, 'I mentioned this extra part for emphasis.' For example, a Shafi'i says regarding an animal hybrid between a gazelle and a sheep: 'There is no zakat on it because it is a hybrid between two origins, one of which definitely has no zakat, so zakat is not obligatory on it, just as if the mothers were gazelles.' The opponent says: 'Your word "definitely" has no effect, for if you only said "one of which has no zakat" would not break the cause. So your word "definitely" is filler in the cause that is not needed.' The Shafi'i says: 'I mentioned it for emphasis, and emphasizing words is an Arabic linguistic practice, so it is not considered filler. For this reason Allah Almighty said: "[Did you not see that] the angels prostrated, all of them together?" (Quran 15:30, 38:73) He emphasized, then emphasized again, and that was not considered vain.'
Source pages p. 440

Commentary
This passage introduces the second type of objection: an objection that a description in the cause is 'hashw' (filler or redundant) — i.e., it has no effect on the cause, and even if it were removed, the cause would still be valid. The author then presents the first of three answers: the extra description is mentioned for emphasis (ta'kid). The example is from a Shafi'i ruling that a hybrid between a gazelle and a sheep is not subject to zakat because it is born from two origins, one of which (the gazelle) definitely has no zakat. The opponent objects that the word 'definitely' (bi-hal) is redundant, because the cause would still be valid without it. The Shafi'i answers that it is for emphasis, and such emphasis is a common feature of Arabic language, citing the Quranic verse where Allah says 'all of them together' (kulluhum ajma'un) — double emphasis. Thus, the extra word is not considered vain or hashw.

Responding to the Hashw Objection: Ta'kid al-Hukm
Author gives the second answer to the hashw objection: emphasis on the ruling via the qadhf example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when an extra description in a cause serves as emphasis (ta'kid al-hukm) rather than filler (hashw) in a juristic analogy.

Core Idea
An extra description can emphasize the ruling by highlighting the gravity of the cause.

In dialectical debates about legal analogy, opponents often object that a description in the cause is redundant (hashw). The mugib (respondent) can answer that the extra description is not vain but serves to emphasize the ruling. This lesson unpacks that second answer using the real example of qadhf (false accusation of unchastity).

Guided Path
1
Identify the opponent's objection
The opponent claims that the phrase 'necessitates the hadd' is hashw (filler) and not needed in the cause. Locate the exact wording of the objection in the passage.
2
Recognize the second answer: emphasis on the ruling
The respondent argues that the extra description was added to emphasize the ruling (ta'kid al-hukm). Understand that emphasis strengthens the causal connection.
3
Analyze the qadhf example
The Shafi'i argues that qadhf leads to rejection of testimony because it is a major sin that necessitates the hadd, like adultery. The hadd (fixed punishment) indicates the gravity of the sin, which reinforces the ruling.
4
Articulate the logical justification
The gravity of the cause (the sin) necessitates emphasis of the ruling. Therefore, mentioning the hadd is not vain—it strengthens the causal link.
5
Apply to another case
Think of another example where an extra description could emphasize a ruling. For instance, in the prohibition of wine, intoxicating effect is the cause. Adding 'it leads to prayer abandonment' emphasizes the severity.
This step is optional but encourages deeper understanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني : : أن يقول : هذه الزيادة ذكرتها لتأكيد الحكم، وذلك مثل أن يقول الشافعي في القذف : إنه يتعلق به رد الشهادة لأنها كبيرة توجب الحد فتعلق بها رد الشهادة كالزنا . . فيقول المخالف : قولك يوجب الحد حشو في العلة لا يحتاج إليه .، والجواب أن يقول : تعلق الحد بها يدل على تأكدها، وتأكد العلة يوجب تأكيد الحكم، وما يوجب تأكيد الحكم لا يعد لغوّا.
And the second: That he says: I mentioned this extra part for emphasis of the ruling. For example, the Shafi'i says regarding false accusation (qadhf): Rejection of testimony is attached to it because it is a major sin that necessitates the hadd (fixed punishment), so rejection of testimony is attached to it like adultery. The opponent says: Your saying 'necessitates the hadd' is filler in the cause that is not needed. The answer is to say: The attachment of the hadd to it indicates its gravity, and the gravity of the cause necessitates emphasis of the ruling, and what necessitates emphasis of the ruling is not considered vain.

Commentary
This passage presents the second answer to the hashw objection: the extra description is meant to emphasize the ruling (ta'kid al-hukm). The example is from qadhf (false accusation of unchastity). A Shafi'i argues that rejection of testimony is attached to qadhf because it is a major sin that necessitates the hadd (fixed punishment), just like adultery. The opponent objects that the phrase 'necessitates the hadd' is redundant, because the main point is that it is a major sin. The answer is that the attachment of the hadd indicates the gravity of the sin, and gravity of the cause reinforces the ruling. Therefore, it is not vain but serves to emphasize the ruling.

Distinguishing Bayan from Hashw in Dialectical Objections
Author gives the third answer to the hashw objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish an objectionable filler (hashw) from a permissible clarification (bayan) by applying the criterion of whether the extra description alters the scope of the cause, using the tahari example.

Core Idea
An extra description is not always filler—it may clarify what the cause already implies.

In this passage, the author presents the third response to the objection that a description in the cause is redundant. The key is whether the description merely repeats what is already understood from the cause or whether it explains its meaning. The example from legal estimation (tahari) illustrates this distinction, and the author contrasts it with a counterexample where an added word changes the cause's scope.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The opponent claims the phrase 'when the number of permissible items is more' is filler because the cause 'a category subject to estimation' already suffices. Find this objection in the Arabic text.
The opponent's statement begins with 'فإنك لو قلت جنس يدخله التحري لكفى'.
2
Understand the response: bayan
The Shafi'i answers that the phrase is a clarification (bayan) of what the cause implies. The cause 'a category subject to estimation' implicitly means estimation applies when permissible items are more. Clarifying this is not redundant because it makes the intended meaning explicit.
The author says: 'هذا بيان لما تقتضيه العلة'.
3
Contrast with the staple/food example
The author distinguishes this from a case where adding 'staple' (qut) to 'food' (tu'm) changes the scope: it excludes non-staple foods. In bayan, the added description does not exclude anything; it only explains.
The author says: 'وذلك ليس ببيان لمعنى العلة' because it excludes.
4
Apply the test
To decide if a description is hashw or bayan, check whether it excludes anything from the cause. If it does not, it is likely bayan. In the tahari case, estimation inherently involves a majority of permissible items, so the phrase does not exclude anything new.
The author says: 'ألا ترى أنه لا يخرج من العلة شيء'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثالث : أن يقول هذه الزيادة ذكرتها للبيان، وذلك مثل أن يقول الشافعي في التحري في الأواني : إنه جنس يدخله التحري إذا كان عدد المباح أكثر ، فدخله التحري وإن لم يكن عدد المباح أكثر كالثياب . فيقول المخالف : لا تأثير لقولك إذا كان عدد المباح أكثر»، فإنك لو قلت جنس يدخله «التحري لكفى، فقولك إذا كان عدد المباح أكثر حشو لا تحتاج إليه، فهو كما لو قال مطعوم مقتات الجنس. فيجيب الشافعي قائلا : هذا بيان لما تقتضيه العلة، وذلك أني لو قلت «جنس يدخله التحري»، لكان معناه : إذا كان عدد المباح أكثر ، وبيان ما يقتضيه الكلام لا يعد حشوا، ويخالف ذكر القوت مع الطعم لأن ذلك ليس ببيان لمعنى العلة، ألا ترى أن ذكر القوت يخرج من العلة ما ليس بقوت؟ وهذا بيان لمعنى العلة، ألا ترى أنه لا يخرج من العلة شيء؟ فوزانه أن يضيف إلى الطعم ما هو بيان لمعناه بأن يقول : مطعوم للآدميين من جنس فيجوز حين كان ذلك معنى المطعوم .
And the third: That he says: I mentioned this extra part for clarification (bayan). For example, a Shafi'i says regarding tahari (estimation) in vessels: 'It is a category that is subject to estimation when the number of permissible items is more, so estimation applies to it even if the number of permissible items is not more, like garments.' The opponent says: 'Your saying "when the number of permissible items is more" has no effect, for if you said "a category subject to estimation" it would suffice. Your saying "when the number of permissible items is more" is filler you do not need, just as if he said "a food that is a staple of the genus".' The Shafi'i answers: 'This is a clarification of what the cause requires. For if I said "a category subject to estimation," its meaning would be: when the number of permissible items is more. Clarifying what the statement implies is not considered filler. It differs from mentioning 'staple' with 'food' because that is not a clarification of the meaning of the cause. Do you not see that mentioning 'staple' excludes from the cause what is not a staple? This is a clarification of the meaning of the cause. Do you not see that it does not exclude anything from the cause? Its measure is that one adds to 'food' what is a clarification of its meaning, by saying: 'a food of the genus that is consumable by humans,' then it is permissible, since that is the meaning of 'food.'
Source pages p. 440

Commentary
This passage presents the third answer to the hashw objection: the extra description is for clarification (bayan) of the cause's meaning. The example is from tahari (estimation) concerning vessels used for pure and impure substances. A Shafi'i says that a category (jins) that is subject to estimation applies when the number of permissible items is more. The opponent objects that 'when the number of permissible items is more' is filler, because the core cause is just 'a category subject to estimation.' The Shafi'i answers that this phrase clarifies the meaning: saying 'a category subject to estimation' implicitly means 'when the number of permissible items is more.' Clarifying the implication is not hashw. The author distinguishes this from an example in legal theory where adding 'staple' to 'food' actually changes the scope of the cause. Here, the clarification does not exclude anything; it merely explains what is already implied.

الحشو

C) Because the opponent does not understand the ruling.

Key Takeaway
An extra description that merely clarifies what the cause implies is not hashw; it is bayan and does not change the scope.

Objection and Answer on Redundant Wording for Emphasis
Author presents an objection that a word is superfluous and the answer that it is for emphasis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and analyze a dialectical objection to redundancy in legal reasoning and the response invoking linguistic emphasis.

Core Idea
Redundancy for emphasis is not logical hashw; Arabic rhetoric justifies it.

This passage from Jadal al-Usuliyyin presents a classic dialectical exchange: an objector claims that an added word in a legal cause is superfluous, and the respondent defends it as emphasis. The argument hinges on the nature of language in legal reasoning. Understanding this helps distinguish genuine logical redundancy from legitimate linguistic emphasis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection (mu'tarid)
Read the opponent's statement: 'قُولُكَ بِحَالٍ لَا تَأْثِيرَ لَهُ... فَهُوَ حَشْوٌ.' What exactly is he claiming about the phrase 'بحال'?
The opponent says the ruling would still hold without it, so it is hashw.
2
Identify the response (jawab)
Al-Shafi'i replies that the phrase is for emphasis (tawkid). How does he justify that emphasis is not hashw?
He cites Arabic linguistic practice and the Quranic verse with multiple emphases.
3
Analyze the evidence
Examine the Quranic verse: 'فَسَجَدَ الْمَلَائِكَةُ كُلُّهُمْ أَجْمَعُونَ.' Why does this support his case? What principle does it illustrate?
The verse repeats emphasis without being vain (laghw). This shows linguistic emphasis is valid.
4
Evaluate the dialectical outcome
Does the response successfully refute the objection? Why or why not? Consider the criteria for a valid jawab in jadal.
The response succeeds by providing a legitimate justification (emphasis) grounded in language and revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيقول المخالف : لا تأثير لقولك بحال»، فإنك لو اقتصرت على قولك : لا زكاة في أحدهما لم ينتقض بشيء، فقولك بحال» حشو في العلة لا يحتاج إليه . فيقول الشافعي : هذا ذكرته للتأكيد، وتأكيد الألفاظ لغة العرب فلا يعد حشوا، ولهذا قال الله تعالى : (فَسَجَدَ الْمَلَائِكَةُ كُلُّهُمْ أَجْمَعُونَ)، فأكد ثم أكد ولم يعد ذلك لغوا .
The opponent says: 'Your statement “in any case” has no effect. If you had limited yourself to saying “No zakat on either of them,” nothing would be refuted. So your statement “in any case” is redundant in the cause and not needed.' Al-Shafi'i says: 'I mentioned this for emphasis. Emphasis of words is the language of the Arabs, so it is not considered redundant. For this reason Allah the Exalted said: “So the angels prostrated, all of them together.” He emphasized, then emphasized, and that was not considered vain.'
Source pages p. 440

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange about whether a redundant word in a legal cause ('illa) is permissible. The opponent argues that the phrase 'in any case' (بحال) adds nothing, so the cause is overdetermined. Al-Shafi'i responds that the addition serves emphasis, which is a valid linguistic practice in Arabic. He cites the Quranic verse where Allah uses multiple emphatic words ('all of them together') without it being vain. The lesson is that not all redundancy is invalid; emphasis can strengthen the cause without being logically superfluous.

حشو
علة

D) It is an example of analogy.

Key Takeaway
Redundancy for emphasis is not logically invalid; it is a legitimate linguistic practice in Arabic legal reasoning.

Answering the Redundancy Objection by Strengthening the Cause
Author shows an objection that a phrase is redundant and the answer that it strengthens the ruling.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a redundancy objection against a causal link in usul al-fiqh and construct the response that the added element strengthens the ruling.

Core Idea
Redundancy objections fail when the added element strengthens the cause.

In dialectical reasoning, an opponent may claim that a part of your causal explanation is unnecessary (hashw). This passage from a usul al-fiqh debate shows how al-Shafi'i responds to such an objection. Instead of being redundant, the added element—that the sin necessitates the hadd penalty—demonstrates the gravity of the cause, which in turn strengthens the ruling. This lesson will teach you to defend an analogy by showing that disputed elements reinforce the causal link, a crucial skill in Islamic legal dialectics.

Guided Path
1
Identify the redundancy objection
Find the part of the analogy that the opponent claims is redundant. In the example, the opponent says 'necessitates the hadd' is hashw because the sin is already described as a major sin (kabira).
The objection targets only one addition, not the entire cause.
2
Extract the defender's justification
Al-Shafi'i argues that the link to the hadd penalty (yujib al-hadd) shows that the cause is severe (ta'akkud al-illa). This severity strengthens the ruling (ta'kid al-hukm).
The principle is that a stronger cause yields a stronger ruling.
3
Understand the logical principle
The key principle: 'Whatever strengthens the cause (ta'akkud al-illa) necessitates strengthening the ruling, hence it is not vain (laghw).'
This principle justifies the addition as meaningful.
4
Apply the principle to a new case
Consider the analogy: Khamr is prohibited because it intoxicates. An opponent says adding 'causes loss of reason' is redundant. How would you defend it? The added element shows the severe effect, strengthening the ruling of prohibition.
Use the same logic: the added element increases the cause's gravity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني : أن يقول : هذه الزيادة ذكرتها لتأكيد الحكم، وذلك مثل أن يقول الشافعي في القذف : إنه يتعلق به رد الشهادة لأنها كبيرة توجب الحد فتعلق بها رد الشهادة كالزنا . فيقول المخالف : قولك يوجب الحد حشو في العلة لا يحتاج إليه . والجواب أن يقول : تعلق الحد بها يدل على تأكدها، وتأكد العلة يوجب تأكيد الحكم، وما يوجب تأكيد الحكم لا يعد لغوّا.
The second: He says, 'I mentioned this addition to strengthen the ruling.' For example, al-Shafi'i says about slander: 'The rejection of testimony is attached to it because it is a major sin that necessitates the hadd penalty, so rejection of testimony is attached to it like zina.' The opponent says: 'Your statement “necessitates the hadd” is redundant in the cause and not needed.' The answer: 'The attachment of the hadd to it indicates its gravity, and the gravity of the cause necessitates the strengthening of the ruling, and whatever necessitates the strengthening of the ruling is not considered vain.'
Source pages p. 440

Commentary
This passage illustrates a second type of objection: the opponent claims that mentioning that the sin 'necessitates the hadd penalty' is redundant because the sin itself is already a major sin. Al-Shafi'i counters that the link to the hadd penalty shows the sin's severity (ta'akkud), and a stronger cause yields a stronger ruling. Hence the addition is not redundant but instrumental in reinforcing the analogy to zina. The key point is that any element that strengthens the causal link is permissible and not hashw.

تأكد العلة

D) It is part of the definition of slander.

Key Takeaway
An element that strengthens the causal link is not redundant; redundancy objections require careful defense by showing how the added element reinforces the ruling.

Distinguishing Bayan from Taqyid in Causal Statements
Author presents an objection that a qualifying phrase is redundant and the answer that it is for clarification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct a jadal exchange on whether an added condition is clarifying or restricting, and apply the distinction to usul al-fiqh examples.

Core Idea
An addition that clarifies the cause is valid; an addition that restricts it requires proof.

In usul al-fiqh debates, one common move is to object that a condition added to a cause is redundant. This passage from al-Shafi'i shows how to answer such an objection by distinguishing between clarifying what the cause already implies and adding a new restriction. You will learn to identify when an addition is genuine bayan and when it is an unjustified restriction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the cause and the added condition
Find the original cause al-Shafi'i proposes (e.g., 'a category that admits taharri') and the condition he adds ('when the number of permissible is more').
2
Understand the opponent's objection
The opponent argues that the condition is redundant (hashw) because the cause alone suffices. Grasp why he thinks it is unnecessary.
The opponent uses the analogy of adding 'staple' to 'food' – which changes the meaning.
3
Distinguish between bayan and taqyid
Al-Shafi'i explains that the condition is bayan (clarification) because it merely makes explicit what the cause already implies. Contrast this with adding a term that restricts the cause (taqyid), like 'staple' restricts 'food'.
Bayan does not change the extension; taqyid narrows it.
4
Apply the distinction to the example
Verify that the condition 'when the number of permissible is more' does not exclude any case that the cause would include, thus it is bayan.
5
Formulate a general rule
State: An addition to a cause is permissible if it clarifies the implicit meaning; if it adds a new restriction, it must be justified separately.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثالث : أن يقول هذه الزيادة ذكرتها للبيان، وذلك مثل أن يقول الشافعي في التحري في الأواني : إنه جنس يدخله التحري إذا كان عدد المباح أكثر ، فدخله التحري وإن لم يكن عدد المباح أكثر كالثياب . فيقول المخالف : لا تأثير لقولك إذا كان عدد المباح أكثر»، فإنك لو قلت جنس يدخله «التحري لكفى، فقولك إذا كان عدد المباح أكثر حشو لا تحتاج إليه، فهو كما لو قال مطعوم مقتات الجنس. فيجيب الشافعي قائلا : هذا بيان لما تقتضيه العلة، وذلك أني لو قلت «جنس يدخله التحري»، لكان معناه : إذا كان عدد المباح أكثر ، وبيان ما يقتضيه الكلام لا يعد حشوا، ويخالف ذكر القوت مع الطعم لأن ذلك ليس ببيان لمعنى العلة، ألا ترى أن ذكر القوت يخرج من العلة ما ليس بقوت؟ وهذا بيان لمعنى العلة، ألا ترى أنه لا يخرج من العلة شيء؟ فوزانه أن يضيف إلى الطعم ما هو بيان لمعناه بأن يقول : مطعوم للآدميين من جنس فيجوز حين كان ذلك معنى المطعوم .
The third: He says, 'I mentioned this addition for clarification.' For example, al-Shafi'i says about taharri (estimation) regarding vessels: 'It is a category that admits taharri when the number of permissible [vessels] is more; so it admits taharri even if the number of permissible is not more, like clothes.' The opponent says: 'Your statement “when the number of permissible is more” has no effect. If you had said “a category that admits taharri,” it would suffice. So your statement “when the number of permissible is more” is redundant and not needed. It is like one saying “a food that is a staple of a region” [without further qualification].' Al-Shafi'i responds: 'This is a clarification of what the cause implies. If I said “a category that admits taharri,” its meaning would be “when the number of permissible is more.” Clarifying what the speech implies is not redundant. It differs from mentioning “staple” with “food” because that is not a clarification of the meaning of the cause. Do you not see that mentioning “staple” excludes from the cause what is not a staple? This is a clarification of the meaning of the cause. Do you not see that it does not exclude anything from the cause? Its equivalent is to add to “food” what clarifies its meaning, such as saying “a food for humans of a kind,” which is permissible since that is the meaning of food.'
Source pages p. 440

Commentary
This passage deals with adding a condition to the cause for clarification. Al-Shafi'i's example: the cause for taharri is that the category admits it, and he adds 'when the number of permissible is more' to clarify the scope. The opponent objects that this condition is implicit in the cause itself. Al-Shafi'i distinguishes between two types of additions: those that clarify what the cause already implies (which are not hashw) and those that restrict the cause (like adding 'staple' to 'food'), which would be a new condition and thus impermissible without justification. The key insight: an addition that merely makes explicit what the cause already implies is valid; an addition that changes the meaning of the cause requires separate justification.

Key Terms
بيان
تحري
حشو

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is a necessary condition for taharri.
B) It clarifies the implicit meaning of the cause.
C) It was revealed in the Quran.
D) It restricts the cause to a subset.

Key Takeaway
A clarifying addition that does not change the scope of the cause is permissible as bayan, while a restricting addition that alters the cause requires separate evidence.

Objection and Answer on Redundancy in Analogical Reasoning
Objection that a phrase is redundant and the answer that it strengthens the analogy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze a jadal exchange where an opponent claims redundancy in an analogy and the respondent defends the addition as taqrib al-far' min al-asl.

Core Idea
Not every repeated phrase is hashw; it can strengthen analogical certainty.

In jadal (dialectical debate), an opponent may object that a phrase in an analogy is redundant or unnecessary. How does the proponent defend it? This lesson examines a classic exchange between al-Shafi'i and a Hanafi over the purification of dog skin by tanning. The Hanafi says mentioning the sheep's skin is hashw; al-Shafi'i replies that it brings the branch closer to the root, increasing the strength of the analogy (taqrib al-far' min al-asl). Understanding this defense is essential for evaluating objections in usul al-fiqh debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mudda'i's position and the analogy
Al-Shafi'i argues that dog skin is not purified by tanning, using an analogy: after tanning, sheep skin becomes pure; dog skin after tanning should remain impure, analogously to the state of life (where dog skin is impure and sheep skin is pure).
The analogy has two parallels: life and post-tanning.
2
Analyze the mu'tarid's objection (redundancy)
The Hanafi opponent says mentioning 'the state in which the skin of a sheep is judged pure' is unnecessary. He argues that referring only to the state where dog skin is judged impure would suffice, and the extra phrase is hashw (redundant).
This objection targets the form of the analogy, not its validity.
3
Evaluate the jawab: defense of the addition
Al-Shafi'i responds that the addition serves taqrib al-far' min al-asl: it explicitly shows the similarity between post-tanning and the state of life. Both states are alike in that they require purity (for sheep skin), so highlighting this similarity strengthens the analogy.
The defense is that any addition that increases certainty (zann) is not hashw.
4
Apply the rule to a similar case
Imagine a jurist says: 'Wine is forbidden because it is intoxicating. Nabidh is also intoxicating, so it is forbidden.' An opponent says mentioning 'intoxicating' twice is redundant. Using taqrib al-far' min al-asl, the jurist would reply that the repetition emphasizes the shared 'illa (cause) and strengthens the analogy.
This shows the principle applies beyond fiqh al-hadith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجواب رابع : أن يقول : هذه الزيادة لتقريب الفرع من الأصل، وذلك مثل أن يقول الشافعي في جلد الكلب : إنه لا يطهر بالدباغ، لأن ما بعد الدباغ حالة يحكم فيها بطهارة جلد الشاة فوجب أن يحكم فيها بنجاسة جلد الكلب، قياسًا على حالة الحياة . فيقول الحنفي : لا تأثير لقولكم يحكم فيها بطهارة جلد الشاة»، فإنك لو قلت حالة يحكم فيها بنجاسة جلد «الكلب لكفى والزيادة عليه حشو. فيقول الشافعي مجيبا : هذه الزيادة ذكرتها لتقريب الفرع من الأصل، وإن ما بعد الدباغ يجري مجرى حالة الحياة، بدليل أنهما يستويان في إيجاب الطهارة، فإذا لم تؤثر الحياة في طهارة جلد الكلب دل على أن الدباغ مثله، وتقريب الفرع الأصل يزيد في الظن، فلا يعد حشوا.
A fourth answer: He says, 'This addition is to bring the branch closer to the root.' For example, al-Shafi'i says about the skin of a dog: 'It is not purified by tanning, because the state after tanning is a state in which the skin of a sheep is judged pure, so it must be judged that the skin of a dog is impure, by analogy to the state of life.' The Hanafi says: 'Your statement “in which the skin of a sheep is judged pure” has no effect. If you had said “a state in which the skin of a dog is judged impure,” it would suffice, and the addition is redundant.' Al-Shafi'i answers: 'I mentioned this addition to bring the branch closer to the root. The state after tanning is analogous to the state of life, on the evidence that both necessitate purity in some cases. Since life did not affect the purity of the dog's skin, it indicates that tanning is similar. Bringing the branch closer to the root increases the strength of the opinion, so it is not considered redundant.'
Source pages p. 440

Commentary
This passage addresses objections to analogical reasoning in fiqh. Al-Shafi'i uses an analogy: the dog's skin after tanning is like a sheep's skin after tanning (which becomes pure), but since the dog's skin is not pure during life, it remains impure after tanning. The Hanafi opponent argues that mentioning the sheep's skin purification is unnecessary because the cause (the state after tanning) is sufficient. Al-Shafi'i counters that the addition highlights the similarity between the branch (dog skin after tanning) and the root (life state), which strengthens the analogy. The principle: any addition that brings the branch closer to the root (taqrib al-far' min al-asl) and increases the certainty (zann) of the analogy is valid.

Key Terms
تقريب الفرع من الأصل
دباغ
حشو

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is a condition for the ruling.
B) It emphasizes that tanning resembles life, strengthening the analogy.
C) It is required by the Quran.
D) It was added by mistake.

Key Takeaway
An addition in an analogy that brings the branch closer to the root (taqrib al-far' min al-asl) and increases certainty is not redundant but a valid strengthening of the argument.

Mu‘arada: Contesting the Cause with an Independent Cause
Definition of mu‘arada and its first type: contesting with an independent cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish mu‘arada bi-‘illa mubtada’a from other types of objections in jadal.

Core Idea
Mu‘arada is not denial; it is presenting an alternative cause to block the opponent's inference.

In this lesson, we examine mu‘arada, a key objection technique in Islamic dialectics. Unlike outright rejection, mu‘arada offers a positive counter-cause. We will parse its definition and analyze the first type: contesting with an independent cause.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic root of mu‘arada
The author explains that mu‘arada comes from 'arada, meaning to confront or stop. This gives the sense of blocking the opponent's proof.
2
Distinguish the two domains of mu‘arada
Mu‘arada can attack the wording (al-nutq) or the cause (al-‘illa). The latter is our focus here.
3
Recognise the first subtype: mu‘arada bi-‘illa mubtada’a
The objector does not deny the original cause but proposes an independent alternative that yields a different ruling.
4
Analyse the example: Maliki vs Shafi'i on salam
The Maliki argues that salam must be deferred because it has specific characteristics. The Shafi'i contests by claiming that salam is a type of sale, and not all sales require deferment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المطلب الثالث : الاعتراض على العلة بالمعارضة . المعارضة : مفاعلة، من عرض له يعرض إذا أوقف بين يديه أو عارضه في طريقه ليمنعه النفوذ فيه، فكان المعترض يقف بين يدي المستدل أو يوقف حجته بين يدي دليله ليمنعه من النفوذ في إثبات الدعوى. قد تكون بالنطق، وقد تكون بالعلة. فأما المعارضة بالنطق، فقد تقدم الكلام فيها. وأما المعارضة بالعلة فعلى ضربين: أ - المعارضة بعلة مبتدأة : وذلك مثل أن يستدل مالكي على أن السلم لا يجوز أن يكون حالا بأن ما اختص بالسلم وجب أن يكون اختصاصه به على وجه التصحيح له، قياسًا على كون المسلم فيه في الذمة. فيعارضه الشافعي بأن هذا هو أحد نوعي البيع، فلم يكن من شرطه الأجل كبيع الأعيان . والطريق في الجواب عنه أمران :
Third topic: Objection to the cause by mu‘arada (contestation). Mu‘arada is a verbal noun from 'he presented' or 'he confronted', meaning to stop [the argument] before it or to confront it on its path to prevent its progress. So the objector stands before the arguer or stops his proof before his evidence to prevent it from establishing the claim. It may be by [objecting to] the wording or by [objecting to] the cause. As for mu‘arada by wording, its discussion has already passed. As for mu‘arada by cause, it is of two types: (a) Mu‘arada with an independent cause. For example, a Maliki argues that salam (advance sale) cannot be spot (haalan) because what is specific to salam must be specific to it for the sake of validating it, by analogy to the fact that the commodity (muslam fihi) is in the dhimmah (deferment). The Shafi'i contests him by [saying] that this is one of the two types of sale, so it does not require a time period (ajal) like the sale of physical goods (a‘yan). The way to answer it is by two things:
Source pages p. 441

Commentary
This passage defines mu‘arada, a specific type of objection in jadal where the opponent presents an alternative cause that would yield a different ruling, thereby contesting the original cause. The author first gives the linguistic meaning of mu‘arada as confronting or stopping something. Then he distinguishes two types: mu‘arada by wording (objecting to the text of the proof) and mu‘arada by cause. The focus here is on the latter, which splits into two: contesting by an independent cause (mu‘arada bi-‘illa mubtada’a) and presumably another type (the text cuts off). The example given: a Maliki argues that salam cannot be spot because it has specific characteristics that require deferment, like the commodity being in the dhimmah. The Shafi'i contests by saying that salam is a type of sale, and not all sales require deferment (e.g., sale of physical goods on the spot). This is an example of proposing a different cause (the type of sale) that does not entail the ruling (deferment). The answer to such mu‘arada involves two methods, which the text does not complete.

علة مبتدأة

C) That salam requires a time period.

Key Takeaway
Mu‘arada bi-‘illa mubtada’a is a constructive objection where the contestant offers a new cause to undermine the original inference, rather than just denying the cause.

Objection by Distinction: Farq bi-Illat al-Hukm
Defines farq bi-illat al-hukm with examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a farq bi-illat al-hukm objection in a qiyas argument and determine the correct respondent strategy based on whether the cause is agreed or disputed.

Core Idea
In jadal, not every distinction topples an analogy; the respondent must know whether to attack the original cause or the branch cause.

In this passage, the author defines a specific type of objection called 'al-farq bi-illat al-hukm' – distinguishing by the cause of the ruling. You will learn the conditions for a valid distinction and how the respondent should reply depending on whether the cause is agreed upon or disputed. Real fiqh examples from divorce and riba illustrate the dialectical moves.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of objection
The passage defines 'al-mu'arada bi-illat al-asl' (objection by the cause of the original), also called 'al-farq'. The objector points to a meaning in the original case and its opposite in the branch to break the analogy.
This is a common move in jadal to challenge qiyas.
2
Extract the condition for a valid farq
The objector must mention a meaning (ma'na) that is attached to the ruling in the original and is opposite in the branch. The meaning may be agreed upon or disputed.
The passage divides farq bi-illat al-hukm into two subtypes based on this.
3
Determine respondent strategy based on cause status
If the cause is agreed upon, the respondent should not attack the original cause but should attack the branch cause. If the cause is disputed, the respondent should attack the original cause.
This is a key procedural rule in jadal.
4
Analyze the first example: divorce before marriage
The Shafi'i analogizes divorce before marriage to an insane person. The Hanafi objects that the insane person lacks legal responsibility, while the husband is responsible. The respondent gives two rebuttals: (1) counter the cause by showing equivalence between non-responsibility and non-ownership, (2) show equality in direct divorce.
This illustrates the 'agreed cause' subtype.
5
Analyze the second example: riba analogy
The Shafi'i analogizes fruit to wheat in riba. The Hanafi objects that wheat is measurable, fruit is not. The respondent attacks the original cause, arguing that measure cannot be the cause because it is a means to avoid riba, not to prohibit it.
This illustrates the 'disputed cause' subtype.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ب - المعارضة بعلة الأصل : وتسمى «الفرق»، وهو أن يذكر المعترض ما يوجب الفرق بين الفرع والأصل، وذلك أن يذكر معنى في الأصل ويعكسه في الفرع، وهو قد يكون فرقا بعلة الحكم، أو فرقًا بدلالة الحكم. ب - 1 - الفرق بعلة الحكم: وهو أن يكون المعنى يتعلق الحكم به في الشرع في الأصل، وبعكسه في الفرع، وهذا لا يخلو إما أن يكون معنى متفقا عليه أو مختلفا فيه . فإن كان معنى متفقا عليه، فالواجب ألا يشتغل المستدل بالقدح في علة الأصل، بل يشتغل بالقدح في علة الفرع، وذلك مثل أن يقول شافعي في الطلاق قبل النكاح : إنه لا يملك المباشرة، فلم تنعقد له صفة الطلاق قياسًا على المجنون . فيقول الحنفي معترضا: المعنى في الأصل أنه غير مكلف، وهذا مكلف أضاف الطلاق إلى ملكه . والجواب عليه من وجهين : أحدهما : أن يقابل علة الأصل بمثلها في التأثير في الحكم، وذلك أن يقول : إن كان في الأصل لم يصح منه لأنه غير مكلف فهذا غير مالك، ولا فرق في الأصول بين غير المكلف وبين غير المالك والدليل عليه أن في البيع من لا يملك كمن ليس بمكلف في أنه لا ينفذ بيعه . والثاني : أن يبطل الفرق بما يستوي فيه الفرع والأصل، فيبين أن هذا جعل في الحكم كالمجنون ألا ترى أنهما في الطلاق المباشر يستويان، فدل على أنهما سيان؟ وإن كانت علة الأصل معنى مختلفًا فيه، فالذي ينبغي على المستدل أن يصرف عنايته إليه أن يتكلم على علة الأصل، وذلك مثل أن يقيس الشافعي في الربا في الفاكهة على البر. فيقول الحنفي : المعنى في البر أنه مكيل جنس وهذا ليس بمكيل ولا موزون . فالجواب أن يقول المستدل : لا يجوز أن يكون الكيل علة، لأن الكيل يتخلص به من الربا، فلا يجوز أن يجعل علة تقتضي تحريم الربا، ولأن الكيل لا يوجد الحكم بوجوده ولا يعدم بعدمه، ولأن التعليل بالكيل يعود على أصله بالإبطال، وما أشبه ذلك
B - Objection by the cause of the original case: It is called 'the distinction' (al-farq), and it is that the objector mentions what necessitates a distinction between the branch and the original case, namely that he mentions a meaning in the original and its opposite in the branch. It may be a distinction by the cause of the ruling, or a distinction by the indication of the ruling. B-1 - The distinction by the cause of the ruling: It is that the meaning is attached to the ruling in the Law in the original case, and its opposite is in the branch. This is either a meaning agreed upon or disputed. If it is an agreed upon meaning, then the respondent must not engage in criticizing the cause of the original, but rather engage in criticizing the cause of the branch. An example is a Shafi'i saying concerning divorce before marriage: 'He does not have the right of direct contact, so the attribute of divorce is not established for him, by analogy to the insane person.' Then the Hanafi objects: 'The meaning in the original is that he is not legally responsible (ghayr mukallaf), while this one is legally responsible and has added divorce to his property.' The response to it is from two aspects: First: that he counter the cause of the original with its like in affecting the ruling, such that he says: 'If in the original it was not valid from him because he is not legally responsible, then this one does not possess (property), and there is no difference in the principles between one not legally responsible and one not possessing. The evidence for it is that in sale, one who does not possess is like one not legally responsible in that his sale is not executed.' Second: that he invalidate the distinction by that in which the branch and the original are equal, showing that this one is made in the ruling like the insane person. Do you not see that they are equal in direct divorce? So it indicates that they are equal.' And if the cause of the original is a disputed meaning, then what is incumbent upon the respondent is to direct his attention to it, to speak about the cause of the original. An example is when the Shafi'i analogizes fruit in riba to wheat. The Hanafi says: 'The meaning in wheat is that it is a measurable genus, and this is not measurable nor weighable.' The response is that the respondent says: 'It is not permissible for measure to be the cause, because measure is a means of avoiding riba, so it cannot be made a cause that necessitates the prohibition of riba; and because the ruling does not exist with the existence of measure nor cease with its non-existence; and because reasoning by measure reverts to invalidating its own principle (asl), and so forth.'
Source pages p. 442

Commentary
This passage defines and illustrates 'al-mu'arada bi-illat al-asl' (objection by the cause of the original case), also called 'al-farq' (distinction). The author explains that a distinguisher mentions a meaning in the original case and its opposite in the branch to break the analogy. He then divides farq into two types: farq bi-illat al-hukm (distinction by the cause of the ruling) and farq bi-dalalat al-hukm (distinction by the indication of the ruling). The first subtype, farq bi-illat al-hukm, occurs when the meaning related to the ruling in the original is opposite in the branch. This meaning may be agreed upon or disputed. If the meaning is agreed upon, the respondent should not attack the cause in the original but rather attack the cause in the branch. The author provides a fiqh example: a Shafi'i arguing that divorce before marriage is invalid by analogy to an insane person (since both lack direct contact). The Hanafi objector distinguishes: the insane person is not legally responsible (mukallaf), while the husband is responsible and has added divorce to his property. Two responses are given: first, counter the cause by showing that lack of ownership is equivalent to lack of legal responsibility (e.g., in sale, a non-owner's sale is void like a non-responsible person's sale). Second, show that the branch and original are equal in some aspect (both are equal in direct divorce). If the cause is disputed, the respondent should attack the cause in the original. Another fiqh example: Shafi'i analogizing fruit to wheat in riba. The Hanafi objects that wheat is a measurable genus, fruit is not. The respondent rebuts: measure cannot be the cause because it is a means to avoid riba, not to prohibit it; also the ruling does not follow the presence or absence of measure; and reasoning by measure would invalidate its own principle (since the Prophetic prohibition of wheat would be undermined).

علة الأصل
علة الفرع

Checkpoint
In the example of divorce before marriage (talaq qabl al-nikah), what is the Hanafi objector's distinction?
A) The insane person is not legally responsible, while the husband is responsible.
B) The husband does not own the wife, while the insane person does.
C) The analogy is invalid because the original is about sale.
D) The cause of the ruling is the same in both cases.

Key Takeaway
The farq bi-illat al-hukm objection forces the respondent to strategically choose between attacking the original cause or the branch cause based on whether the cause is agreed upon.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider a qiyas: 'Dry dates (tamr) are prohibited in riba by analogy to wheat, because both are staple foods.' An objector says: 'Wheat is measurable (makil), while dry dates are not.' Is the cause agreed or disputed? What should the respondent do? Write a brief response (jawab).
?
Formulate a farq bi-illat al-hukm objection to the following analogy: 'Slaughtering an animal by a woman is valid by analogy to slaughtering by a man, because both are humans.' Provide the distinction and then write a respondent's reply.

Distinction by Indication of the Ruling: First Type
Presents the first of four types of farq bi-dalalat al-hukm with a fiqh example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and construct the first type of farq bi-dalalat al-hukm (distinction by indication of the ruling) in a jadal exchange using a fiqh example.

Core Idea
A ruling specific to the branch reveals a difference in cause, breaking a false analogy.

In jadal (dialectic), one way to defeat an analogy is to show that the branch has a ruling that the original does not, indicating a difference in effective cause. This lesson unpacks the first type of 'distinction by indication of the ruling' using the classical fiqh debate on overlapping waiting periods (tadakhul al-idda).

Guided Path
1
Identify the analogy being attacked
Find the original case (asl) and the branch (far') that the opponent claims are similar. Here, the opponent analogizes the waiting period (idda) to a debt term, arguing that both can expire for two persons.
The original is the term of a debt (ajal al-dayn); the branch is the waiting period after divorce.
2
Locate the distinguishing ruling (hukm)
The objector (Shafi'i) points out a ruling that applies to the branch but not the original: marriage cannot involve two husbands simultaneously, while a debt can involve two debtors. This ruling is specific to the branch.
The ruling is that marriage is not valid for two persons, so the waiting period cannot serve two husbands.
3
Analyze the objector's reasoning
The objector argues that because the branch has this unique ruling, the analogy fails. This is a farq bi-dalalat al-hukm: the distinction is based on a ruling (hukm) that applies specifically to the branch, indicating that the effective cause (illa) differs.
The objector does not deny that the waiting period is a term; rather, he shows a contextual difference.
4
Examine the respondent's counter-strategies
The respondent gives two replies: (1) a counterexample showing that the ruling can apply (a man's two waiting periods from the same wife can overlap), and (2) the stronger response: explaining the underlying rationale (hikma) that marriage is for lineage purity, while the waiting period is for womb clearance, so overlapping is allowed.
The second reply is preferred because it engages with the purpose of the rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
2 - الفرق بدلالة الحكم وهو على أربعة أضرب : الأول : الفرق بين الفرع والأصل بحكم يختص بالفرع : وذلك مثل أن يقول : إن العدتين تتداخلان، لأنه أجل، فجاز أن ينقضي بمضي عدة واحدة في حق اثنين، أصله أجل الدين. فيقول شافعي : المعنى في أجل الدين أن سبب الأجل يجوز إن وجد لاثنين، فجاز أن يتعلق بمضي الأجل في حق اثنين، وليس كذلك فيما عاد إلى مسألتنا، فإن النكاح لا يقع فيه العقد لاثنين فلا يتعلق بمضي العدة فيه حق اثنين. والجواب عنه من وجهين أحدهما: أن يكسر ذلك إن ظفر به، مثل أن يقول : لا يمتنع أن يجوز تداخل السبب الموجب لأجل ، ذلك فإنه يتداخل الأجل، كالرجل في خاصة نفسه ومع لا يجوز أن يتزوج زوجته مع ذلك، فإن عدتيها منه تتداخلان. والثاني : وهو الذي عليه المعول : أن يبين المعنى الذي لأجله لم يصح النكاح لاثنين وجاز تداخل العدة ، منهما فيقول : النكاح شرع لتخليص الأنساب، والسفاح محرم لخلطها، فلذلك لم يجز أن يطرأ نكاح على نكاح ؛ وأما العدة مدة ضربت لبراءة الرحم، والبراءة تحصل من الزوجين جميعا بمضي مدة فإنما هي
2 - The distinction by the indication of the ruling, and it is of four types: First: The distinction between the branch and the original by a ruling specific to the branch. An example is when one says: 'The two waiting periods (iddatan) overlap (tadakhulan), because it is a term (ajal), so it is permissible that it expires by the passage of one waiting period on behalf of two persons, its original case being the term of a debt.' The Shafi'i says: 'The meaning in the term of a debt is that the cause of the term is permissible if it exists for two persons, so it is permissible that the expiry of the term concerns two persons; but this is not the case in our issue, because marriage does not occur by a contract for two persons, so the expiry of the waiting period does not entail the right of two persons.' The response to it is from two aspects: First: that he break (counter) it if he finds a counterexample, such as saying: 'It is not impossible that the overlapping of the cause necessitating a term is permissible, for that term overlaps, like a man in his own special case, and while it is not permissible for him to marry his wife along with that, his two waiting periods from her overlap.' Second: which is the relied upon: that he explain the reason why marriage is not valid for two persons and the overlapping of the waiting period is allowed, saying: 'Marriage was legislated to purify lineages, and fornication is prohibited because it mixes them; therefore it is not permissible that a marriage supervenes upon another marriage. As for the waiting period, it is a period set for the clearing of the womb, and clearing occurs from both husbands together with the passage of the period, so it is...'
Source pages p. 443

Commentary
This passage presents the first of four types of 'al-farq bi-dalalat al-hukm' (distinction by the indication of the ruling). The objector distinguishes between the branch and the original by a ruling that is specific to the branch, not shared by the original. The example concerns whether two waiting periods (iddat) can overlap (tadakhul al-iddatain). The respondent argues that they do overlap because the waiting period is a term (ajal) that can expire on behalf of two persons, analogizing to the term of a debt which can apply to two debtors. The Shafi'i objector distinguishes: in debt, the cause of the term can exist for two persons (two debtors can share a term), whereas in marriage, the marriage contract cannot be for two persons (a woman cannot be simultaneously married to two men), so the expiry of the waiting period cannot serve two husbands. Two responses are given. First, counterexample: a man who divorces his wife and then remarries her, then divorces again—the two waiting periods from the same man can overlap, showing that the principle can accommodate overlapping. Second (the stronger response): explain the underlying rationale. Marriage is for clarifying lineage, so two simultaneous marriages would confuse it. The waiting period is for clearing the womb, which can be achieved from both husbands by the mere passage of time, so overlapping serves its purpose.

تداخل العدة

C) The waiting period clears the womb, which cannot be for two persons.
D) The respondent's analogy to debt is invalid because debt is not a term.

Key Takeaway
The first type of farq bi-dalalat al-hukm highlights a ruling that applies exclusively to the branch, revealing a difference in effective cause and breaking the analogical reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The original is gold, the branch is crops. Identify a ruling specific to the branch that breaks the analogy (hint: consider the exemption for expenses in farming).
?

Use the first type of farq bi-dalalat al-hukm to object, citing a ruling specific to food (e.g., prohibition of hoarding).

Responding to a Disputed 'Illa of the Original
Author explains how to respond when the cause of the original case is disputed, with an example on riba.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a response when the 'illa of the original case is disputed in a jadal exchange, using the three counter-arguments from this passage.

Core Idea
Defend the analogy by attacking the opponent's proposed 'illa, not by proving the branch.

In jadal (dialectical debate), when you propose a qiyas (analogy) and the opponent rejects the 'illa (effective cause) of the original case, you cannot simply retreat to the branch. The correct method is to redirect the discussion to the 'illa of the original and dismantle the opponent's alternative 'illa. This lesson walks through the technique with a classic example from riba debates.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the situation
Identify that the opponent is not denying the analogy itself but is proposing a different 'illa for the original case. In the example, the Hanafi says the 'illa of barley is that it is a measured genus (makil jins), not the general category of food.
The opponent's move is called 'ibdal or ta'yin of the 'illa.
2
Identify the opponent's objection
Extract the opponent's proposed 'illa and note why it allegedly excludes the branch. Here, the Hanafi claims fruits are neither measured nor weighed, so they fall outside the 'illa.
3
Recall the three counter-arguments
The passage provides three reasons against the opponent's 'illa: (1) measuring is a means to avoid riba, so it cannot be a cause that prohibits; (2) the ruling does not co-vary with measuring; (3) if measuring were the 'illa, it would invalidate the original ruling itself.
These are standard jadal arguments: munafat (conflict), 'adam al-tard wa al-'aks, and naqd.
4
Apply the method to another example
Take a different analogy from fiqh (e.g., qiyas of narcotics on wine) and practice refuting an alternative 'illa such as 'it causes intoxication' using similar counter-arguments.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن كانت علة الأصل معنى مختلفًا فيه، فالذي ينبغي على المستدل أن يصرف عنايته إليه أن يتكلم على علة الأصل، وذلك مثل أن يقيس الشافعي في الربا في الفاكهة على البر. فيقول الحنفي : المعنى في البر أنه مكيل جنس وهذا ليس بمكيل ولا موزون . فالجواب أن يقول المستدل : لا يجوز أن يكون الكيل علة، لأن الكيل يتخلص به من الربا، فلا يجوز أن يجعل علة تقتضي تحريم الربا، ولأن الكيل لا يوجد الحكم بوجوده ولا يعدم بعدمه، ولأن التعليل بالكيل يعود على أصله بالإبطال، وما أشبه ذلك
And if the 'illa of the original case is a disputed meaning, then what the arguer should do is direct his attention to it: to discuss the 'illa of the original. An example is when al-Shafi'i makes an analogy regarding riba in fruits to barley. The Hanafi says: the meaning in barley is that it is a measured genus, and this (fruit) is neither measured nor weighed. The response is that the arguer should say: It is not permissible for measuring to be the 'illa, because by measuring one extricates oneself from riba, so it cannot be made a cause that necessitates the prohibition of riba. Also, because the ruling does not exist with the existence of measuring nor cease with its absence. Also, because using measuring as the 'illa would invalidate the original case. And similar arguments.
Source pages p. 443

Commentary
This passage presents a method in jadal: when the opponent attacks an analogy by denying the 'illa of the original (the asl), the proponent must defend the 'illa of the original. The example: al-Shafi'i analogized fruits to barley in riba. The Hanafi objected that the 'illa in barley is that it is a measured foodstuff, which fruits are not. The response lists three reasons why measuring cannot be the 'illa: (1) measuring is a means to avoid riba, so it cannot be a cause that incurs prohibition; (2) the ruling does not follow measuring causally (absence/presence); (3) if measuring were the cause, it would undermine the original ruling itself. This illustrates the technique of 'kalam 'ala 'illat al-asl' (discussing the cause of the original).

The First Type of Farq: Ruling Specific to the Branch
Author lists the first type of farq with two examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the first type of farq in a jadal exchange and apply the 'break' (kasar) and explanation (tabyin) responses to a given analogy.

Core Idea
The first type of farq neutralizes an analogy by showing a ruling unique to the branch.

In jadal, when an opponent draws an analogy (tamthil), the responder can invalidate it by pointing out a ruling that applies only to the branch, not the original. This lesson unpacks the first of four types of farq, using two classical usuli examples on 'iddah and sujud al-tilawah. You will learn how to recognize this move and how to respond effectively.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original and branch
In each example, find the asl (original) and far' (branch). For the 'iddah example, the original is the term of debt (ajal al-dayn) and the branch is the 'iddah of a woman. For sujud al-tilawah, the original is sujud al-salb (prayer prostration) and the branch is sujud al-tilawah.
The opponent's analogy assumes the two have the same effective cause ('illah).
2
Locate the ruling specific to the branch
The Shafi'i responder points out a ruling (hukm) that applies to the branch but not the original. In the 'iddah example: 'marriage does not occur for two' – the debt term can apply to two persons, but 'iddah cannot because marriage is for one. In the sujud al-tilawah example: 'it can be performed on a riding animal without excuse' – unlike prostration in prayer.
This ruling is unique to the branch, breaking the analogy.
3
Distinguish the two response methods
The author gives two ways to answer: (1) 'Break' (kasar) by finding a counterexample where the claimed difference does not hold. For 'iddah, the Shafi'i says a man divorcing his wife twice in one 'iddah is a case where two 'iddahs overlap. (2) Explanation (tabyin) of the underlying meanings: marriage is for lineage, so it cannot overlap; 'iddah is for clearing the womb, which can be achieved from both husbands by one period.
The second method is considered the more reliable (al-mu'awwal).
4
Apply to the sujud al-tilawah example
The Shafi'i argues that the difference in ruling (permissibility on a mount) stems from the cause: sujud al-tilawah's reason (recitation) occurs on the mount, while the core prayer's reason (the prayer itself) does not. This explains the difference without breaking the analogy.
The response discusses the 'illah of both original and branch.
5
Evaluate the strength of the farq
Consider whether the specific ruling truly distinguishes the branch such that the analogy collapses. In both examples, the responder shows that the branch has a property not shared by the original, which undermines the analogical transfer of the ruling.
A strong farq must be genuine and not arbitrary.
