الفرق بدلالة الحكم وهو على أربعة أضرب : الأول : الفرق بين الفرع والأصل بحكم يختص بالفرع : وذلك مثل أن يقول : إن العدتين تتداخلان، لأنه أجل، فجاز أن ينقضي بمضي عدة واحدة في حق اثنين، أصله أجل الدين. فيقول شافعي : المعنى في أجل الدين أن سبب الأجل يجوز إن وجد لاثنين، فجاز أن يتعلق بمضي الأجل في حق اثنين، وليس كذلك فيما عاد إلى مسألتنا، فإن النكاح لا يقع فيه العقد لاثنين فلا يتعلق بمضي العدة فيه حق اثنين. والجواب عنه من وجهين أحدهما: أن يكسر ذلك إن ظفر به، مثل أن يقول : لا يمتنع أن يجوز تداخل السبب الموجب لأجل ذلك فإنه يتداخل الأجل، كالرجل في خاصة نفسه مع لا يجوز أن يتزوج زوجته مع ذلك، فإن عدتيها منه تتداخلان. والثاني : وهو الذي عليه المعول : أن يبين المعنى الذي لأجله لم يصح النكاح لاثنين وجاز تداخل العدة منهما فيقول : النكاح شرع لتخليص الأنساب، والسفاح محرم لخلطها، فلذلك لم يجز أن يطرأ نكاح على نكاح ; وأما العدة مدة ضربت لبراءة الرحم، والبراءة تحصل من الزوجين جميعا بمضي مدة واحدة كأجل الدين الذي ضرب لحلول الدين وجواز المطالبة به، وذلك يحصل بمضي مدة واحدة، فاستويا. ومثاله: أيضًا أن يقول حنفي في سجود التلاوة : إنه سجود يجوز فعله في الصلاة، فكان واجبًا كسجود صلاة الصلب فيقول الشافعي : المعنى في سجود صلاة الصلب أنه لا يجوز فعله على الراحلة من غير عذر وليس كذلك سجود التلاوة فإنه يجوز فعله على الراحلة من غير عذر، فهو كسجود النفل . والجواب عنه أن يتكلم على علة الأصل وعلة الفرع بكل ما يتكلم على التعليل، والذي يختص به أن علة جواز فعله على الراحلة، وهو أن يقول : إنما جاز فعله على الراحلة لأنه وجد سببه وهو على الراحلة، وسجود الصلب لم يوجد سببه على الراحلة، فلذلك لم يجز فعله على الراحلة
Difference by indication of the ruling, and it is of four types. First: the difference between the branch and the original by a ruling specific to the branch. Example: saying that two 'iddahs overlap because it is a term, so it is permissible that they be fulfilled by the passing of one 'iddah for two persons; its original is the term of debt. A Shafi'i says: the meaning in the term of debt is that the cause of the term can exist for two, so it can be attached to the passing of the term for two persons; it is not so in our issue, because the marriage contract does not occur for two, so the right of two persons does not attach to the passing of the 'iddah. The answer to this is from two aspects: one is to break it if he finds a break, such as saying: it is not impossible that the cause necessitating the term may overlap—it overlaps the term, like a man with his own self; it is not permissible for him to marry his wife during that, yet her two 'iddahs from him overlap. The second, which is relied upon, is to explain the meaning for which marriage is not valid for two and overlapping of 'iddah is permissible, saying: marriage was legislated to purify lineages, and illicit sex is forbidden because it mixes them; therefore it is not permissible for a marriage to follow a marriage. As for the 'iddah, it is a period set for clearing the womb, and clearance is achieved from both husbands by the passing of one period, like the term of debt set for the maturity of the debt and the permissibility of claiming it; that is achieved by the passing of one period, so they are equal. Another example: a Hanafi says regarding prostration of recitation: it is a prostration permissible to do in prayer, so it is obligatory like the prostration of the core prayer. The Shafi'i says: the meaning in the prostration of the core prayer is that it is not permissible to do it on a riding animal without an excuse; the prostration of recitation is not like that, because it is permissible to do it on a riding animal without an excuse, so it is like the supererogatory prostration. The answer to this is to discuss the cause of the original and the cause of the branch with all that is discussed about causation. What is specific to it is that the cause of its permissibility on the riding animal—and he says: it was only permissible on the riding animal because its reason occurred while he was on the riding animal, and the prostration of the core prayer did not have its reason occur on the riding animal, so it is not permissible to do it on the riding animal.
Source pages p. 443

Commentary
This passage presents the first type of farq (distinction) in jadal, which is 'difference by a ruling specific to the branch'. The first example concerns overlapping of 'iddahs (waiting periods). The opponent argues that two 'iddahs can overlap because they are a term, like a debt term. The Shafi'i objects that the cause of the debt term allows it to be for two persons, but marriage does not. The answer uses two methods: first, 'breaking' by finding a counterexample where overlapping does occur (e.g., a man who divorces his wife twice during one 'iddah, her two 'iddahs overlap). Second, explaining the underlying meaning: marriage is for lineage, so it cannot overlap, but 'iddah is for clearing the womb, which can be achieved from two husbands in one period. The second example concerns sujud al-tilawah (prostration of recitation) analogized to sujud al-salb (core prayer prostration). The Shafi'i objects that the core prayer prostration cannot be done on a riding animal without excuse, while sujud al-tilawah can. The response is to discuss the cause of the original and branch, specifying that the cause for permissibility on the mount is that the reason (the recitation) occurred on the mount, while the reason for the core prayer (the prayer itself) did not.

Key Terms
الفرق بدلالة الحكم
تكسر
العدة
سجود التلاوة

C) 'Iddah always lasts three months.
D) Overlapping of 'iddahs is never allowed.

Key Takeaway
The first type of farq invalidates an analogy by showing a ruling that applies only to the branch, not the original.

The Second Type of Farq: Intention in Divorce by Writing vs. Speech
Author illustrates the second type of farq with a Maliki-Shafi'i debate on divorce by writing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the difference in ruling (farq) between spoken and written divorce regarding intention, and evaluate the dialectical responses used to break an analogy.

Core Idea
The difference in ruling itself – intention – can break an analogy even when the base ruling is undisputed.

In dialectical debate (jadal), one method to refute an analogy is to show a difference in the ruling itself in a non-disputed matter. Here, the Maliki argues that divorce by writing is like speech; the Shafi'i objects by pointing out that speech works without intention but writing does not. This lesson walks through the analogical argument, the objection, and the respondent's two defense strategies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Maliki's analogical argument
The Maliki claims that divorce by writing is valid because writing conveys intent, analogizing it to spoken divorce. Find the elements of his qiyas: the original case (speech), the ruling (valid divorce), the effective cause (conveying intent), and the new case (writing).
The analogy is based on the common property of conveying intent.
2
Find the Shafi'i's objection: the undisputed difference
The Shafi'i points out that spoken divorce is effective even if the speaker denies intent, but written divorce requires intent. This is a clear difference in the ruling (tatbiq) in a matter that is not disputed (the role of intention in speech vs. writing).
The difference is in the ruling itself, not in the underlying rationale.
3
Analyze the first response: 'breaking' the analogy
The Maliki respondent tries to 'break' (kasar) the objection by finding a counterexample: even in speech, some expressions (kinayat) require intention, so the claimed difference is not universal. Thus the analogy can be defended by narrowing the scope.
This response does not deny the difference but shows it is not absolute.
4
Analyze the second response: explaining the underlying meaning
Alternatively, the respondent can explain why speech and writing differ in terms of intention: speech is inherently expressive, while writing is symbolic and requires additional intent. This preserves the analogy by clarifying the cause.
This response accepts the difference but provides a rational explanation for it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : الفرق بنفس الحكم في غير مواضع الخلاف : مثل أن يقول مالكي : إن الكتاب يقع به الطلاق، لأنه حروف تنبئ عن المراد، فجاز أن يقع به الطلاق كالنطق . فيقول شافعي معترضا: المعنى في النطق أنه يكون طلاقا وإن قال «لم أرد به الطلاق»، وليس كذلك في ما عاد إلى مسألتنا فإنه إن قال لم أرد به الطلاق لم يكن طلاقا، وكذلك إذا قال أردت به الطلاق»، كقيامه وقعوده. والجواب عن مثل هذا ما تقدم، وهو أن يكسر إن وجد كسرا، فيقول : إنه لا يكون طلاقا يمتنع أن يكون طلاقا مع عدم النية، ولا يكون طلاقا مع عدمها، كجميع الكنايات عندكم وبعضها عندنا. والثاني : أن يبين المعنى الذي لأجله افترق النطق والكناية في باب النية
Second: the difference by the ruling itself in non-disputed matters. Example: a Maliki says: divorce occurs by writing because it is letters indicating intent, so it is permissible that divorce occur by it, like speech. A Shafi'i objects: the meaning in speech is that it is divorce even if he says 'I did not intend divorce', but it is not so in our issue: if he says 'I did not intend divorce', it is not divorce. Similarly, if he says 'I intended divorce', like standing and sitting. The answer to this is as previously mentioned: he should break it if he finds a break, saying: it is not divorce; it is impossible that it is divorce without intention, and it is not divorce when intention is absent, like all indirect expressions according to you and some of them according to us. And the second: to explain the meaning for which speech and indirect expression differ regarding intention.
Source pages p. 444

Commentary
This passage presents the second type of farq: difference by the ruling itself in matters that are not disputable. The example: a Maliki claims that divorce by writing is valid because writing conveys intent, analogizing it to spoken divorce. The Shafi'i objects by pointing out that spoken divorce takes effect even if the speaker denies intent, while written divorce does not; it requires intent. Thus there is a difference in the ruling (tatbiq) between speech and writing in an undisputed matter (the role of intention). The response uses two methods: first, 'breaking' by finding a counterexample where even spoken words without intention are not considered divorce (e.g., indirect expressions like kinayat). Second, explaining the underlying meaning that distinguishes speech from writing in terms of intention.

Key Takeaway
The second type of farq uses a clear, undisputed difference in the ruling to challenge an analogy, forcing the respondent to either break the objection or explain the underlying rationale for the difference.

Third Type of Farq: Resemblance by Similar Ruling
Author begins an example of zakat on a minor's property, illustrating the third type of farq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the third type of farq in jadal and reconstruct the analogical argument it counters.

Core Idea
A farq is valid when the branch's ruling resembles its own parallel ruling, not the original.

In jadal, the opponent may try to block an analogy by highlighting a difference (farq) between the original and the branch. The third type of farq is subtle: it points to a ruling that resembles the branch's own ruling, rather than a ruling that differs from the original. This lesson unpacks that concept with a fiqhi example from zakat on a minor's property.

Guided Path
1
Identify the analogical claim
What is the analogical claim being made? The passage states: 'Indeed, just as the tithe is obligatory on his crop, so zakat is obligatory on his wealth, like an adult.' The claimant (Maliki or Shafi'i) is arguing for zakat on a minor's property by analogy to the tithe on the minor's crop.
2
Locate the original (asl) and branch (far')
The original (asl) is the obligation of tithe (ʻushr) on the minor's crop. The branch (far') is the obligation of zakat on the minor's other wealth (e.g., money, livestock). The claimant treats the ruling of the original (tithe on crop) as similar to the ruling sought for the branch (zakat on wealth).
Note: The adult's zakat is not explicitly mentioned but implied by 'like an adult'.
3
Find the farq
The objector (proponent of the farq) would argue that the ruling of the original (tithe on crop) is actually similar to a ruling that is specific to the branch (zakat on wealth of the minor itself) and not to the original. In other words, the resemblance is between the branch's own two rulings, not between the original and the branch.
4
Determine the type of farq
This is the third type: the farq is based on a ruling that resembles the branch's own ruling. The analogy breaks because the original's ruling (tithe) is not similar to the branch's sought ruling (zakat on wealth) except through a resemblance that already exists within the branch itself.
Contrast with the first type (a ruling unique to the branch) and second type (a ruling that negates the original).
5
Reconstruct the counter-objection
The objector says: 'The ruling you used (tithe on crop) is not parallel to zakat on an adult's wealth; rather, it resembles the ruling you want for the branch (zakat on the minor's wealth). So the analogy is circular.' This is how the farq functions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : الفرق بحكم يشاكل حكم الفرع وذلك مثل أن يقول مالكي أو شافعي في إيجاب الزكاة في مال الصبي : إنه من وجب العشر في زرعه، وجبت الزكاة في ماله كالبالغ
Third: the difference by a ruling that resembles the branch's ruling. Example: a Maliki or Shafi'i regarding the obligation of zakat on a minor's property: 'Indeed, just as the tithe is obligatory on his crop, so zakat is obligatory on his wealth, like an adult.'
Source pages p. 444

Commentary
This passage introduces the third type of farq: difference by a ruling that resembles the branch's ruling. The example is cut off, but the pattern is that the opponent analogizes zakat on a minor's property to zakat on an adult's property, using the obligation of 'ushr (tithe) on the minor's crop as the original. The objection and response would follow, but are not included in this window. The point is that the branch (zakat on wealth) has a ruling that resembles a similar ruling on the branch itself (the 'ushr on crop of the minor), rather than being similar to the original (zakat on adult's wealth).

Key Takeaway
The third type of farq exposes an analogy where the ruling used is actually similar to the branch's own ruling, making the analogy circular.

Farq as a Refutation Tool in Jadal
Author gives an example of farq by description in a debate on sujud al-tilawa

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a farq (relevant distinction) in a jadal exchange and explain how it invalidates an analogical argument in Islamic legal reasoning.

Core Idea
Farq breaks an analogy by exposing a decisive difference in the cause.

In this passage, we see a classic jadal move: when a Hanafi argues that sujud al-tilawa is obligatory by analogy to the obligatory prayer prostration, the Shafi'i objects by pointing out a key difference: permissibility on a mount. This distinction (farq) targets the analogical link and forces the Hanafi to defend the underlying cause. Understanding this move is essential for engaging in rigorous usul al-fiqh debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi's analogy
Find the original case (asl), branch (far'), and the claimed shared cause ('illa) in the Hanafi's argument. Here, asl = sujud al-salb (obligatory prayer prostration), far' = sujud al-tilawa, and the shared 'illa is being 'a prostration performed in prayer'.
2
Locate the Shafi'i's objection (farq)
The Shafi'i does not simply deny the analogy; he provides a specific difference: the asl cannot be performed on a mount without excuse, but the far' can. This difference in a secondary ruling indicates a difference in the primary cause.
The farq is not arbitrary; it must be relevant to the ruling.
3
Analyze the reply (jawab)
The Hanafi responses by discussing the causes of both asl and far'. He explains that the cause for permissibility on a mount is that the reason (sabab) for the prostration occurred while on the mount, whereas for the asl, the reason does not occur on the mount.
This shows that the Hanafi tries to show that the difference does not undermine the analogy because it is secondary.
4
Assess who wins by jadal rules
According to the rules of jadal, the objector has the burden to provide a valid farq. The Shafi'i's farq seems strong because it directly relates to the ruling. The Hanafi's response may or may not be sufficient; in this text, the exchange is presented as an illustration of how farq works.
In practice, the strength of the farq depends on whether the difference is truly relevant to the 'illa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومثاله :أيضًا أن يقول حنفي في سجود التلاوة : إنه سجود يجوز فعله في الصلاة، فكان واجبًا كسجود صلاة الصلب فيقول الشافعي : المعنى في سجود صلاة الصلب أنه لا يجوز فعله على الراحلة من غير عذر وليس كذلك سجود التلاوة فإنه يجوز فعله على الراحلة من غير عذر، فهو كسجود النفل . والجواب عنه أن يتكلم على علة الأصل وعلة الفرع بكل ما يتكلم على التعليل، والذي يختص به أن علة جواز فعله على الراحلة، وهو أن يقول : إنما جاز فعله على الراحلة لأنه وجد سببه وهو على الراحلة، وسجود الصلب لم يوجد سببه على الراحلة، فلذلك لم يجز فعله على الراحلة.
And an example of that is: a Hanafi says regarding the prostration of recitation: It is a prostration permissible to perform during prayer, so it is obligatory like the prostration of the obligatory prayer. Then the Shafi'i objects: The meaning in the prostration of the obligatory prayer is that it is not permissible to perform it on a mount without excuse, but it is not so for the prostration of recitation, for it is permissible to perform it on a mount without excuse, so it is like the supererogatory prostration. The reply is to discuss the cause of the original and the cause of the branch with everything that is discussed regarding causation, and what is specific to it is that the cause of its permissibility on a mount is because its reason occurred while on the mount, while the reason for the obligatory prostration did not occur on the mount, therefore it is not permissible on the mount.
Source pages p. 444

Commentary
This passage presents a typical dialectical exchange in Islamic legal reasoning (jadal). The Hanafi argues by analogy: the prostration of recitation (sujud al-tilawa) is similar to the prostration in the obligatory prayer (sujud al-salb) because both are prostrations done in prayer, so it should be obligatory. The Shafi'i objects by distinguishing (farq) the two cases. He points out a relevant difference: the obligatory prostration cannot be performed on a mount without excuse, whereas the recitation prostration can. This difference in the ruling (hukm) indicates a difference in the underlying cause ('illa). The reply explains that the cause for permissibility on a mount is the presence of the reason (sabab) while on the mount, a condition not met for the obligatory prostration. This showcases a standard refutation method: identifying a decisive difference that breaks the analogical link.

Key Terms
علة
أصل
فرع
فرق

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) The obligatory prostration is required in all prayers.

Key Takeaway
Farq is a powerful refutation tool that breaks an analogy by revealing a relevant difference in the cause, and mastering it is essential for rigorous jadal in Islamic legal theory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Second Type of Farq: Distinction by the Ruling Itself
Explains the second type of farq and provides a legal example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define al-farq bi nafs al-hukm and distinguish it from other types of distinction in jadal.

Core Idea
A distinction based on the ruling itself can break an analogy without appealing to attributes.

In jadal (dialectical debate), not all distinctions are based on descriptive features of the original and branch. Sometimes the difference lies in the ruling (hukm) itself. This lesson introduces the second type of farq, where the opponent points out that the ruling differs in a way that invalidates the analogy. Understanding this tool is crucial for evaluating analogical arguments in Islamic legal debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling being compared
Find the legal ruling (hukm) that is applied to both the original (asl) and branch (far'). In the example, the ruling is 'divorce is effective'.
The ruling must be the same for the analogy to hold.
2
Compare the ruling in the original vs. branch
Look at how the ruling operates in each case. Does it have the same conditions? The Shafi'i notes that oral divorce is effective even without intention, while written divorce requires intention.
A difference in the conditions of the ruling constitutes a difference in the ruling itself.
3
Recognize that the difference is in the legal effect itself
The distinction here is not about a descriptive attribute (like 'letters' vs 'sound') but about the legal consequence: whether intention is required. This is farq bi nafs al-hukm.
The opponent uses this difference to argue that the analogy fails.
4
Understand why this breaks the analogy
If the ruling differs between the original and branch, the analogy is invalid because the very thing you are trying to extend (the ruling) does not apply uniformly.
The burden shifts to the original proponent to explain why the difference does not matter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : الفرق بنفس الحكم في غير مواضع الخلاف : مثل أن يقول مالكي : إن الكتاب يقع به الطلاق، لأنه حروف تنبئ عن المراد، فجاز أن يقع به الطلاق كالنطق . فيقول شافعي معترضا: المعنى في النطق أنه يكون طلاقا وإن قال «لم أرد به الطلاق»، وليس كذلك في ما عاد إلى مسألتنا فإنه إن قال لم أرد به الطلاق لم يكن طلاقا، وكذلك إذا قال أردت به الطلاق»، كقيامه وقعوده. والجواب عن مثل هذا ما تقدم، وهو أن يكسر إن وجد كسرا، فيقول : إنه لا النية، ولا يكون طلاقا مع عدمها، كجميع الكنايات أن يكون طلاقا يمتنع عندكم وبعضها عندنا. والثاني : أن يبين المعنى الذي لأجله افترق النطق والكناية في باب النية.
The second: Distinction by the ruling itself in non-disputed matters, such as when a Maliki says: The written word effects divorce, because it is letters that convey the intended meaning, so it is permissible for divorce to occur by it, like speech. Then a Shafi'i objects: The meaning in speech is that it constitutes divorce even if he says 'I did not intend divorce', but it is not so in our case, because if he says 'I did not intend divorce' it is not divorce, and similarly if he says 'I intended divorce', like his standing and sitting. The reply to such is as previously, and that is to break (the analogy) if a break exists, so he says: Indeed, there is no intention, and it is not divorce without it, like all kinayat (ambiguous expressions) that it is impossible for them to be divorce according to you, and some according to us. And the second (method): To explain the reason for which speech and written expression differ regarding intention.
Source pages p. 444

Commentary
This passage describes the second type of distinction (farq) in jadal: distinguishing by the ruling itself (al-farq bi nafs al-hukm) in cases that are not universally agreed upon. The example involves divorce by written communication (kitab) versus oral pronouncement (nutq). The Maliki argues analogically: just as oral divorce is effective because words convey meaning, written divorce should also be effective. The Shafi'i objects by noting a key difference in the ruling: oral divorce is effective even without explicit intention, whereas written divorce requires intention—without it, it is not divorce. This difference in the ruling (hukm) itself breaks the analogy. The reply explains two strategies: first, 'break' (kasr) the analogy by showing the inconsistency in the opponent's own position (e.g., some expressions like kinayat require intention anyway); second, elucidate the reason (ma'na) behind the difference, namely that oral and written expressions differ fundamentally in how intention operates. This illustrates how the ruling's own attributes can serve as a valid distinction.

Key Terms
فرق بنفس الحكم
كناية
كسر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example of divorce by writing, what is the key difference in the ruling (hukm) between oral and written divorce that the Shafi'i points out?
A) Oral divorce requires two witnesses.
B) Written divorce requires intention; oral divorce does not.
C) Oral divorce is always revocable.
D) Written divorce is not recognized in Islamic law.

Key Takeaway
Al-farq bi nafs al-hukm is a powerful tool in jadal: it attacks the analogy by highlighting a discrepancy in the legal ruling itself, not just in attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the following analogy: 'Analogy between selling dates and selling wheat for interest because both are foodstuffs.' Use farq bi nafs al-hukm to construct an objection: find a difference in the ruling that breaks the analogy (e.g., one is measured by weight, the other by volume).

Debate Strategy: Kasr and Bayan al-Ma'na in Written Divorce
Exemplifies the two reply methods to a farq objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the two reply strategies (kasr and bayan al-ma'na) to a given analogical argument in Islamic legal debate.

Core Idea
When faced with a farq bi nafs al-hukm, the defender can break the opponent's consistency or explain the reason for the difference.

In the previous lesson, we introduced farq bi nafs al-hukm. Here we delve deeper into the specific example of divorce by writing, and the two dialectical responses: kasr (breaking the analogy by pointing out inconsistency) and bayan al-ma'na (explaining the rationale behind the difference). Mastering these replies is essential for defending an analogy against a farq-based objection.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Maliki's analogical argument
The Maliki argues that written divorce is valid because it conveys meaning, like oral speech. The ruling (divorce) should apply.
This is the initial position (mudda'i).
2
Identify the Shafi'i's farq objection
The Shafi'i objects that the ruling differs: oral divorce works without intention, written requires intention. This is a farq bi nafs al-hukm.
This objection threatens the analogy.
3
Analyze the first reply: kasr
The defender can respond by 'breaking' (kasr) the opponent's consistency: if the opponent (Shafi'i) allows some kinayat (ambiguous expressions) to be divorce without intention, then his principle is inconsistent.
Kasr aims to show that the opponent's own rules allow what he denies.
4
Analyze the second reply: bayan al-ma'na
Alternatively, the defender can explain the reason (ma'na) for the difference: oral speech is inherently communicative, while writing is a deliberate act that requires intention to be effective.
This response tries to justify the difference, not deny it.
5
Evaluate which reply is more effective
Kasr is a strong strategic move if inconsistency exists; bayan al-ma'na may salvage the analogy by showing the difference is justified. In practice, the choice depends on the specific case.
Both are valid dialectical responses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : الفرق بنفس الحكم في غير مواضع الخلاف : مثل أن يقول مالكي : إن الكتاب يقع به الطلاق، لأنه حروف تنبئ عن المراد، فجاز أن يقع به الطلاق كالنطق . فيقول شافعي معترضا: المعنى في النطق أنه يكون طلاقا وإن قال «لم أرد به الطلاق»، وليس كذلك في ما عاد إلى مسألتنا فإنه إن قال لم أرد به الطلاق لم يكن طلاقا، وكذلك إذا قال أردت به الطلاق»، كقيامه وقعوده. والجواب عن مثل هذا ما تقدم، وهو أن يكسر إن وجد كسرا، فيقول : إنه لا النية، ولا يكون طلاقا مع عدمها، كجميع الكنايات أن يكون طلاقا يمتنع عندكم وبعضها عندنا. والثاني : أن يبين المعنى الذي لأجله افترق النطق والكناية في باب النية.
The second: Distinction by the ruling itself in non-disputed matters, such as when a Maliki says: The written word effects divorce, because it is letters that convey the intended meaning, so it is permissible for divorce to occur by it, like speech. The reply to such is as previously, and that is to break (the analogy) if a break exists, so he says: Indeed, there is no intention, and it is not divorce without it, like all kinayat (ambiguous expressions) that it is impossible for them to be divorce according to you, and some according to us. And the second (method): To explain the reason for which speech and written expression differ regarding intention.

Third Type of Farq: Distinction by a Resembling Ruling
Author explains the third type of farq: distinguishing by a ruling that resembles the branch's ruling

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and evaluate the third type of farq (distinction by a resembling ruling) in a munazara exchange.

Core Idea
A farq must use a ruling that truly resembles the branch's ruling, not just any difference.

In dialectical debates over analogies (qiyas), the objector may seek to break the analogy by pointing to a ruling that resembles the branch's ruling but leads to a different outcome. This lesson analyzes that move and the proper response, using the example of zakat on a minor's wealth.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original analogy
Find the asl (original case), far' (branch case), 'illah (cause), and hukm (ruling) in the analogy. Here, the Maliki/Shafi'i analogizes the minor to the adult based on the obligation of tithe on crop.
The adult has both tithe on crop and zakat on wealth; the minor has tithe on crop, so must have zakat.
2
Locate the farq
Identify the distinguishing ruling introduced by the objector. The Hanafi points out that the adult is obligated for Hajj on his wealth, while the minor is not, and uses this to break the analogy.
The farq is 'the minor is not obligated for Hajj, so zakat should not follow.'
3
Check resemblance of the farq's ruling to the branch's ruling
Determine whether the ruling used in the farq truly resembles the branch's ruling in a way relevant to the 'illah. Here, the author notes that the method of obligation for zakat and Hajj are different, so the farq is invalid.
The farq fails because the resemblance is superficial—the rulings belong to different categories of obligation.
4
Analyze the reply
The reply invalidates the farq by discussing the causes and showing that the analogy between zakat and Hajj is not sound. The debater must address the 'illah directly, not merely point to another ruling.
A valid farq must directly challenge the 'illah; otherwise, it is merely a superficial distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : الفرق بحكم يشاكل حكم الفرع وذلك مثل أن يقول مالكي أو شافعي في إيجاب الزكاة في مال الصبي : إنه من وجب العشر في زرعه، وجبت الزكاة في ماله كالبالغ . فيقول الحنفي : البالغ يتعلق الحج بماله فتعلقت الزكاة بماله، وهذا لا يتعلق وجوب الحج بماله فلم يتعلق وجوب الزكاة بماله . والجواب أن يتكلم على العلتين بكل ما يتكلم به على العلل، والذي يعنى به أن يفسد اعتبار الزكاة بالحج بأن طريق أحدهما مخالف لطريق الآخر في الوجوب فلا يصح اعتبار أحدهما بالآخر.
The third: Distinction by a ruling that resembles the branch's ruling, such as when a Maliki or Shafi'i says regarding the obligation of zakat on a minor's wealth: 'He on whom the tithe is obligatory on his crop, zakat is obligatory on his wealth, like the adult.' Then the Hanafi says: 'The adult is subject to the obligation of Hajj on his wealth, so zakat is also obligated on his wealth, but this one (the minor) is not subject to the obligation of Hajj on his wealth, so zakat is not obligated on his wealth.' The reply is to discuss both causes with everything discussed about causes, and the intended meaning is that he invalidates the analogy of zakat with Hajj by showing that the method of one is contrary to the method of the other in obligation, so it is not valid to analogize one with the other.
Source pages p. 444

Commentary
This passage outlines the third type of distinction (farq): distinguishing by a ruling that is similar to the branch's ruling (al-farq bi hukm yushakil hukm al-far'). The example involves zakat on a minor's wealth. The Maliki/Shafi'i argue by analogy: just as an adult pays zakat on his wealth (including crop tithe), so should a minor. The Hanafi objects by introducing a parallel ruling: the adult is obligated to perform Hajj, which links to zakat in some way, but the minor is not obligated for Hajj, and therefore the analogy fails. The reply explains that this is not a valid distinction because the method of obligation for zakat and Hajj are different; comparing them is invalid. This type of farq uses a third ruling (the branch's ruling resembling) to create a distinction that breaks the original analogy, though the reply shows that such a move must be carefully examined for relevance.

Key Terms
فرق بحكم يشاكل حكم الفرع
علة
قياس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) The adult is obligated to perform Hajj; the minor is not.
C) The minor's crop does not require tithe.
D) The adult's zakat is calculated differently.

Key Takeaway
The third type of farq requires that the distinguishing ruling actually resembles the branch's ruling in a way that undermines the analogy; otherwise, the objection is invalid.

The Fourth Type of Farq: Countering a Distinction by Similarities
Author explains the fourth type of farq: distinguishing by a type of similarity that cuts both ways

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a counter-argument against a dialectical distinction by showing that multiple shared attributes outweigh a single difference.

Core Idea
A single difference does not break an analogy if many similarities outweigh it.

In jadal, opponents often try to break an analogy by pointing out a single difference between the original case and the new case. The fourth type of farq teaches you how to respond: by showing that despite that difference, there are many other similarities that make the analogy hold. This lesson unpacks the method using a real argument about maintenance (nafaqa) for relatives.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original analogy
Find the original case (cousin) and the new case (another relative like a brother from marriage). The Shafi'i claims that since maintenance is not obligatory for a cousin when religion differs, it should not be obligatory for other relatives.
The original analogy is: cousin : no maintenance :: other relative : no maintenance.
2
Spot the opponent's distinction
The opponent points out one difference: the cousin relationship does not prohibit marriage, while the new relative does (e.g., brother from marriage is mahram).
This is the farq bi darb min al-shubah – distinguishing by a type of similarity that cuts the other way.
3
List other shared attributes
The responder lists several other shared rulings between the two cases: both involve inheritance (mīrāth), both involve testimony (shahāda), and both involve retaliation (qisās). These similarities suggest the cases are alike in many ways.
The more shared attributes, the weaker the single difference becomes.
4
Apply the principle of preponderance
If the number or importance of shared attributes outweighs the single difference, the original analogy stands. The rule is 'consideration of the majority of similarities is better' (iʿtibār akthar al-ashbāh awlā).
This is not about counting alone; the juristic weight of each attribute matters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : الفرق بضرب من ضروب الشبه : وذلك مثل أن يقول شافعي في نفقة غير الوالد والولد إنها لا تجب، لأن كل قرابة لا يجب بها النفقة مع اتفاق الدين فلا يجب بها النفقة مع اختلاف الدين كقرابة ابن العم . فيقول المخالف معترضا: المعنى في الأصل أن تلك القرابة لا يتعلق بها تحريم المناكحة، وهذه القرابة يتعلق بها تحريم المناكحة، فهي كقرابة الولد. والجواب أن يتكلم على العلتين بكل ما يتكلم على العلل، والذي يختص بهذا أن يقال : الفرق يجمع مثله، فيقول : إن كان الأصل والفرع افترقا في تحريم المناكحة إلا أنهما اجتمعا في ثبوت الميراث، أو يقول : إن كان إذا افترق الأصل والفرع في المناكحة فيوجب افتراقهما في النفقة، فقد استويا في الشهادة فيجب أن يستويا في إسقاط النفقة أو يقول : إنهما قد افترقا في تحريم المناكحة، إلا أنهما متساويان في أحكام كثيرة، كقبول الشهادة ووجوب القصاص، واعتبار أكثر الأشباه أولى.
The fourth: Distinction by a type of similarity, such as when a Shafi'i says regarding maintenance for other than parents and children: It is not obligatory, because every kinship relationship for which maintenance is not obligatory when religion agrees, it is not obligatory when religion differs, like the cousin. Then the opponent objects: The meaning in the original is that such kinship does not entail prohibition of marriage, whereas this kinship entails prohibition of marriage, so it is like the kinship of a child. The reply is to discuss both causes with everything discussed about causes, and what is specific to this is that it is said: The distinction can be countered by a similar one, so he says: Although the original and branch differ in prohibition of marriage, they still agree in the establishment of inheritance; or he says: If they differ in marriage, necessitating a difference in maintenance, yet they are equal in testimony, so they should be equal in dropping maintenance; or he says: They have differed in prohibition of marriage, but they are equal in many rulings, such as accepting testimony and the obligation of retaliation, and considering the majority of similarities is better.
Source pages p. 445

Commentary
This passage explains the fourth type of distinction (farq): distinguishing by a type of similarity (al-farq bi darb min durub al-shubah). The example involves the obligation of maintenance (nafaqa) for relatives other than parents and children, focusing on the effect of religious difference. The Shafi'i argues that maintenance is not obligatory for a non-Muslim relative (like a cousin) if it is not obligatory for a Muslim relative. The opponent objects by pointing out that the original (cousin) does not entail prohibition of marriage, whereas the branch (say, a brother from marriage) does, so it is like a child. The reply discusses several counter-arguments: one can show that despite the difference in marriage prohibition, they agree in inheritance; or that equality in one ruling (testimony) should imply equality in another; or that despite one difference, many similarities (like testimony and retaliation) argue for maintaining the original ruling. The principle is that if multiple similarities outweigh a single difference, the analogy may stand. This shows the complexity of jadal where multiple properties are weighed.

Key Terms
شبه
نفقة
تحريم المناكحة
ميراث
شهادة
قصاص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the maintenance example, what objection does the opponent raise against the Shafi'i's original claim?
A) The Shafi'i ignored the obligation of inheritance.
B) The kinship in question entails prohibition of marriage, unlike the cousin.
C) The religious difference does not affect maintenance.
D) The support for children is mandatory, but not for other relatives.

Key Takeaway
When faced with a distinction based on a single similarity, counter by showing that multiple other similarities outweigh that one difference, following the principle of preponderance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the analogy: 'Zakat is due on agricultural produce measured at the time of harvest (ʿidd al-hisad). Some argue it is also due on fruits, analogizing to grains. The opponent objects: grains are staple food, fruits are not. ?
In a jadal exercise, take the position that the prohibition of gambling (qimar) extends to online stock trading. The opponent distinguishes: stock trading involves genuine risk and ownership, while gambling is pure chance. Reply by showing that both involve uncertainty, both can lead to loss of money, and both are often transactions of properties, and that these similarities outweigh the difference.

Understanding Qalb: The Reversal Objection
Author defines qalb and gives a partial Hanafi example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a qalb objection and construct one using a given cause and original from usul al-fiqh.

Core Idea
Qalb turns the opponent's own cause against them to derive a contradictory ruling.

In dialectical debate (jadal), one powerful tool is qalb (reversal). Instead of attacking the opponent's analogy from outside, the objector uses the opponent's own claimed cause and original case to derive a contradictory ruling. This lesson unpacks the definition and the partial Hanafi example, equipping you to recognise and construct qalb objections in Islamic legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proponent's claimed cause ('illa) and original case (asl)
In the Hanafi example, the proponent (Hanafi) claims that i'tikaf is like standing at 'Arafat because both are 'specific seclusions' that are not purely devotional by themselves. The cause is 'being a specific seclusion not purely devotional by itself' and the original is 'standing at 'Arafat'.
2
Understand the ruling the proponent derives from the analogy
The Hanafi concludes that fasting is a condition for i'tikaf because the original (standing at 'Arafat) also requires fasting to be valid as a devotion.
3
Check whether a contradictory ruling can be derived from the same cause and original
For a valid qalb, the objector must show that from the same cause and original, a ruling opposite to the proponent's conclusion follows logically. For example, one might argue that standing at 'Arafat itself requires conditions that i'tikaf does not, or that the analogy actually proves the opposite.
The passage cuts off before the full objection; the key is that qalb must preserve the original attributes and cause.
4
Apply the qalb framework to the Hanafi example
Construct the qalb: The objector accepts that i'tikaf is like standing at 'Arafat, but then derives a different ruling, e.g., that i'tikaf does not require fasting because the original does not require it in the same way. This challenges the Hanafi's conclusion without denying the analogy.
This step illustrates how qalb turns the opponent's reasoning against them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المطلب الرابع: الاعتراض على العلة بالقلب مع تبقية الأصل والوصف بحالهما ؛ أو هو ربط المعترض حكما مخالفا لحكم المستدل بعلة المستدل وأصل المستدل ؛ فمثلا إذا قال الحنفي مستدلا على أن الصوم شرط في الاعتكاف : الاعتكاف لبث مخصوص فلا يكون بمجرده قربة، كالوقوف بعرفة .
The fourth topic: The objection to the cause by reversal (qalb) while preserving the original and the attribute as they are; or it is the objector's linking of a contradictory ruling to the cause and original of the proponent. For example, when the Hanafi argues that fasting is a condition for i'tikaf: 'I'tikaf is a specific seclusion, so it is not purely a devotional act by itself, like standing at 'Arafat.'
Source pages p. 445

Commentary
This passage introduces a key dialectical technique called qalb (reversal). The author defines it as the objector taking the proponent's claimed cause ('illa) and the original case (asl) and deriving a conflicting ruling (hukm mukhalif) from them, thereby refuting the analogy. The cause and original attributes remain unchanged. The example is given: a Hanafi argues that fasting is a condition for i'tikaf (seclusion in the mosque), analogizing i'tikaf to the standing at 'Arafat (wuquf), which is not a pure devotion by itself. The objector would then reverse to show that this analogy actually undermines the Hanafi's point. However, the passage cuts off before the full objection is stated. The definition makes clear that qalb is a powerful tool in jadal for turning the opponent's argument against them.

Key Terms
قلب
علة
أصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the definition of qalb (reversal) according to this passage?
A) Changing the opponent's cause to a different one.
B) Linking a contradictory ruling to the opponent's cause and original while preserving the original and attribute.
C) Rejecting the analogy outright without argument.
D) Introducing a new analogy to support the original claim.

Key Takeaway
Qalb allows an objector to use the opponent's own cause and original to derive a contradictory ruling, refuting the analogy without introducing new premises.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Using qalb, construct an objection that derives a contradictory ruling from the same cause and original (e.g., that nabidh is permissible if it does not intoxicate, or that the cause leads to a different ruling).

The Fourth Method: Al-Farq (Distinction) by a Kind of Similarity
Author presents the fourth method of objecting to a cause using distinction by similarity, with an example from nafaqa.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify an al-farq objection in an analogical argument from Islamic jurisprudence and evaluate its validity by assessing the relevance of the distinction.

Core Idea
A distinction breaks an analogy only if the distinguishing attribute is relevant to the ruling.

In dialectical reasoning, one way to object to an analogy is to point out a difference between the original and the branch. But not every difference is sufficient—the opponent must argue that the distinction matters. This passage presents a complete example from the law of maintenance (nafaqa), where a Shafi'i jurist's analogy is challenged by a distinction based on the prohibition of marriage (tahrim al-munakaha). The author then provides three possible responses, showing how the proponent of the analogy can counter the objection.

Guided Path
1
Identify the underlying analogical argument
Find the Shafi'i's claim: maintenance is not obligatory for non-parent/non-child relatives. He argues based on a general rule about religious difference. What is the original case and what is the branch?
The original is a cousin (ibn al-amm) with religious difference; the branch is a cousin with agreement in religion.
2
Locate the opponent's distinction (farq)
The opponent points out that the child (walad) is different from the cousin because the child is a mahram relative with a prohibition of marriage (tahrim al-munakaha). How does this distinction aim to break the analogy?
3
Assess the relevance of the distinction
In jadal, a distinction is only effective if the distinguishing attribute is relevant to the ruling. Ask: Does the prohibition of marriage logically affect the obligation of maintenance?
Consider if maintenance is linked to marriage prohibition in Islamic law.
4
Analyze the counter-arguments
The author offers three possible responses: (a) they agree on inheritance, (b) equality in testimony should imply equality in maintenance, (c) they are equal in many rulings, so the more similar aspects should be weighted. What is the logic of each response?
These responses argue that other similarities outweigh the distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : الفرق بضرب من ضروب الشبه : وذلك مثل أن يقول شافعي في نفقة غير الوالد والولد إنها لا تجب، لأن كل قرابة لا تجب بها النفقة مع اختلاف الدين فلا يجب بها النفقة مع اتفاق الدين كقرابة ابن العم. فيقول المخالف معترضا: المعنى في الأصل أن تلك القرابة لا يتعلق بها تحريم المناكحة، وهذه القرابة يتعلق بها تحريم المناكحة، فهي كقرابة الولد. والجواب أن يتكلم على العلتين بكل ما يتكلم على العلل، والذي يختص بهذا أن يقال : الفرق يجمع مثله، فيقول : إن كان الأصل والفرع افترقا في تحريم المناكحة إلا أنهما اجتمعا في ثبوت الميراث، أو يقول : إن كان إذا افترق الأصل والفرع في المناكحة فيوجب افتراقهما في النفقة، فقد استويا في الشهادة فيجب أن يستويا في إسقاط النفقة أو يقول : إنهما قد افترقا في تحريم المناكحة، إلا أنهما متساويان في أحكام كثيرة، كقبول الشهادة ووجوب القصاص، واعتبار أكثر الأشباه أولى.
The fourth: distinction by a kind of similarity: such as a Shafi'i saying regarding the maintenance of other than the father and child that it is not obligatory, because every kinship for which maintenance is not obligatory with difference of religion, then maintenance is not obligatory with agreement of religion, like the kinship of a cousin. So the opponent objects saying: The meaning in the original is that that kinship is not connected to the prohibition of marriage, and this kinship is connected to the prohibition of marriage, so it is like the kinship of a child. The answer is to speak about both causes with everything spoken about causes, and what is specific to this is to say: the distinction includes its like, so he says: if the original and the branch differ in the prohibition of marriage but they agree in the establishment of inheritance, or he says: if when the original and branch differ in marriage, then their difference in maintenance is necessitated, but they are equal in testimony so they should be equal in dropping maintenance, or he says: they have differed in the prohibition of marriage, but they are equal in many rulings, like acceptance of testimony and obligation of retaliation, and consideration of the more similar is more appropriate.
Source pages p. 445

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth method of objecting to a cause (al-‘illa) in dialectical reasoning: al-farq (distinction) by a kind of similarity. The author provides a complete example from the jurisprudence of nafaqa (maintenance).

First, a Shafi'i jurist argues that maintenance is not obligatory for non-parent/non-child relatives because any kinship for which maintenance is not obligatory when religion differs (e.g., a non-Muslim relative) should also not obligate maintenance when religion agrees (e.g., a Muslim cousin). This is an analogical argument: the original (non-Muslim relative) has no maintenance due to difference of religion, so the branch (Muslim cousin) should also have no maintenance since the cause (lack of maintenance in case of religious difference) is applied.

The opponent (presumably a Hanafi or another) objects by pointing out a distinguishing feature: in the original case (the non-Muslim relative), there is no prohibition of marriage (tahrim al-munakaha), whereas in the branch case (the Muslim cousin), there is a prohibition of marriage (since cousins are marriageable? Actually, in Islamic law, cousins are marriageable, so there is no prohibition; wait, the text says 'this kinship is connected to the prohibition of marriage' which would be the case for mahram relatives. But the example seems to be about a relative like a child who is mahram. So the opponent claims that the kinship of the child (walad) is mahram, and the cousin is not mahram, so they are different. Actually, the text: 'المعنى في الأصل أن تلك القرابة لا يتعلق بها تحريم المناكحة، وهذه القرابة يتعلق بها تحريم المناكحة' — meaning the original kinship (cousin? ) does not have marriage prohibition, while this kinship (child) does. But the example is confusing. Possibly the original is the non-Muslim relative who is not mahram? Let's parse carefully.

The Shafi'i's analogy: 'كل قرابة لا تجب بها النفقة مع اختلاف الدين فلا يجب بها النفقة مع اتفاق الدين كقرابة ابن العم.' So he uses the cousin as an example of a relative with no maintenance despite agreement of religion because of the general rule. The opponent objects: the meaning in the original (the cousin) is that there is no marriage prohibition, but in this kinship (the child) there is marriage prohibition, so it is like the kinship of a child. So the opponent is saying that the child is different because there is marriage prohibition (i.e., the child is mahram, so maintenance is obligatory). Actually, the opponent is arguing that the child should be exempted because of the marriage prohibition, so the Shafi'i's generalization fails.

The author then gives the answer: the one using the distinction must speak about both causes, and specifically he can respond in several ways: (1) even if they differ in marriage prohibition, they agree on inheritance; (2) if difference in marriage prohibition necessitates difference in maintenance, then equality in testimony should necessitate equality in dropping maintenance; (3) they differ in marriage prohibition but are equal in many other rulings like testimony and retaliation, so considering the more similar aspects is better.

This illustrates that in jadal, the validity of a distinction depends on whether the distinguishing attribute is relevant to the legal effect. The answer provides counter-arguments that show similarity in other rulings should be given weight.

Key Terms
الفرق
العلة
الأصل
الفرع
تحريم المناكحة

A) The child inherits from the uncle
B) The child is a mahram relative with prohibition of marriage
C) The child is a non-Muslim
D) The child's testimony is not accepted

Key Takeaway
Al-farq is a dialectical method that requires the distinguishing attribute to be relevant; the proponent can counter by showing other similarities outweigh the difference.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In the following analogical argument: 'Wine is forbidden because it intoxicates; all intoxicants are forbidden via qiyas. Beer is like wine in intoxication, so beer is forbidden.' Identify a possible al-farq objection based on a difference between wine and beer (e.g., wine is made from grapes, beer from barley). Then assess whether that distinction is relevant to the ruling of prohibition.
?
Using the three counter-arguments from the passage, construct a response to the objection you identified.

Dialectic -- Jadal

Qalb (Inversion): Using the Opponent's Own 'Illa Against Him
Defines qalb, its types, and exemplifies with fiqhi disputes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a qalb argument and distinguish it from general mu'arada in a jadal exchange.

Core Idea
Qalb turns the opponent's own 'illa and asl into a weapon against him.

In jadal, a powerful technique is to accept your opponent's premises but invert their conclusion. This is qalb. Unlike mu'arada, where you bring a new 'illa, qalb uses the very same 'illa to derive an opposite ruling, exposing its inconsistency. The passage defines qalb and illustrates it with fiqhi examples, showing how it can invalidate the opponent's reasoning unless properly countered.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proponent's original qiyas
Extract the 'illa (effective cause), asl (original case), and ruling from the proponent's argument. This sets the baseline.
Example: Maliki uses death (asl) with 'illa 'removing taklif' to inherit khiyar.
2
Check if the objector uses the same 'illa and asl
If the objector uses a different 'illa or asl, it is mu'arada. If he uses the exact same ones, it is qalb. This is the key distinction.
In the Hanafi's response, he uses the same 'illa (death removes taklif) and asi (death/insanity).
3
Determine the type of qalb
Explicit qalb: the opposite ruling is stated clearly. Implicit qalb (qalb al-taswiya): only equality of two cases is shown without stating the opposite ruling.
The Hanafi's inversion is explicit because he states 'so the option must not be passed on.'
4
Assess whether the inversion invalidates the 'illa
A valid 'illa must be exclusive to the original ruling. If it can support the opposite, the 'illa is flawed. Check if the objector's inversion shows that the 'illa is not exclusive.
The Maliki's 'illa of 'removing taklif' could support both inheritance and non-inheritance, so it may be invalid.
5
Analyze the proponent's possible response
The proponent can respond by showing a relevant difference between the original and the inverted case that breaks the analogy. This is the core of jadal defense.
The Maliki responds that death involves an heir, unlike insanity, so the analogy fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والقلب في حقيقته نوع من المعارضة، إلا أن الفرق بينهما أن العلة المذكورة في المعارضة والأصل المذكور فيها يكونان مغايرين للعلة والأصل اللذين ذكرهما المستدل بخلاف القلب فإن علته وأصله هما علة المستدل وأصله. ينقسم القلب إلى قسمين رئيسين : قلب تم فيه التصريح بالحكم، وقلب لم يتم فيه التصريح بالحكم ويسمى قلب التسوية . أ - القلب الصريح وهو يفسد العلة المستدل بها، لأنه يجب أن تكون العلة تتعلق بالحكم الذي يعلق عليها وتختص به من حيث لا يصلح أن يعلق عليها ضده، فإذا بين المعترض أنه يصح أن يعلق عليها ضده وما ينافيه، خرجت بذلك عن أن تكون علة، وذلك مثل أن يستدل المالكي على أن الخيار في البيع موروث، بأن الموت معنى يزيل التكليف، فوجب ألا يبطل الخيار، كالجنون والإغماء. فيقول الحنفي معترضا: أقلب هذه العلة فأقول : إن الموت معنى يبطل التكليف، فوجب ألا يثقل الخيار إلى الوارث كالجنون والإغماء . . وطريق المالكي في الجواب عن ذلك أن يتكلم على القلب بما يبطله ليسلم له دليله، وذلك أن يقول : إن قولك «لا ينقل الخيار إلى الوارث»، يقتضي أن يكون ثم وارث، ولا يصح وارث مع الجنون والإغماء، وأيضًا فإنه لا يمتنع أن لا يبطل الخيار بالجنون والموت ويستويان في ذلك وإن كان الموت ينفرد بنقل ذلك إلى الورثة، ألا ترى أن خيار الرد بالعيب قد استوى بالجنون والموت وأنه لا يبطل ذلك فإن الموت ينقله إلى الورثة دون الجنون. ومثال ذلك أيضًا أن يقول الحنفي في الاستدلال على وجوب مسح ربع الرأس في الوضوء مسح الرأس ركن في الوضوء، فلا يكفي فيه أقل ما يطلق عليه الاسم، كالوجه. فيقول له الشافعي معترضا مسح الرأس ركن في الوضوء فلا يكفي فيه الربع، قياسًا على الوجه فقياس الشافعي أنتج أن مسح الرأس لا يكفي فيه الربع، وهذا خلاف مذهب الحنفي، فكان هذا القياس مبطلا لمذهب الحنفي صراحة مع كونه لم يثبت مذهب الشافعي لوجود مذهب ثالث وهو مسح الجميع كما يقول الإمام مالك رضي الله عنه. ومن ذلك كذلك : أن يقول الشافعي في البيع الموقوف : إنه باطل، لأنه عقد في حق الغير من غير ولاية ولا استنابة، فلم يعقد في حق ذلك الغير، كالشراء. فيقول الحنفي : أقلب فأقول : فلم يبطل لعدم الإذن، كالشراء. والجواب بالنسبة للمستدل أن يقول : هذه الأوصاف لا تؤثر في حكم القلب لأنك لو قلت «عقد فلم يبطل لعدم الإذن، لم ينتقض بشيء .. ثم إن هذه الأوصاف تقتضي إبطال العقد، فلا يجوز أن يعلق عليها الصحة. ومن أمثلته أيضًا : أن يستدل شافعي أو حنبلي في بيع الفضولي أنه باطل، لأنه عقد على ملك العين بغير ولاية ولا نيابة فلم ينعقد، كما ساغ ملكه لغيره أو لنفسه.
And qalb (inversion) in its reality is a type of mu'arada (objection), except that the difference between them is that the 'illa (effective cause) mentioned in mu'arada and the asl (original case) mentioned in it are different from the 'illa and asl that the proponent mentioned, unlike qalb, for its 'illa and asl are the proponent's 'illa and asl. Qalb is divided into two main types: qalb in which the ruling is explicitly stated, and qalb in which the ruling is not explicitly stated, called qalb al-taswiya (inversion of equalization). a) Explicit qalb: it invalidates the 'illa used by the proponent, because the 'illa must be attached to the ruling that depends on it and be specific to it in such a way that its opposite cannot be attached to it. So when the objector shows that its opposite or contradictory can be attached to it, it thereby ceases to be a valid 'illa. An example is when a Maliki argues that the option (khiyar) in a sale is inherited, because death is a meaning that removes legal responsibility, so it must not void the option, like insanity and unconsciousness. The Hanafi says in objection: I invert this 'illa and say: death is a meaning that voids legal responsibility, so the option must not be passed on to the heir, like insanity and unconsciousness. The Maliki's way of answering this is to discuss qalb in a way that invalidates it so that his evidence remains sound. That is by saying: Your statement 'it does not transfer the option to the heir' requires that there be an heir, and an heir is not valid with insanity and unconsciousness. Also, it is not impossible that the option is not voided by death and insanity, and they are equal in that, even if death alone transfers it to the heirs. Do you not see that the option of return due to a defect is equal between insanity and death, and that it is not voided by either, for death transfers it to the heirs while insanity does not. Another example: The Hanafi argues for the obligation of wiping a quarter of the head in wudu: wiping the head is a pillar of wudu, so the least amount that the name applies to is not sufficient, like the face. The Shafi'i says in objection: wiping the head is a pillar of wudu, so a quarter is not sufficient, by analogy with the face. The Shafi'i's analogy produced that wiping the head is not sufficient with a quarter, contrary to the Hanafi school. So this analogy explicitly invalidated the Hanafi school, even though it did not establish the Shafi'i school because there is a third school, which is wiping all of it as Imam Malik (may Allah be pleased with him) says. Also from that: The Shafi'i says about a suspended sale (al-bay' al-mawquf): it is invalid, because it is a contract involving the right of another without guardianship (wilaya) or deputyship (isti'naba), so it is not concluded concerning that other, like purchase. The Hanafi says: I invert and say: it is not invalid due to lack of permission, like purchase. The proponent's answer is to say: These descriptions do not affect the ruling of qalb, because if you said 'a contract, so it is not invalid due to lack of permission', it would not be refuted by anything... Moreover, these descriptions necessitate invalidating the contract, so validity cannot be attached to them. And among its examples also: A Shafi'i or Hanbali argues that the sale of a fuduli (unauthorized agent) is invalid, because it is a contract over the property of a specific person without guardianship or deputyship, so it is not concluded, just as it is not valid for his ownership of something for another or for himself.
Source pages p. 446

Commentary
This passage is a definition and illustration of the dialectical technique known as qalb (inversion), which is a specific type of mu'arada (objection). The author first distinguishes qalb from general mu'arada: in mu'arada, the objector brings a different 'illa (effective cause) and asl (original case) than the proponent, whereas in qalb, the objector uses the very same 'illa and asl to derive a ruling opposite to the proponent's conclusion. This inversion is considered a strong refutation because it shows that the proponent's 'illa is not restricted to his desired ruling.

The author then divides qalb into two categories: explicit qalb (where the opposite ruling is stated clearly) and implicit qalb (qalb al-taswiya, where the ruling is not explicitly stated but the equality between two cases is shown). The explicit qalb is explained as invalidating the proponent's 'illa, because a valid 'illa must be exclusive to the ruling it supports; if it can support the opposite ruling as well, it is not a proper 'illa.

Several examples from fiqh are presented. In the first, a Maliki argues that the option (khiyar) in a sale is inherited upon the death of the party, using as 'illa that death removes legal responsibility (taklif), analogizing to insanity and unconsciousness where the option is not voided. The Hanafi objector inverts: the same 'illa (death removes taklif) leads to the opposite ruling that the option is not transferred to the heir (because the heir is not present), again analogizing to insanity and unconsciousness. The author then describes how the Maliki can respond by arguing that the Hanafi's inversion fails because an heir exists only in the case of death, not in insanity, so the analogy breaks down. Additionally, the Maliki can point out that the option of return due to a defect remains in both death and insanity, but only death transfers it to heirs, showing that the two cases are not perfectly analogous.

The second example involves wudu: the Hanafi argues that wiping a quarter of the head suffices, because wiping the head is a pillar like the face, where the least amount is not enough. The Shafi'i inverts: the same reasoning leads to requiring the whole head (since the face requires washing all of it). The author notes that this inversion invalidates the Hanafi view but does not establish the Shafi'i view, as there is a third opinion (Maliki) requiring wiping the entire head.

The third example concerns a suspended sale (bay' mawquf): the Shafi'i argues it is invalid because it involves a contract on behalf of another without authorization, analogous to buying without permission. The Hanafi inverts: it is not invalid because lack of permission does not automatically invalidate, as in the case of a purchase without ownership. The response from the Shafi'i is that the descriptions (lack of guardianship and deputyship) actually entail invalidity, so they cannot support validity.

The fourth example briefly mentions the sale of a fuduli (unauthorized agent) where the same pattern applies.

Throughout, the passage emphasizes that qalb is a dialectical tool to expose inconsistency in the proponent's reasoning, and it requires careful response to demonstrate why the inversion does not truly refute the original argument.

Key Terms
القلب
المعارضة
العلة
الأصل

Key Takeaway
Qalb is a powerful dialectical technique that turns the opponent's own reasoning against him by using the same 'illa and asl to derive an opposite ruling.

The Mechanism of Qalb (Inversion)
First part of the passage with examples of qalb.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the qalb technique in a jadal exchange and explain how it uses the opponent's own attributes to produce a contradictory ruling.

Core Idea
Qalb turns the opponent's own reasoning against him by applying his attributes to produce a counter-conclusion.

In this passage, the author illustrates qalb through several examples from fiqh debates. Qalb is a powerful tool in jadal because it does not introduce new premises; it uses the opponent's own descriptions ('illa) to derive a contradictory ruling, revealing inconsistency. This lesson focuses on understanding how the mu'tarid performs the inversion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mustadil's original analogy
Find the original argument in the passage. For instance, the Hanafi argues that wiping a quarter of the head suffices by analogy with the face. Note the attributes (awsaf) used: 'wiping the head is a pillar of wudu' and 'the least that the name applies to is not sufficient'.
2
Extract the key attributes or cause
The mustadil's description (wasf) is the basis of his analogy. In the wiping example, the attribute is that wiping the head is a pillar, so the minimal amount is insufficient.
3
See how the mu'tarid applies the same attributes
The Shafi'i takes the same attribute ('wiping the head is a pillar') and applies it to argue that a quarter is not sufficient. This inverts the conclusion without adding new premises.
4
Evaluate the effect of the inversion
The qalb shows that the original analogy leads to a contradictory ruling, thus invalidating the mustadil's position. Note that the Shafi'i does not assert a positive ruling; he only shows inconsistency.

دليله، وذلك أن يقول : إن قولك لا ينقل الخيار إلى الوارث»، يقتضي أن يكون ثم وارث، ولا يصح وارث مع الجنون والإغماء، وأيضًا فإنه لا يمتنع أن لا يبطل الخيار بالجنون والموت ويستويان في ذلك وان كان الموت ينفرد بنقل ذلك إلى الورثة، ألا ترى أن خيار الرد بالعيب قد استوى بالجنون والموت وأنه لا يبطل ذلك فإن الموت ينقله إلى الورثة دون الجنون بهما، ومع : ومثال ذلك أيضًا أن يقول الحنفي في الاستدلال على وجوب مسح ربع الرأس في الوضوء مسح الرأس ركن في الوضوء، فلا يكفي فيه أقل ما يطلق عليه الاسم، كالوجه. : فيقول له الشافعي معترضا مسح الرأس ركن في الوضوء فلا يكفي فيه الربع، قياسًا على الوجه فقياس الشافعي أنتج أن مسح الرأس لا يكفي فيه الربع، وهذا خلاف مذهب الحنفي، فكان هذا القياس مبطلا لمذهب الحنفي صراحة مع كونه لم يثبت مذهب الشافعي لوجود مذهب ثالث وهو مسح الجميع كما يقول الإمام مالك رضي الله عنه ومن ذلك كذلك : أن يقول الشافعي في البيع الموقوف : إنه باطل، لأنه عقد في حق الغير من غير ولاية ولا استنابة، فلم يعقد في حق ذلك الغير، كالشراء. فيقول الحنفي : أقلب فأقول : فلم يبطل لعدم الإذن، كالشراء. والجواب بالنسبة للمستدل أن يقول : هذه الأوصاف لا تؤثر في حكم القلب لأنك لو قلت «عقد فلم يبطل لعدم الإذن، لم ينتقض بشيء .. ثم إن هذه الأوصاف تقتضي إبطال العقد، فلا يجوز أن يعلق عليها الصحة أمثلته أيضًا : أن يستدل شافعي أو حنبلي في بيع الفضولي أنه باطل، لأنه عقد على ملك العين بغير ولاية ولا نيابة فلم ينعقد، كما ساغ ملكه لغيره أو لنفسه اتفاقا منه على إذنه بأن يقول : ابتعت عبد زيد هذا بمائة، فيقول الحنفي معترضا : أقلب فأقول : فلم يبطل لعدم الإذن، كالشراء. والجواب أن يقول المستدل : هذه الأوصاف لا تؤثر في حكم القلب، فإنك لو قلت «عقد فلم يبطل لعدم الإذن»، لم ينتقض عليك بشيء.. ثم إن هذه الأوصاف تقتضي إبطال العقد، فلا يجوز أن يعلق عليها الصحة، لأن العلل لا يعلق عليه ضد مقتضاها
His proof: that he says: 'Your statement that the option is not transferred to the heir' necessitates that there be an heir, and an heir is not valid with insanity and unconsciousness. Also, it is not impossible that the option is not nullified by insanity and death, and they are equal in that, even if death alone transfers it to the heirs. Do you not see that the option of rejection due to a defect is equal with insanity and death, and that is not nullified, for death transfers it to the heirs without insanity. And with them both: And an example of that also is that the Hanafi says in inferring the obligation of wiping a quarter of the head in ablution: 'Wiping the head is a pillar of ablution, so the least that the name applies to is not sufficient, like the face.' So the Shafi'i says to him objecting: 'Wiping the head is a pillar of ablution, so a quarter is not sufficient, by analogy with the face.' So the Shafi'i's analogy produced that wiping the head is not sufficient with a quarter, and this contradicts the Hanafi's doctrine. So this analogy clearly invalidated the Hanafi's doctrine, even though it did not establish the Shafi'i's doctrine due to the existence of a third doctrine, which is wiping all of it, as Imam Malik (may Allah be pleased with him) says. And from that also: that the Shafi'i says about a suspended sale: 'It is invalid, because it is a contract concerning the right of another without guardianship or deputization, so it is not concluded concerning that other's right, like a purchase.' So the Hanafi says: 'I invert and say: so it is not invalidated due to lack of permission, like a purchase.' And the answer for the arguer is to say: 'These descriptions do not affect the ruling of inversion, because if you said "a contract so it is not invalidated due to lack of permission," nothing would refute you... Then these descriptions require the invalidation of the contract, so it is not permissible to attach validity to them.' Also among its examples: that a Shafi'i or Hanbali argues in the case of an unauthorized sale that it is invalid, because it is a contract on the ownership of the eye (specific property) without guardianship or deputization, so it is not concluded, just as it is permissible for his ownership to be for another or for himself, with his agreement on his permission, by saying: 'I bought this slave of Zaid for one hundred.' So the Hanafi objects and says: 'I invert and say: so it is not invalidated due to lack of permission, like a purchase.' And the answer is that the arguer says: 'These descriptions do not affect the ruling of inversion, for if you said "a contract so it is not invalidated due to lack of permission," nothing would refute you... Then these descriptions require the invalidation of the contract, so it is not permissible to attach validity to them, because the causes (illal) are not attached to what contradicts their requirement.'
Source pages p. 447

Commentary
This passage provides multiple examples of the dialectical technique of qalb (inversion) in juristic debate (jadal). The author first gives an example involving the transfer of an option (khiyar) upon death versus insanity. The arguer (mustadil) uses a proof that the opponent's statement implies an heir, but no valid heir exists with insanity. Then an example about wiping the head in ablution: the Hanafi argues that wiping a quarter of the head is obligatory by analogy with the face, but the Shafi'i inverts by saying that a quarter is not sufficient, also by analogy with the face, thus invalidating the Hanafi's position without establishing his own because a third position (wiping all) exists. Next, an example about a suspended sale: the Shafi'i argues it is invalid because it is a contract concerning another's right without authorization, analogizing to a purchase. The Hanafi inverts by saying that by the same logic, it would not be invalid due to lack of permission, like a purchase. The arguer responds that these descriptions (being a contract, etc.) do not affect the inversion ruling because the same description would not be refuted, and the descriptions actually require invalidation, so validity cannot be attached. Finally, an example about unauthorized sale (bai' al-fuduli): a Shafi'i or Hanbali argues it is invalid because it is a contract on another's property without guardianship, analogous to a purchase. The Hanafi inverts similarly. The arguer's response is identical: the descriptions do not affect the inversion, and attaching validity would contradict the requirement of the cause ('illa). Throughout, the author shows how qalb is used to expose inconsistency or to block the opponent's analogy by reversing the reasoning.

Key Terms
قلب (qalb): In this context, a dialectical technique where the opponent (mu'tarid) takes the same attributes (awsaf) or cause ('illa) used by the arguer and applies them to produce a contradictory ruling, thus invalidating the original argument. [Note: In later uṣūl works, qalb is often systematized as a form of mu‘āraḍa (counter-argument) specifically in qiyās.]
علة ('illa): The effective cause or legal rationale that is used as the basis for analogy (qiyās). In these examples, the 'illa is derived from the description (wasf) such as 'contract concerning another' or 'lack of permission'. [Note: The concept of 'illa is central to uṣūl al-fiqh; in jadal texts, it is used more loosely as the point of contention in the inversion.]
مستدل (mustadill): The arguer who presents the original proof or analogy. He attempts to establish a ruling for the branch (far') by analogy with the original (aṣl).
معترض (mu'tariḍ): The objector or opponent who challenges the original argument, often by performing qalb (inversion) to show inconsistency.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example of wiping the head, what attribute does the Shafi'i use in his qalb?
A) Wiping the head is a pillar of wudu
B) The least that the name applies to is sufficient
C) The face analogy proves wiping a quarter
D) The obligation of wiping a quarter is derived from the face

Key Takeaway
Qalb exposes inconsistency by applying the opponent's own attributes to derive a contradictory conclusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Using the example of the suspended sale (buyu' al-mawquf) from the passage, identify the mustadil's original attributes (being a contract concerning another's right without authorization) and show how the mu'tarid applies the same attributes to achieve a contradictory ruling (that it is not invalid due to lack of permission).

Defending Against Qalb: The Mustadil's Response
Second part of the passage focusing on the mustadil's response to qalb.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the mustadil's standard response to a qalb objection, specifically the argument that the descriptions do not affect the inversion ruling and that the 'illa cannot be attached to a contradictory ruling.

Core Idea
The mustadil defends his analogy by asserting that his attributes inherently require his ruling, and qalb does not produce a valid counter-analogy.

This lesson examines how the mustadil (original arguer) responds when his opponent performs qalb. In the passage, the mustadil consistently replies that the descriptions (awsaf) do not affect the ruling of inversion, and that the 'illa (cause) cannot be attached to a ruling that contradicts its inherent requirement. Understanding this response is crucial for both deploying and countering qalb.

Guided Path
1
Read the mustadil's original argument and the mu'tarid's qalb
Focus on the examples of suspended sale and unauthorized sale. Note the mustadil's description (e.g., 'contract concerning another's right without guardianship') and the mu'tarid's inversion using the same description to claim the contract is not invalid.
2
Identify the mustadil's counter-claim
The mustadil says: 'These descriptions do not affect the ruling of inversion.' This means that even if the same descriptions are used, they do not lead to a valid contradiction because the descriptions inherently require invalidation.
3
Analyze the reasoning behind the response
The mustadil argues that if you said 'a contract so it is not invalidated due to lack of permission,' nothing would refute that. This implies that the qalb does not produce a necessary contradiction; it is just a statement without refutational force.
4
Understand the final principle
The mustadil states that descriptions requiring invalidation cannot be attached to validity, because 'the causes (illal) are not attached to what contradicts their requirement.' This is a principle of jadal: the 'illa dictates a specific ruling, and cannot be used for the opposite.

Commentary
