Qalb al-Taswiya: Forcing Consistency in Dialectical Analogy
Author defines qalb al-taswiya and provides two examples from fiqh.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the structure of qalb al-taswiya in a jadal exchange and apply it to a new fiqh example.

Core Idea
Qalb al-taswiya uses the opponent's own premise to force negation of his disputed ruling.

In dialectical reasoning, sometimes the most effective refutation is to demand that the opponent apply his own logic consistently. This is qalb al-taswiya (inversion of equivalence), a sophisticated tool from the jadal tradition. By examining its structure and two classical fiqh examples, you will learn how to turn the opponent's analogy against him.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings in the original (aṣl)
Locate the two rulings (ḥukm) that apply to the original case: one that is agreed to be negated for the branch (farʿ), and another that is disputed between the opponents.
These rulings must be present in the original and must both be relevant to the branch.
2
Determine the arguer's claim
The arguer (mustadill) tries to prove the disputed ruling for the branch using qiyās (analogy) from the original. Note which ruling he wants to affirm.
3
Understand the objector's demand for equivalence
The objector (muʿāriḍ) demands that the two rulings be treated equally (taswiya) in the branch, just as they are in the original. This forces consistency.
The objector does not need to introduce new evidence; he uses the opponent's own qiyās structure.
4
Apply the agreed-upon negation
Since both parties already agree that the first ruling is negated for the branch, demanding equivalence forces the negation of the second ruling as well. The objector's conclusion contradicts the arguer's claim.
This implicit invalidation (butlān) is the heart of qalb al-taswiya.
5
Trace the move in the fiqh examples
Walk through the two examples: the sale of unspecified goods (Hanafi vs. Maliki) and divorce under duress (Hanafi vs. Shafiʿi). Identify each element: aṣl, farʿ, the two rulings, the disputed ruling, and the objector's demand for equivalence.
Practice on the divorce example: both agree acknowledgment under duress is invalid (first ruling). The Shafiʿi demands equivalence between acknowledgment and the divorce itself, so the divorce must also be invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ب - قلب التسوية : وهو قياس يكون الأصل فيه مشتملا على حكمين أحدهما منفي عن الفرع اتفاقا، وثانيهما مختلف بين الخصمين على ثبوته ،له فالمستدل يريد أن يثبت أن هذا الحكم المختلف فيه للفرع بالقياس على الأصل، فيقول له المعترض: يجب التسوية بين الحكمين في الفرع كما سُوِّي بينهما في الأصل، ولكن التسوية بين الحكمين في الفرع يلزمها عدم ثبوت الحكم الثاني فيه ضرورة أنهما متفقان على نفي الحكم الأول عنه، وبذلك يبطل مذهب المعترض ضمنا الوصية . بيع مثاله : أن يستدل الحنفي على أن الأعيان المجهولة الصفة عند المشتري جائز، لأنه عنده يقتضي لتمليك العين، فوجب ألا يفسد بجهالة العين المملكة، كالوصية، فيقول المالكي : أقلب هذه العلة فأقول : إن هذا عندي يقتضي تمليك العين، فوجب أن يستوي فيه عدم الوصف وعدم التعين قياسًا على أصل هو ومثاله كذلك : أن يقول الحنفي في الاستدلال على أن طلاق المكره يقع عليه : المكره مكلف مالك للعصمة فيقع عليه الطلاق كالمختار، فيقول له الشافعي معترضا المكره مكلف مالك للعصمة فيسوى بين إيقاع الطلاق منه وإقراره به كالمختار ؛ فكل من الحنفي والشافعي يقول : إن المكره لا يؤاخذ بإقراره بالطلاق لسلب الاختيار عنه ولكنهما مختلفان في إيقاع الطلاق منه، فالحنفي يرى إيقاعه عليه، والشافعي لا يرى ذلك بل يقول بعدم إيقاعه عليه
B - Qalb al-Taswiya (Inversion of Equivalence): It is an analogy in which the original (aṣl) contains two rulings, one of which is agreed to be negated for the branch (far‘), and the other is disputed between the two opponents as to its affirmation. So the arguer wants to prove that this disputed ruling applies to the branch by analogy with the original. So the objector says to him: 'Equivalence (taswiya) between the two rulings must be observed in the branch, just as equivalence was observed in the original. But the equivalence between the two rulings in the branch necessitates the non-affirmation of the second ruling in it, inevitably because they agree on the negation of the first ruling from it. And thereby the doctrine of the objector is implicitly invalidated.' A bequest (wasiyya). A sale. Its example: that the Hanafi argues that unspecified tangible assets (a‘yān majhūla al-ṣifa) for the buyer are permissible, because, in his view, it necessitates the transfer of ownership of the eye, so it must not be invalidated by ignorance of the specific eye being transferred, like bequest. So the Maliki says: 'I invert this cause and say: This, in my view, necessitates the transfer of ownership of the eye, so it must be that the lack of description and lack of specification are equal in it, by analogy with an original.' And its example also: that the Hanafi argues in inferring that the divorce of a coerced person takes effect: 'The coerced person is a legally responsible person (mukallaf) who possesses the marital bond (iṣma), so divorce takes effect from him, like the voluntary person.' So the Shafi'i objects and says: 'The coerced person is a legally responsible person who possesses the marital bond, so he is equal between effecting divorce from him and his acknowledgment of it (iqrār), like the voluntary person.' So both the Hanafi and the Shafi'i say that the coerced person is not held accountable for his acknowledgment of divorce due to his lack of free choice, but they differ regarding the effect of divorce from him. The Hanafi sees that it takes effect, while the Shafi'i does not see that and instead says it does not take effect.
Source pages p. 448

Commentary
This passage defines a specific type of qalb called qalb al-taswiya (inversion of equivalence). The scenario is a qiyās where the original (aṣl) has two rulings: one that both parties agree is negated for the branch (far‘), and another that is disputed. The arguer wants to prove the disputed ruling for the branch. The objector (mu‘tariḍ) demands that the two rulings be treated equally in the branch, as they are in the original. But since both agree to negate the first ruling for the branch, demanding equality forces the negation of the second ruling as well, thereby defeating the arguer's own claim. The author then gives two examples. First: the Hanafi argues that selling unspecified goods (a‘yān majhūla al-ṣifa) is permissible because the contract transfers ownership, and ignorance of the specific item does not invalidate it, analogizing to a bequest (wasiyya) where the bequeathed item may be unspecified. The Maliki objector inverts by saying that if the cause is the transfer of ownership, then according to the same principle, the lack of description and lack of specification should be treated equally, but they agree that lack of specification in bequest is problematic? Actually the example is a bit condensed: the Maliki says the same cause requires equality between the two types of ignorance, and since both agree that ignorance of the specific bequeathed item is not allowed? The text says 'like bequest' and the Maliki inverts by saying equality is required, leading to the Hanafi's own argument being invalidated. Second example: the Hanafi argues that divorce under duress takes effect because the coerced person is still legally responsible, like a voluntary person. The Shafi'i objects by demanding equality between the effect of divorce and the acknowledgment of divorce. Both agree that acknowledgment under duress is not valid. Therefore, by requiring equality, the Hanafi's claim that divorce takes effect is undermined. This type of qalb is a sophisticated dialectical tool that forces consistency.

Key Terms
قلب التسوية
تسوية
عارض

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) That divorce under duress takes effect
B) That acknowledgment of divorce under duress is not valid
C) That the coerced person is not a mukallaf
D) That the coerced person loses his free choice completely

Key Takeaway
Qalb al-taswiya is a dialectical move that forces consistency by requiring the opponent to treat two rulings equally, thereby undermining his own conclusion.

Qalb al-Taswiya: A Dialectical Objection in Analogical Argument
Definition and example of qalb al-taswiya with sale analogy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the structure of a qalb al-taswiya objection in a jadal exchange and reconstruct the objector's demand for equalization.

Core Idea
Qalb al-taswiya forces consistency: if two rulings are equal in the source, they must be equal in the target.

In dialectical argumentation (jadal), the objector has several tools to challenge an analogy. One of the most powerful is qalb al-taswiya (inversion of equalization). This lesson walks through its definition and a concrete example from the Hanafi-Maliki debate on the validity of selling unknown items. By understanding this objection, you will see how jadal sharpens legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings in the original case
Locate the two rulings that the author mentions: one is agreed to be negated from the branch, and the other is disputed. In the example, the bequest (wasiyya) has both lack of description and indeterminacy; the agreed negated ruling is that the bequest is not fully specified (indeterminacy), but the Hanafi claims it is valid despite that.
The first ruling is negated from the branch (sale) by agreement; the second (validity) is disputed.
2
Spot the proponent's claim
The proponent uses analogy to affirm the disputed ruling for the branch. The Hanafi argues that sale of an unknown item is valid because sale transfers ownership, just as a bequest transfers ownership even when the item is not fully described.
The proponent's analogy relies on a shared attribute (ownership transfer) while ignoring others (lack of description vs. indeterminacy).
3
Reconstruct the objector's demand for equalization
The objector insists that the branch must treat both rulings equally, just as they are treated in the original. Since both parties agree that the first ruling (e.g., indeterminacy) is negated from the branch, the second ruling (validity) must also be negated to maintain consistency.
This demand is the core of qalb al-taswiya: equal treatment forces the negation of the disputed ruling.
4
Trace the implication
The objector's demand leads to the conclusion that the disputed ruling cannot hold in the branch, because equalization would contradict the agreed negation. This refutes the proponent's claim without directly attacking the analogy's premise.
The proponent then tries to show that the attributes are not comparable, but the objection has already shifted the burden.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ب - قلب التسوية : وهو قياس يكون الأصل فيه مشتملا على حكمين أحدهما منفي عن الفرع اتفاقا، وثانيهما مختلف بين الخصمين على ثبوته ،له فالمستدل يريد أن يثبت أن هذا الحكم المختلف فيه للفرع بالقياس على الأصل، فيقول له المعترض: يجب التسوية بين الحكمين في الفرع كما سُوِّي بينهما في الأصل، ولكن التسوية بين الحكمين في الفرع يلزمها عدم ثبوت الحكم الثاني فيه ضرورة أنهما متفقان على نفي الحكم الأول عنه، وبذلك يبطل مذهب المعترض ضمنا. مثاله : أن يستدل الحنفي على أن الأعيان المجهولة الصفة عند المشتري جائز، لأنه عنده يقتضي لتمليك العين، فوجب ألا يفسد بجهالة العين المملكة، كالوصية، فيقول المالكي : أقلب هذه العلة فأقول : إن هذا عندي يقتضي تمليك العين، فوجب أن يستوي فيه عدم الوصف وعدم التعين قياسًا على أصل هو ومثاله كذلك. والجواب أن يقول المستدل : هذه الأوصاف لا تؤثر في حكم القلب، فإنك لو قلت «عقد فلم يبطل لعدم الإذن»، لم ينتقض عليك بشيء.. ثم إن هذه الأوصاف تقتضي إبطال العقد، فلا يجوز أن يعلق عليها الصحة، لأن العلل لا يعلق عليه ضد مقتضاها.
B - Qalb al-Taswiya (Inversion of Equalization): It is an analogy in which the principal case contains two rulings, one of which is agreed to be negated from the branch, and the second is disputed between the two opponents regarding its affirmation for it [the branch]. So the proponent wants to prove that this disputed ruling applies to the branch by analogy to the principal. Then the objector says to him: Equalization between the two rulings in the branch is necessary just as they were equalized in the principal. But equalization between the two rulings in the branch necessitates the non-affirmation of the second ruling in it, necessarily because they agree on the negation of the first ruling from it, and thereby the doctrine of the objector is implicitly invalidated. Example: The Hanafi argues that unknown items (not described) in a sale are permissible because a sale necessitates ownership of the item, so it should not be invalidated by ignorance of the owned item, like a bequest. Then the Maliki says: I invert this cause and say: This according to me necessitates ownership of the item, so ignorance of description and indeterminacy should be equalized in it by analogy to a principal that is like this as well. The answer is that the proponent says: These attributes have no effect on the ruling of the inversion, because if you said 'a contract that is not invalidated without permission' it would not be refuted against you by anything... Furthermore, these attributes necessitate the invalidation of the contract, so validity cannot be attached to them, because causes are not attached to something opposite to their requirement.
Source pages p. 448

Commentary
This passage defines and illustrates 'qalb al-taswiya' (inversion of equalization), a specific type of objection (i'tirad) in dialectical argumentation (jadal). The author first gives a clear definition: it is an analogy where the original case (asl) has two rulings—one that both parties agree is negated from the branch (far'), and another that is in dispute. The proponent wants to prove the disputed ruling for the branch via analogy. The objector then demands consistency: just as the two rulings are equalized in the original (i.e., both apply or both are negated together), they must be equalized in the branch. Since both agree that the first ruling is negated from the branch, equalization forces the negation of the second ruling as well, thus refuting the proponent's claim. The author then provides a concrete example from fiqh: the Hanafi argues that selling an item with unknown description (e.g., without specifying the exact object) is valid, analogizing to a bequest (wasiyya) which is valid even if the bequeathed item is not fully specified. The Maliki objector performs qalb by pointing out that in the original (bequest), the owner may not know the exact item, but also the bequest is not fully specified—so the two attributes (lack of description and lack of determination) are equalized. By analogy, in the sale, both attributes should be considered equally, but since the Hanafi presumably does not allow such indeterminacy in sale, the analogy fails. The proponent's answer (jawab) is then provided: these attributes (like lack of description) do not affect the core ruling of the qalb, because the objector's own logic would lead to absurdities (e.g., if you said a contract is not invalidated by lack of permission, it would not be refuted by anything). Moreover, these attributes actually necessitate invalidity, so they cannot be used to justify validity. This commentary explains each step: definition → illustration → objection → answer, all based solely on the Arabic text.

Key Terms
قلب التسوية
مستدل
معترض
علة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) That they are both negated.
C) That they are treated equally as in the original case.
D) That the disputed ruling is affirmed by another analogy.

Key Takeaway
Qalb al-taswiya forces the proponent to confront the logical consequences of his analogy by demanding consistent treatment of all analogous attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Write the objector's demand for equalization.

Jadal on Divorce under Coercion
First jadal example: Hanafi vs Shafi'i on coerced divorce.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze a jadal exchange by identifying the claimant's analogy and the opponent's counter-analogy (qalb) in the Hanafi-Shafi'i dispute on divorce under coercion.

Core Idea
In jadal, the opponent can invert your analogy to defeat your argument.

This lesson dissects the first jadal example from the chapter: a Hanafi argues by qiyas that a coerced divorce is valid like a free person's. The Shafi'i counters by turning the analogy against him. Let's walk through the logical structure step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi's claim and analogy
The Hanafi claims the coerced's divorce is valid. He uses qiyas: the coerced (فَرْع) is like the free (أَصْل) because both are responsible agents (مكلف مالك للعصمة). Thus, the ruling (validity of divorce) transfers.
The effective cause ('illa) is responsibility and capacity for infallibility.
2
Extract the Shafi'i's objection
The Shafi'i does not directly attack the 'illa. Instead, he argues that the coerced's confession of divorce is not considered valid. Since the Hanafi himself would treat the coerced's confession as invalid, consistency requires treating his pronouncing of divorce as invalid too. This is a qalb (inversion) using the Hanafi's own reasoning against him.
3
Compare the two analogies
The Hanafi's analogy: coerced ≈ free (both responsible). The Shafi'i's analogy: coerced's divorce ≈ coerced's confession (both actions of coerced). The Shafi'i shows that the coerced's action is not treated uniformly by the Hanafi, breaking the original analogy.
4
Assess the debate outcome
The Shafi'i's response, if accepted, invalidates the Hanafi's claim because the Hanafi cannot consistently uphold his analogy without contradicting his treatment of confession. The debate ends with the Shafi'i's objection standing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحنفي يستدل على صحة دعواه بقياس المكره على المختار بجامع أن كلا منهما مكلف مالك للعصمة، والشافعي يخالفه في ذلك ويقول : يجب أن يُسَوَّى بين إيقاع الطلاق من المكره وإقراره به كما سَوَّى بينهما في المختار، وبما أن إقرار المكره بالطلاق لا يعتبر فيكون إيقاع الطلاق منه غير معتبر كذلك، وبذلك لا يتم للحنفي ما ادعاه

ومن أمثلته أيضًا أن يقول الحنفي في النية في الوضوء : إنه طهارة بمائع، فلم تفتقر إلى النية ، كإزالة النجاسة . فيقول الشافعي : أقلب فأقول : فاستوى مائعها وجامدها في النية، كإزالة النجاسة والجواب عنه أن يقول المستدل : لا تجوز التسوية بين المائع والجامد في النية، لأن عندك لا يستويان ألا ترى أن في التيمم يجب تعيين نية الفرض وفي الوضوء لا يجب؟
The Hanafi argues for the validity of his claim by analogy of the coerced to the free, by the commonality that each of them is a responsible person possessing [the capacity for] infallibility. The Shafi'i disagrees with him on that and says: It is necessary to equate between the occurrence of divorce from the coerced and his confession of it, just as he equated between them in the case of the free. And since the confession of the coerced to divorce is not considered, so the occurrence of divorce from him is also not considered. Thus, the Hanafi's claim does not hold.

Another example: The Hanafi says regarding intention in ablution: It is purification with a liquid, so it does not need intention, like removing impurity. The Shafi'i says: I will invert and say: So its liquid and solid are equal in requiring intention, like removing impurity. The response to it is that the arguer says: Equating between liquid and solid in requiring intention is not permissible, because according to you they are not equal. Do you not see that in tayammum, specifying the intention of the obligatory [act] is required, while in ablution it is not required?
Source pages p. 449

Commentary
This passage presents two examples of jadal (dialectical exchange) between Hanafi and Shafi'i jurists. The first example deals with divorce under coercion. The Hanafi argues by qiyas: a coerced person is like a free one because both are responsible agents (mukallaf) with infallibility. Therefore, if a free person's divorce is valid, so is the coerced's. The Shafi'i counters by distinguishing between the coerced's action and his confession: since his confession is not considered valid, his divorce should also not be considered valid. Thus, the Shafi'i uses a qiyas of equivalence between the two cases of the coerced, breaking the Hanafi's analogy.

The second example concerns intention (niyya) in ablution (wudu). The Hanafi argues that ablution is purification with liquid, so it does not require intention, analogizing it to removing impurity (which also uses liquid and does not require intention). The Shafi'i employs a "qalb" (inversion) argument: he turns the analogy around and says that if liquid and solid are equal in removing impurity (both requiring intention? Actually the text says 'فاستوى مائعها وجامدها في النية' which seems to mean that both liquid and solid are equal in requiring intention, using the same analogy of removing impurity. But this is a counter-analogy. The Hanafi then defends by pointing out that the Shafi'i himself does not treat liquid and solid equally in all cases: in tayammum, intention is required for the solid (dry ablution), while in wudu it is not. Thus, the Shafi'i's inconsistency undermines his response. This exchange illustrates the use of qiyas, qalb, and the importance of consistency in legal analogy.

Key Terms
القياس
المكره
القلب

C) The coerced and the free are not analogous because the coerced lacks intention.

Key Takeaway

Jadal on Intention in Ablution
Second jadal example: Hanafi vs Shafi'i on intention in wudu.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and evaluate the technique of qalb (inversion) in a jadal exchange, using the Hanafi-Shafi'i dispute on intention in wudu as a case study.

Core Idea
Qalb turns the opponent's analogy against him by applying it to a different case, exposing inconsistency.

The second jadal example concerns intention (niyya) in wudu. The Hanafi argues that wudu, being purification with a liquid, does not require intention, like removing impurity. The Shafi'i inverts the analogy to claim that both liquid and solid forms of purification require intention. Let's see how the debate unfolds.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi's qiyas
The Hanafi argues: wudu is purification with a liquid (water), so it does not need intention, just as removing impurity (istinja/istijmar) does not require intention. The original case (asl) is removing impurity; the new case (far') is wudu; the commonality is being purification with a liquid.
The 'illa is being a liquid-based purification.
2
Analyze the Shafi'i's qalb (inversion)
The Shafi'i responds by inverting: 'I will invert and say: So its liquid and solid are equal in requiring intention, like removing impurity.' He takes the Hanafi's own class of 'purification' and applies it to both liquid (wudu) and solid (tayammum) forms. Since tayammum requires intention, the Shafi'i claims that wudu should also require it. This turns the Hanafi's analogy on its head.
The Shafi'i's qalb is not a direct refutation but a counter-analogy.
3
Examine the Hanafi's response
The Hanafi defends by pointing out inconsistency: 'You (Shafi'i) do not treat liquid and solid equally in all cases. In tayammum, specifying the intention of the obligation is required, while in wudu it is not required.' Thus, the Shafi'i's own position shows that the two are not equal, so his qalb fails.
The Hanafi's jawab (response) attacks the Shafi'i's premise.
4
Determine the debate outcome
The Hanafi successfully exposes an inconsistency in the Shafi'i's position: the Shafi'i himself distinguishes between wudu and tayammum regarding intention. Therefore, the Shafi'i's qalb does not hold, and the Hanafi's original analogy remains intact.

Source Text

The Objection of Corruption of Placement (Fasād al-Waḍ‘): Definition and Rationale
Defines the fasād al-waḍ‘ objection and its underlying rationale.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the logical reasoning behind the fasād al-waḍ‘ objection and identify when a proposed cause contradicts its intended effect.

Core Idea
A cause that demands the opposite of its ruling is corrupt in placement because it violates the inherent suitability between cause and effect.

In Islamic legal dialectic, a valid cause (illah) must be consistent with the ruling it supports. But sometimes an opponent can show that the cause actually requires the opposite ruling. This is the fasād al-waḍ‘ objection. This lesson unpacks the wisdom analogy underlying the objection: just as placing an object in an unsuitable location is irrational, so too is attaching a ruling to a cause that demands its opposite.

Guided Path
1
Identify the coextensive cause
The passage first defines a coextensive cause as one that accompanies the ruling in existence and non-existence. This is the baseline for the objection.
2
Understand the analogy of placement
The author explains that 'placing a thing in a locus' (waḍ‘) implies suitability. If the locus does not suit the thing, the placement is contrary to wisdom and thus corrupt.
3
Apply the analogy to causation
Just as a physical object must suit its place, a cause must suit its effect (the ruling). The cause's natural exigency (muqtaḍā) must align with the ruling.
4
Define the objection
Therefore, if a cause demands the opposite of the claimed ruling, it is a corrupt placement (fasād al-waḍ‘). This forms the logical basis for the objection.

The Fourth Topic: Objections to the Coextensive Cause (al-‘illa al-ṭardiyya). By coextensive cause we mean its coextensiveness with the ruling in existence and non-existence, i.e. wherever the cause exists, the ruling exists. The objections to this type of cause are of various types, detailed as follows: The First Sub-topic: The Objection to the Cause by Corruption of Placement (fasād al-waḍ‘). It is called that the objector shows that the ruling attached to the cause is such that the cause demands its opposite. That is because placing a thing is putting it in a locus with a certain form or quality. If that locus or that quality is not suitable for it, then its placement is contrary to wisdom, and whatever is contrary to wisdom is corrupt. So we say here that if the cause demands the opposite of the claimed ruling or its contrary, that is contrary to wisdom, for it is the nature of the cause to be suitable for its effect, not that it contradicts it. Thus it is corrupt in placement by this consideration. It occurs in two ways: A – That the cause is attached to the opposite of its exigency. This is known by two methods: A-1 – What is known by explicit text (al-nuṭq). For example, a Hanafi says regarding the impurity of the leftover water of beasts of prey: 'It is a beast of prey with fangs, so its leftover is impure, like the pig.' Then a Shafi‘i objects, saying: 'Being a beast of prey has been made in the Law a cause for purity. The evidence is what is related that the Prophet (peace be upon him) was invited to the house of some people and responded, and was invited to the house of others and did not respond. It was said: So-and-so invited you and you responded, and so-and-so invited you and you did not respond. He said: 'In the house of so-and-so there is a dog.' It was said: And in the house of so-and-so there is a cat. He said: 'The cat is a beast of prey,' thus making being a cat a beast of prey a cause for purity. So it is not permissible to make it a cause for impurity.' Another example: A Hanafi argues for the permissibility of selling fresh dates for dried dates equally at the time of contract, and that any deficiency that occurs eventually does not prevent the validity of the contract before it, because it loses weight due to drying, so it does not prevent the validity of the sale, like selling new dates for old. Then a Maliki says: 'You have attached to the cause the opposite of its exigency, because the deficiency due to drying was made by the Prophet (peace be upon him) a cause for prohibiting the sale when he said to the questioner: 'Do the fresh dates decrease when they dry?' They said: Yes. He said: 'Then do not.' And what the Lawgiver has made a cause for prohibition cannot be made a cause for permissibility.' The answer to this is that the arguer (must) discuss the report mentioned by the questioner and show that what he mentioned is a cause for the ruling; if he does not do so, his analogy is invalid. A-2 – What is known from the principles (al-uṣūl). For example, a Hanafi says regarding intentional killing: 'It is a meaning that necessitates execution, so it does not necessitate expiation, like apostasy.' Then a Shafi‘i objects: 'You have attached to the cause the opposite of its exigency, for being that which necessitates execution necessitates that it is a cause for severity, so it is not permissible to make it a cause for dropping expiation.' The answer: He shows that it necessitates only what it is attached to, because if it is severe by the obligation of execution, it is in no need of another severity. Another example: A Shafi‘i argues that one who intentionally breaks his fast in Ramadan by eating has no expiation, because he broke his fast by something that can validly occur from a single person, so expiation is not obligatory for him, like one who breaks his fast while traveling. Then a Maliki says: 'You have attached to the cause the opposite of its exigency, because breaking the fast in Ramadan, despite the severity of its sin, is not evidence for dropping expiation, but rather is evidence for severity of its ruling.' The answer: He shows that he has attached to it only what agrees with its exigency, if he finds a way to that.
Source pages p. 450

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical explanation of one type of objection against a purported legal cause (al-‘illa al-ṭardiyya), namely the objection of ‘corruption of placement’ (fasād al-waḍ‘). The author first defines the coextensive cause as a cause that accompanies the ruling in both existence and non-existence. Then he introduces the first objection: that the cause demands the opposite of the ruling attached to it. The reasoning is that placing a thing in an unsuitable context is irrational (against wisdom), so if the cause contradicts its effect, the placement is corrupt. This objection has two sub-types. The first sub-type (A) is when the cause is linked to the opposite of its exigency (ḍidd muqtaḍāha). This is recognized by two methods: (1) by explicit text (al-nuṭq) and (2) by principles (al-uṣūl). Under the first method, the author gives two examples from usul al-fiqh. In the first example, a Hanafi argues that wild animals with fangs cause impurity (like pigs). A Shafi‘i objects using a prophetic hadith where the Prophet treated a cat (a wild animal) as a cause for purity, thus the same attribute cannot be a cause for impurity. The second example involves the sale of fresh dates for dried dates: a Hanafi says that the drying process does not void the contract because it is a future deficiency, analogous to selling new dates for old. A Maliki objects that the Prophet made the drying process a cause for prohibition (in the hadith), so it cannot be a cause for permissibility. The answer (jawāb) to such objections is for the original arguer to examine the textual evidence and show that what the objector cited is actually a cause for the intended ruling; otherwise his analogy fails. The second method (A-2) is based on principles. In the first example, a Hanafi argues that intentional killing (like apostasy) does not require expiation because it already obligates execution. A Shafi‘i objects that execution indicates severity, so it should not drop expiation. The answer is that the cause only necessitates what it is attached to; the severity of execution suffices, so no further severity (expiation) is needed. In the second example, a Shafi‘i argues that breaking the fast intentionally in Ramadan does not require expiation because the act itself (eating) is possible even for one who is not traveling. A Maliki objects that the severity of the sin in Ramadan rather demands expiation. The answer is that the arguer must show that the cause only necessitates what agrees with it, if possible. The passage thus illustrates a standard dialectical technique in Islamic legal theory: objecting that the proposed cause, if accepted, would lead to an absurdity because its proper exigency is the opposite of the claimed ruling.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what makes a cause 'corrupt in placement'?
A) The cause does not exist in all cases.
B) The cause demands the opposite of the ruling.
C) The cause is not mentioned in scripture.
D) The cause is coextensive with the ruling.

Key Takeaway
Fasād al-waḍ‘ is a dialectical objection that invalidates a proposed cause when the cause intrinsically requires the opposite ruling.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Examine the following statement: 'Wine is forbidden because it intoxicates; likewise, every intoxicant is forbidden.' An objector says: 'Intoxication is the cause for flogging, not for prohibition.' Identify which type of objection is being made and write a short response as the original arguer.
?
In the debate about drying dates, the Mālikī objects that the Prophet made drying a cause for prohibition. Using the definition of fasād al-waḍ‘, explain why this objection fits the structure.

Dialectic -- Jadal
intermediate
10 min
The Two Methods of Recognizing Fasād al-Waḍ‘: By Text (al-Nuṭq) and by Principles (al-Uṣūl)
Explains the two methods of the fasād al-waḍ‘ objection with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify objections of fasād al-waḍ‘ into those based on explicit text and those based on legal principles, and construct a response for the original arguer.

Core Idea
The fasād al-waḍ‘ objection is proven either by explicit textual precedent or by the logical principles of the legal school.

After understanding the rationale of fasād al-waḍ‘, we now explore the two methods by which an objector can demonstrate that a cause demands the opposite of its ruling. The first method relies on explicit scriptural evidence (al-nuṭq), while the second relies on established legal principles (al-uṣūl). This lesson walks through the examples and the required response.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the two methods
The passage divides the objection into two sub-types: (A) when the cause is attached to the opposite of its exigency. This is known by two methods: via explicit text (al-nuṭq) and via principles (al-uṣūl).
2
Analyze the first method (al-nuṭq) examples
The first example: Hanafi claims beast of prey causes impurity; Shafi‘i objects citing hadith that the cat (a beast) was a cause for purity. Second example: Hanafi claims drying dates does not void sale; Maliki objects that the Prophet made drying a cause for prohibition.
In each case, the objector cites a textual precedent where the same attribute was assigned the opposite ruling.
3
Analyze the second method (al-uṣūl) examples
The first example: Hanafi claims intentional killing (like apostasy) does not require expiation; Shafi‘i objects that execution indicates severity, so it should not drop expiation. Second example: Shafi‘i claims breaking fast intentionally has no expiation; Maliki objects that the sin suggests severity, not leniency.
Here the objection is based on the principle that a cause for severity cannot also be a cause for leniency.
4
Understand the required answer (jawāb)
In both methods, the original arguer must respond by either showing that the cited text or principle actually supports his ruling, or that the objector misinterpreted the evidence. If he cannot, his analogy fails.
This preserves the dialectical structure: each objection expects a reply.

The Fourth Topic: Objections to the Coextensive Cause (al-‘illa al-ṭardiyya). By coextensive cause we mean its coextensiveness with the ruling in existence and non-existence, i.e. wherever the cause exists, the ruling exists. Commentary
This passage is a dialectical explanation of one type of objection against a purported legal cause (al-‘illa al-ṭardiyya), namely the objection of ‘corruption of placement’ (fasād al-waḍ‘). The author first defines the coextensive cause as a cause that accompanies the ruling in both existence and non-existence. Then he introduces the first objection: that the cause demands the opposite of the ruling attached to it. The reasoning is that placing a thing in an unsuitable context is irrational (against wisdom), so if the cause contradicts its effect, the placement is corrupt. This objection has two sub-types. The first sub-type (A) is when the cause is linked to the opposite of its exigency (ḍidd muqtaḍāha). This is recognized by two methods: (1) by explicit text (al-nuṭq) and (2) by principles (al-uṣūl). Under the first method, the author gives two examples from usul al-fiqh. In the first example, a Hanafi argues that wild animals with fangs cause impurity (like pigs). A Shafi‘i objects using a prophetic hadith where the Prophet treated a cat (a wild animal) as a cause for purity, thus the same attribute cannot be a cause for impurity. A Maliki objects that the Prophet made the drying process a cause for prohibition (in the hadith), so it cannot be a cause for permissibility. The passage thus illustrates a standard dialectical technique in Islamic legal theory: objecting that the proposed cause, if accepted, would lead to an absurdity because its proper exigency is the opposite of the claimed ruling.

Which method of recognizing fasād al-waḍ‘ does the Mālikī use when objecting to the Ḥanafī's argument that drying dates does not void the sale?
A) By analogy (qiyās)
B) By explicit text (al-nuṭq)
C) By principles (al-uṣūl)
D) By consensus (ijmā‘)

Key Takeaway
Fasād al-waḍ‘ can be established either by explicit scriptural texts or by legal principles, and each requires a specific response from the original proponent of the cause.

The Objection of Attaching Contrary to the 'Illa's Implication
Author explains the objection 'attaching contrary to the 'illa's implication' with three examples from Hanafi-Shafi'i debates.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when an opponent is making the objection 'attaching contrary to the 'illa's implication' and explain its logical structure.

Core Idea
The objection targets using an 'illa in a way that contradicts its original implication.

This lesson introduces a key dialectical objection in jadal: claiming that an opponent has used an 'illa in a manner opposite to its required implication. Understanding this objection helps you evaluate analogical arguments in fiqh and usul al-fiqh. We will look at the structure of the objection and how it appears in debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the 'illa in the argument
Locate the 'illa (effective cause) that the proponent uses to extend a ruling from an original case to a new case. The 'illa must have a clear implication (muqtada).
The implication is the ruling that the 'illa logically demands.
2
Determine the implied ruling of the 'illa
Based on how the 'illa is used in the original case (asl), deduce what ruling it implies. For example, if the 'illa is used to prohibit, its implication is prohibition.
3
Compare the proponent's use to the implication
Check whether the proponent applies the 'illa to yield a ruling that matches or contradicts its implication. If it contradicts, the objection 'attaching contrary' applies.
The objector claims the 'illa's implication is one thing, but the proponent uses it for the opposite.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
علة للطهارة، فلا يجوز أن يجعل علة للنجاسة. ومثاله أيضًا أن يستدل حنفي على جواز بيع الرطب بالتمر متساويا حال العقد، وأن ما يحدث من النقص في حال النهاية لا يمنع صحة العقد قبله، لأنه ينقص بحدث الجفوف فلم يمنع صحة البيع كبيع التمر الحديث بالقديم. فيقول مالكي: علقت على العلة ضد مقتضاها لأن النقص بالجفوف جعله النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم في منع البيع علة لما قال للسائل: "أينقص الرطب إذا جف؟" قالوا: نعم، قال: "فلا إذا". وما جعله صاحب الشرع علة في المنع، لا يجوز أن تجعله علة في الإباحة. والجواب عنه أن يتكلم المستدل على الخبر الذي ذكره السائل، ويبين أن ما ذكره علة للحكم، وإن لم يفعل كان قياسه غير صحيح.

أ-2- ما يعرف من جهة الأصول: ومثاله: أن يقول حنفي في قتل العمد: إنه معنى يوجب القتل فلا يوجب الكفارة كالردة. فيقول شافعي معترضا: علقت على العلة ضد المقتضى، فإن كونه موجبًا للقتل سبب التغليظ، فلا يجوز أن يجعل سببًا لإسقاط الكفارة. فالجواب أن يبين أنه لا يقتضي إلا ما علق عليه لأنه إذا تغلظ بوجوب القتل يستغني عن تغليظ آخر. ومثاله كذلك: أن يستدل شافعي على أن من أفطر في رمضان عامدًا بالأكل، فلا كفارة عليه، لأنه أفطر بما يصح وقوعه من الواحد، فلم تجب عليه كفارة، كما لو أفطر في السفر. فيقول له مالكي: علقت على العلة ضد مقتضاها لأن وقوع الفطر منه في رمضان، مع شدة مأثمه، لا يكون دليلا على سقوط الكفارة، وإنما يكون دليلا على تغليظ حكمه. والجواب أنه يبين لم يعلق عليها إلا ما وافق مقتضاها، إن وجد إلى ذلك سبيلا.
It is an 'illa for purity, so it is not permissible to make it an 'illa for impurity. Another example: A Hanafi argues for the permissibility of selling fresh dates for dried dates equal at the time of contract, and that the deficiency occurring at the end does not prevent the validity of the contract before it, because it decreases by the occurrence of drying, so it did not prevent the validity of the sale, like selling fresh dates for old dates. A Maliki says: You attached to the 'illa that which is contrary to its implication, because the deficiency by drying—the Prophet made it an 'illa for prohibiting the sale when he said to the questioner: 'Does the fresh date decrease when it dries?' They said: Yes. He said: 'Then no.' And what the Lawgiver made an 'illa for prohibition, it is not permissible to make it an 'illa for permissibility. The response to this is that the proponent should speak about the report mentioned by the questioner and clarify that what he mentioned is an 'illa for the ruling, and if he does not do so, his analogy is invalid.

A-2- What is known from the perspective of principles: Another example: A Hanafi says about deliberate killing: It is a meaning that necessitates killing, so it does not necessitate expiation, like apostasy. A Shafi'i objects: You attached to the 'illa that which is contrary to its implication, because its being a cause for killing is a reason for severity, so it is not permissible to make it a reason for dropping expiation. The response is that he should clarify that it necessitates only what it is attached to, because if it is severe by the obligation of killing, it is independent of another severity. Another example: A Shafi'i argues that whoever breaks the fast in Ramadan deliberately by eating has no expiation upon him, because he broke the fast by something that can occur from a single person, so expiation is not obligatory upon him, as if he broke the fast while traveling. A Maliki says to him: You attached to the 'illa that which is contrary to its implication, because the occurrence of breaking the fast by him in Ramadan, along with the severity of his sin, is not evidence for dropping expiation, but rather evidence for intensifying his ruling. The response is that he clarifies that he did not attach to it except what agrees with its implication, if there is a way to that.
Source pages p. 451

Commentary
This passage discusses a dialectical objection known as 'attaching to the 'illa that which is contrary to its implication' (علقت على العلة ضد مقتضاها). The author presents three examples from fiqh debates to illustrate this objection and its proper response. The objection is raised when an opponent claims that the 'illa used in an analogy, if consistently applied, would lead to a ruling opposite to the one intended, or that the 'illa has been used in a way that contradicts how the Lawgiver used it. The first example involves a Hanafi arguing for the permissibility of selling fresh dates for dried dates using the 'illa of deficiency by drying, which the Prophet actually used to prohibit such a sale. The Maliki objects that this 'illa cannot be used for permissibility because the Prophet used it for prohibition. The response requires the proponent to show that his use of the 'illa is consistent with its original context. The second example concerns deliberate killing and expiation; the Shafi'i argues that the 'illa of causing killing should lead to severity, not dropping expiation. The Hanafi response is that the 'illa only necessitates what it is attached to, and if killing is already severe, it does not need another severity. The third example is about breaking the fast in Ramadan; the Shafi'i uses the 'illa of it being an act that can occur from a single person, but the Maliki objects that the severity of the sin in Ramadan should intensify the ruling, not drop expiation. The response is that the proponent must demonstrate that his 'illa actually agrees with its implication. Throughout, the key point is that the objector must be answered by clarifying the true implication of the 'illa.

Key Terms
العلة
المقتضى
الجواب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the objection 'attaching contrary to the 'illa's implication', what is the objector claiming?
A) The 'illa is weak and irrelevant.
B) The 'illa's implication contradicts the ruling the proponent derives from it.
C) The 'illa has no implication at all.
D) The 'illa is not found in the original case.

Key Takeaway
The objection 'attaching contrary to the 'illa's implication' is a dialectical tool to expose inconsistency in analogical reasoning by comparing the 'illa's implied ruling with the proponent's conclusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Find a real fiqh debate where one party uses an 'illa that another claims contradicts its original implication. Identify the 'illa and explain the objection.
?
In the example of deliberate killing and expiation, state the 'illa used by the Hanafi and why the Shafi'i objects using the 'attaching contrary' objection.

The Dates Example: Hanafi vs. Maliki
Author explains the objection 'attaching contrary to the 'illa's implication' with three examples from Hanafi-Shafi'i debates.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the dialectical exchange in the dates example and evaluate the strength of the objection and response.

Core Idea
The same 'illa can be used for opposite rulings if its implication is misunderstood.

The first example in the passage involves a Hanafi arguing that selling fresh dates for dried dates is permissible because the deficiency occurring after the contract does not affect its validity, using the 'illa of 'deficiency by drying'. A Maliki objects that the Prophet used this 'illa to prohibit, so using it for permissibility is contradictory. Let's walk through this debate step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proponent's analogy
The Hanafi argues: Selling fresh dates for dried dates is permissible because the deficiency that occurs later (drying) does not invalidate the contract, similar to selling fresh dates for old dates where such deficiency is accepted.
The 'illa is 'deficiency occurring after the contract'.
2
Identify the objector's claim
The Maliki objects: The Prophet used the deficiency by drying as an 'illa to prohibit the sale in the hadith: 'Does it decrease when it dries?... Then no.' Therefore, using the same 'illa for permissibility is attaching a contrary implication.
3
Understand the objector's reasoning
The objector argues that an 'illa established by the Lawgiver for prohibition cannot be reversed for permission without a new justification.
4
Evaluate the response strategy
The author says the proponent must address the hadith and show that his use of the 'illa is consistent with its original context. If he cannot, his analogy is invalid.

Responding to the 'Attaching Contrary' Objection
Author explains the objection 'attaching contrary to the 'illa's implication' with three examples from Hanafi-Shafi'i debates.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to formulate a valid response (jawab) to the objection of attaching contrary to the 'illa's implication, using the examples provided.

Core Idea
A proper response clarifies that the 'illa's implication is correctly applied, often by showing it necessitates only what it is attached to.

The author provides responses for each example. This lesson focuses on the strategy behind the jawab. In the second example (deliberate killing), the Hanafi responses that the 'illa (necessitating killing) only necessitates that, not expiation, because severity is already satisfied. In the third example (breaking fast), the response is to show that the 'illa (occurring from a single person) actually agrees with its implication. Let's analyze these strategies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the structure of the objection
The objector says the 'illa's implication is X, but the proponent uses it to derive Y, which contradicts X. For example, in the killing case, the 'illa implies severity, but the proponent uses it to drop expiation (leniency).
2
Examine the proponent's response in the killing example
The Hanafi responds that the 'illa only necessitates what it is attached to: if killing is already severe (obligation of qisas), it does not need another severity (expiation). So the 'illa's implication is actually that no further severity is needed.
This reframes the implication.
3
Examine the response in the fasting example
The Shafi'i proponent must show that his 'illa (breaking fast by something that can occur from one person) actually implies dropping expiation, not intensification. The author says he must find a way to demonstrate that the 'illa agrees with its implication.
This requires detailed analysis.
4
Generalize the response strategy
The proponent must either: (a) show that the 'illa's true implication is different from what the objector claims, or (b) demonstrate that his use is consistent with the 'illa's established implication from the source.

Checkpoint
In the deliberate killing example, what is the Hanafi's response to the Shafi'i's objection?
A) He changes his 'illa to a different one.
B) He argues that the 'illa only necessitates what it is attached to, and since it already requires killing, it does not require expiation.
C) He admits the objection is valid and drops his claim.
D) He cites a different hadith to support his position.

Key Takeaway
A proper response to the 'attaching contrary' objection involves clarifying the true scope of the 'illa's implication, often by showing it necessitates only what it is directly attached to.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
For the second example (killing), write a brief dialogue: the Shafi'i raises the objection, and the Hanafi gives the response as described. Then explain why the response is effective.
?
For the third example (fasting), propose a possible way the Shafi'i proponent could show that his 'illa agrees with its implication. (Hint: consider the concept of 'singleness' versus 'multiplicity'.)

Dialectic -- Jadal

Understanding Fasad al-I'tibar (Corruption of Consideration)
Author defines fasad al-i'tibar with examples conflicting with Quran, Sunnah, and Ijma'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify examples of fasad al-i'tibar by checking if an analogy contradicts a stronger textual evidence (Quran, Sunnah, or Ijma').

Core Idea
Fasad al-i'tibar is an analogy that ignores stronger textual or consensual evidence.

In jadal and usul al-fiqh, not all analogies are valid. Some are corrupt because they base their reasoning on a consideration that contradicts a stronger source of law. This lesson defines fasad al-i'tibar and shows three examples from the passage, each conflicting with a different primary source: Quran, Sunnah, and Ijma'.

Guided Path
1
Understand the dual definition
The author gives two definitions: (1) considering something by what does not warrant it, (2) an analogy that contradicts the text (nass) or consensus (ijma'). The key is that the 'consideration' (i'tibar) is placed against a stronger evidence.
Both definitions point to the same core idea.
2
Identify the three types of contradiction
The passage provides examples of fasad al-i'tibar contradicting (a) Quran, (b) Sunnah, and (c) Ijma'. For each, note how the analogy is set up and why it fails.
Each example uses a different source of law.
3
Analyze the Ramadan intention example
The argument: Ramadan fast requires night intention (like make-up fasts). The rebuttal: Quran verse shows that any fasting person gets reward, implying validity. The analogy is corrupt because it ignores the explicit Quranic indication.
The Quranic evidence is stronger.
4
Analyze the salam in animals example
The argument: salam (advance payment) in animals is invalid due to uncertainty (like mixed goods). The rebuttal: Prophet permitted salam. The analogy contradicts Sunnah.
Sunnah overrides qiyas.
5
Analyze the husband washing wife example
The argument: a man cannot wash his deceased wife because looking is forbidden (like non-mahram). The rebuttal: silent consensus from Ali washing Fatima. The analogy contradicts Ijma'.
Ijma' (even silent) is a stronger evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ب - فساد الاعتبار: وهو أن يعتبر الشيء بما لا يقتضي اعتباره به، أو هو أن يعتبر حكما بحكم يخالفه، وقد يكون هذا في اعتبار الفرع بالأصل، وقد يكون في اعتبار الفرع بالعلة. وقيل هو: أن يكون القياس مخالفًا للنص أو الإجماع، وسمي هذا فساد الاعتبار، لأن اعتبار القياس مع النص أو الإجماع اعتبار له مع دليل أقوى منه، وهو اعتبار فاسد وظلم لأنه وضع له في غير موضعه.

مثال ما خالف نص الكتاب قولنا: يشترط تبييت النية لرمضان، لأنه مفروض، فلا يصح صوم النية من النهار كالقضاء، فيقال: هذا فاسد الاعتبار لمخالفته نص الكتاب وهو قوله تعالى: (وَالصَّائِمِينَ وَالصَّائِمَاتِ) ... (أعد الله لهم مغفرة وأجرا عظيما)، فإنه يدل على أن كل من صام يحصل له الأجر العظيم، وذلك مستلزم للصحة، وهذا قد صام، فيكون صومه صحيحا.

ومثال ما خالف السنة قولنا: لا يصح السلم في الحيوان، لأنه عقد يشتمل على الغرر، فلا يصح كالسلم في المختلطات، فيقال: هذا فاسد الاعتبار لمخالفته ما روي عن النبي أنه رخص في السلم.

ومثال ما خالف الإجماع أن يقول الحنفي: لا يجوز أن يغسل الرجل زوجته، لأنه يحرم النظر إليها، فحرم غسلها كالأجنبية، فيقال له: هذا فاسد الاعتبار، لمخالفته الإجماع السكوتي وهو أن عليا غسل فاطمة ولم ينكر عليه، والقضية في مظنة الشهرة، فكان ذلك إجماعا.
B - Corruption of Consideration: It is to consider something by what does not warrant considering it, or to consider a ruling by a ruling that contradicts it. This may occur in considering the branch by the root, or in considering the branch by the 'illa. It is also said: It is that the analogy contradicts the text or consensus, and this is called corruption of consideration, because considering the analogy alongside the text or consensus is considering it with a stronger evidence, and it is a corrupt and unjust consideration because it places it in other than its proper place.

An example of contradicting the Book: Our saying: Intention must be made at night for Ramadan, because it is an obligatory fast, so fasting with intention from the daytime is not valid like make-up fasts. It is said: This is corruption of consideration because it contradicts the text of the Book, namely His saying: 'And the fasting men and fasting women... Allah has prepared for them forgiveness and a great reward.' For it indicates that everyone who fasts obtains a great reward, and that entails validity, and this person fasted, so his fast is valid.

An example of contradicting the Sunnah: Our saying: Salam (advance payment) in animals is not valid because it is a contract that involves uncertainty, so it is not valid like salam in mixed goods. It is said: This is corruption of consideration because it contradicts what is related from the Prophet that he permitted salam.

An example of contradicting consensus: When a Hanafi says: It is not permissible for a man to wash his wife because he is forbidden from looking at her, so washing her is forbidden like a non-mahram. It is said to him: This is corruption of consideration because it contradicts the silent consensus that Ali washed Fatima and no one objected, and the case was well-known, so that constituted consensus.
Source pages p. 452

Commentary
This passage defines 'corruption of consideration' (فساد الاعتبار) as a type of invalid analogy in jadal. The author gives two definitions: (1) considering something based on what does not justify considering it, or considering a ruling by a contradictory ruling; (2) an analogy that contradicts a primary textual evidence (nass) or consensus (ijma'). The term 'consideration' (i'tibar) refers to the act of using one thing as a basis for another, typically in qiyas. The corruption occurs when the basis is inappropriate because a stronger evidence overrides it. The author then provides three examples, each contradicting a different source of law: Quran, Sunnah, and Ijma'. The first example: requiring night intention for Ramadan fasts based on analogy with make-up fasts, but the Quranic verse indicates validity of any fast, so the analogy is corrupt. The second: invalidating salam in animals due to uncertainty, but the Prophet permitted salam, so analogy contradicts Sunnah. The third: a Hanafi argument that a husband cannot wash his wife's body after death because looking at her is forbidden, analogy with non-mahram; but the silent consensus from the act of Ali washing Fatima shows it is allowed. Each example illustrates how the analogical consideration is 'corrupt' because it disregards stronger evidence.

Key Terms
فساد الاعتبار
الاعتبار
النص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which example in the passage illustrates fasad al-i'tibar that contradicts consensus?
A) Requiring night intention for Ramadan fasts.
B) Invalidating salam in animals due to uncertainty.
C) A husband washing his wife's body being forbidden.
D) Selling fresh dates for dried dates.

Key Takeaway
Fasad al-i'tibar occurs when an analogy contradicts a stronger textual or consensual evidence, rendering the consideration corrupt.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify which primary source (Quran, Sunnah, or Ijma') is contradicted in each of the three examples from the passage.
?
Consider an analogy: 'Wine is prohibited because it intoxicates; date wine (nabidh) is also intoxicating, so it is prohibited.' Does this analogy risk fasad al-i'tibar? Why or why not? (Assume no contradictory text.)
